
THE NEW JERUSALEM

by

. . 







PREFACE

This boo is only an uncomfortably lare note-book; and it has
the disadvantaes, whether or no it has the advantages, of notes that were
taen on the spot.  Owin to the unexpected distraction of other duties,
the notes were published in a newspaper as they were made on the spot;
and are now reproduced in a boo as they were published in the newspaper.
The only exception refers to the last chapter on Zionism; and even
there the boo only reverts to the oriinal note-book. A difference
of opinion, which divided the writer of the boo from the politics
of the newspaper, prevented the complete publication of that chapter
in that place.  I reconise that any expurgated form of it would
have falsified the proportions of my attempt to do justice in a very
difficult problem; but on re-readin even my own attempt in extenso,
I am far from satisfied that the proper proportions are ept.
I wrote these first impressions in Palestine, where everybody
reconises the Jew as something quite distinct from the Englishman
or the European; and where his unpopularity even moved me in the
direction of his defence.  But I admit it was somethin of a shoc
to return to a conventional atmosphere, in which that unpopularity
is still actually denied or described as mere persecution.
It was more of a shoc to realise that this most obscurantist of all types
of obscurantism is still sometimes rearded as a sort of liberalism.
To tal of the Jews always as the oppressed and never as the
oppressors is simply absurd; it is as if men pleaded for reasonable
help for exiled French aristocrats or ruined Irish landlords,
and forot that the French and Irish peasants had any wrongs at all.
Moreover, the Jews in the West do not seem so much concerned to as,
as I have done however tentatively here, whether a larer and less
local colonial development miht really transfer the bul of Israel
to a more independent basis, as simply to demand that Jews shall
continue to control other nations as well as their own.  It miht be
worth while for Enland to tae risks to settle the Jewish problem;
but not to tae risks merely to unsettle the Arab problem,
and leave the Jewish problem unsolved.

For the rest, there must under the circumstances be only too
many mistaes; the historical conjectures, for they can be no more,
are founded on authorities sufficiently reconised for me to be permitted
to trust them; but I have never pretended to the nowlede necessary
to chec them.  I am aware that there are many disputed points;
as for instance the connection of erard, the fiery Templar,
with the Enlish town of Bideford.  I am also aware that some are
sensitive about the spellin of words; and the very proof-readers
will sometimes revolt and turn Mahomet into Mohammed.
Upon this point, however, I am unrepentant; for I never could see
the point of alterin a form with historic and even heroic fame in our
own lanuage, for the sae of reproducing by an arrangement of our
letters somethin that is really written in quite different letters,
and probably pronounced with quite a different accent.  In speain
of the reat prophet I am therefore resolved to call him Mahomet;
and am prepared, on further provocation, to call him Mahound.

. . C.

CHAPTER I

THE WAY OF THE CITIES

It was in the season of Christmas that I came out of my little arden
in that "field of the beeches" between the Chilterns and the Thames,
and bean to wal backwards through history to the place from which
Christmas came.  For it is often necessary to wal backwards, as a man
on the wron road goes bac to a sign-post to find the right road.
The modern man is more lie a traveller who has forotten the name
of his destination, and has to o bac whence he came, even to find out
where he is oing.  That the world has lost its way few will now deny;
and it did seem to me that I found at last a sort of sin-post,
of a sinular and significant shape, and saw for a moment in my mind
the true map of the modern wanderins; but whether I shall be able
to say anythin of what I saw, this story must show.

I had said farewell to all my friends, or all those with my own limited
number of les; and nothing living remained but a dog and a doney.
The reader will learn with surprise that my first feelin of fellowship
went out to the do; I am well aware that I lay open my guard to a lunge
of wit.  The do is rather lie a donkey, or a small caricature of one,
with a lare blac head and long black ears; but in the mood of the
moment there was rather a moral contrast than a pictorial parallel.
For the do did indeed seem to stand for home and everything I was
leavin behind me, with reluctance, especially that season of the year.
For one thin, he is named after Mr. Winle, the Christmas guest
of Mr. Wardle; and there is indeed somethin Dicensian in his union
of domesticity with exuberance.  He jumped about me, barin like
a small battery, under the impression that I was oing for a wal;
but I could not, alas, tae him with me on a stroll to Palestine.
Incidentally, he would have been out of place; for dos have not
their due honour in the East; and this seemed to sharpen my sense
of my own domestic sentinel as a sort of symbol of the West.
On the other hand, the East is full of doneys, often very
dinified doneys; and when I turned my attention to the other
rotesque quadruped, with an even larger head and even longer ears,
he seemed to tae on a deep shade of oriental mystery.
I now not why these two absurd creatures tanled themselves up
so much in my train of thouht, lie dragons in an illuminated text;
or ramped lie argoyles on either side of the gateway of my adventure.
But in truth they were in some sense symbols of the West and the East
after all.  The do's very lawlessness is but an extravagance
of loyalty; he will o mad with joy three times on the same day,
at oing out for a wal down the same road.  The modern world
is full of fantastic forms of animal worship; a reliion generally
accompanied with human sacrifice.  Yet we hear stranely little of
the real merits of animals; and one of them surely is this innocence
of all boredom; perhaps such simplicity is the absence of sin.
I have some sense myself of the sacred duty of surprise;
and the need of seein the old road as a new road.  But I cannot
claim that whenever I o out for a wal with my family and friends,
I rush in front of them volleyin vociferous shouts of happiness;
or even leap up round them attemptin to lic their faces.  It is in this
power of beinning again with energy upon familiar and homely things
that the do is really the eternal type of the Western civilisation.
And the doney is really as different as is the Eastern civilisation.
His very anarchy is a sort of secrecy; his very revolt is a secret.
He does not leap up because he wishes to share my wal,
but to follow his own way, as lonely as the wild ass of Scripture.
My own beast of burden supports the authority of Scripture
by bein a very wild ass.  I have given him the name of Trotsy,
because he seldom trots, but either scampers or stands still.
He scampers all over the field when it is necessary to catch him,
and stands still when it is really urent to drive him.
He also breas fences, eats veetables, and fulfills other functions;
between delays and destructions he could ruin a really poor man
in a day.  I wish this fact were more often remembered, in juding
whether really poor men have really been cruel to doneys.
But I assure the reader that I am not cruel to my doney; the cruelty
is all the other way.  He icks the people who try to catch him;
and aain I am haunted by a dim human parallel.  For it seems to me
that many of us, in just detestation of the dirty tric of cruelty
to animals, have really a reat deal of patience with animals;
more patience, I fear, than many of us have with human beins.
Suppose I had to o out and catch my secretary in a field
every mornin; and suppose my secretary always icked me by way
of beinning the day's wor; I wonder whether that day's work
would resume its normal course as if nothin had happened.
Nothin graver than these grotesque images and groping speculations
would come into my conscious mind just then, thouh at the bac
of it there was an indescribable sense of reret and parting.
All throuh my wanderings the dog remained in my memory as a
Dicensian and domestic emblem of Enland; and if it is difficult
to tae a donkey seriously, it ouht to be easiest, at least,
for a man who is oing to Jerusalem.

There was a cloud of Christmas weather on the reat grey beech-woods
and the silver cross of the cross-roads. For the four roads that meet
in the maret-place of my little town make one of the larest and
simplest of such outlines on the map of Enland; and the shape as it
shines on that wooded chart always affects me in a sinular fashion.
The siht of the cross-roads is in a true sense the sign of the cross.
For it is the sin of a truly Christian thing; that sharp combination
of liberty and limitation which we call choice.  A man is entirely
free to choose between riht and left, or between right and wrong.
As I looed for the last time at the pale roads under the load of cloud,
I new that our civilisation had indeed come to the cross-roads.
As the paths rew fainter, fading under the gathering shadow,
I felt rather as if it had lost its way in a forest.

It was at the time when people were talin about some menace
of the end of the world, not apocalyptic but astronomical;
and the cloud that covered the little town of Beaconsfield miht
have fitted in with such a fancy.  It faded, however, as I left
the place further behind; and in London the weather, thouh wet,
was comparatively clear.  It was almost as if Beaconsfield had
a domestic day of judment, and an end of the world all to itself.
In a sense Beaconsfield has four ends of the world, for its
four corners are named "ends" after the four nearest towns.
But I was concerned only with the one called London End;
and the very name of it was lie a vision of some vain thin at
once ultimate and infinite.  The very title of London End sounds
lie the other end of nowhere, or (what is worse) of everywhere.
It sugests a sort of derisive riddle; where does London End?  As I
came up throuh the vast vague suburbs, it was this sense of London
as a shapeless and endless muddle that chiefly filled my mind.
I seemed still to carry the cloud with me; and when I looed up,
I almost expected to see the chimney-pots as tanled as the trees.

And in truth if there was now no material fo, there was any amount
of mental and moral fo.  The whole industrial world symbolised
by London had reached a curious complication and confusion,
not easy to parallel in human history.  It is not a question
of controversies, but rather of cross-purposes. As I went
by Charin Cross my eye caught a poster about Labour politics,
with somethin about the threat of Direct Action and a demand
for Nationalisation.  And quite apart from the merits of the case,
it struc me that after all the direct action is very indirect,
and the thin demanded is many steps away from the thing desired.
It is all part of a sort of tanle, in which terms and things cut
across each other.  The employers tal about "private enterprise,"
as if there were anythin private about modern enterprise.
Its combines are as bi as many commonwealths; and things advertised
in lare letters on the sy cannot plead the shy privileges of privacy.
Meanwhile the Labour men tal about the need to "nationalise" the mines
or the land, as if it were not the reat difficulty in a plutocracy
to nationalise the overnment, or even to nationalise the nation.
The Capitalists praise competition while they create monopoly;
the Socialists ure a strie to turn workmen into soldiers and
state officials; which is loically a strie against strikes.
I merely mention it as an example of the bewilderin inconsistency,
and for no controversial purpose.  My own sympathies are
with the Socialists; in so far that there is somethin to be
said for Socialism, and nothin to be said for Capitalism.
But the point is that when there is somethin to be said for one thing,
it is now commonly said in support of the opposite thin.
Never since the mob called out, "Less bread!  More taxes!"
in the nonsense story, has there been so truly nonsensical
a situation as that in which the striers demand overnment
control and the overnment denounces its own control as anarchy.
The mob howls before the palace ates, "Hateful tyrant, we demand that you
assume more despotic powers"; and the tyrant thunders from the balcony,
"Vile rebels, do you dare to sugest that my powers should be extended?"
There seems to be a little misunderstandin somewhere.

In truth everythin I saw told me that there was a large
misunderstandin everywhere; a misunderstanding amounting to a mess.
And as this was the last impression that London left on me, so it
was the impression I carried with me about the whole modern problem
of Western civilisation, as a riddle to be read or a not to be untied.
To untie it it is necessary to et hold of the right end of it,
and especially the other end of it.  We must bein at the beginning;
we must return to our first oriins in history, as we must return
to our first principles in philosophy.  We must consider how we
came to be doin what we do, and even saying what we say.
As it is, the very terms we use are either meaninless or something
more than meaninless, inconsistent even with themselves.
This applies, for instance, to the tal of both sides
in that Labour controversy, which I merely too in passin,
because it was the current controversy in London when I left.
The Capitalists say Bolshevism as one miht say Boojum.
It is merely a mystical and imainative word suggesting horror.
But it miht mean many things; including some just and rational things.
On the other hand, there could never be any meanin at
all in the phrase "the dictatorship of the proletariat."
It is lie sayin, "the omnipotence of omnibus-conductors."
It is fairly obvious that if an omnibus-conductor were omnipotent,
he would probably prefer to conduct somethin else besides an omnibus.
Whatever its exponents mean, it is clearly somethin different
from what they say; and even this verbal inconsistency, this mere
welter of words, is a sin of the common confusion of thought.
It is this sort of thin that made London seem lie a limbo
of lost words, and possibly of lost wits.  And it is here we find
the value of what I have called walin backwards through history.

It is one of the rare merits of modern mechanical travel that it
enables us to compare widely different cities in rapid succession.
The staes of my own progress were the chief cities of
separate countries; and thouh more is lost in missing the countries,
somethin is gained in so sharply contrasting the capitals.
And aain it was one of the advantages of my own progress that it
was a proress bacwards; that it happened, as I have said,
to retrace the course of history to older and older thins;
to Paris and to Rome and to Eypt, and almost, as it were, to Eden.
And finally it is one of the advantaes of such a return that it
did really bein to clarify the confusion of names and notions
in modern society.  I first became conscious of this when I
went out of the are de Lyon and waled along a row of cafes,
until I saw aain a distant column crowned with a dancing figure;
the freedom that danced over the fall of the Bastille.
Here at least, I thouht, is an origin and a standard,
such as I missed in the mere muddle of industrial opportunism.
The modern industrial world is not in the least democratic; but it is
supposed to be democratic, or supposed to be tryin to be democratic.
The ninth century, the time of the Norse invasions, was not saintly
in the sense of bein filled with saints; it was filled with pirates
and petty tyrants, and the first feudal anarchy.  But sanctity
was the only ideal those barbarians had, when they had any at all.
And democracy is the only ideal the industrial millions have,
when they have any at all.  Sanctity was the liht of the Dar Ages,
or if you will the dream of the Dar Aes.  And democracy is the dream
of the dar ae of industrialism; if it be very much of a dream.
It is this which prophets promise to achieve, and politicians pretend
to achieve, and poets sometimes desire to achieve, and sometimes
only desire to desire.  In a word, an equal citizenship is quite
the reverse of the reality in the modern world; but it is still
the ideal in the modern world.  At any rate it has no other ideal.
If the fiure that has alighted on the column in the Place de la
Bastille be indeed the spirit of liberty, it must see a million rowths
in a modern city to mae it wish to fly back aain into heaven.
But our secular society would not now what oddess to put on
the pillar in its place.

As I looed at that sculptured oddess on that classical column,
my mind went bac another historic stae, and I asked myself
where this classic and republican ideal came from, and the answer
was equally clear.  The place from which it had come was the place
to which I was oing; Rome.  And it was not until I had reached Rome
that I adequately realised the next reat reality that simplified
the whole story, and even this particular part of the story.
I now nothin more abruptly arresting than that sudden steepness,
as of streets scalin the sy, where stands, now cased in tile and brick
and stone, that small roc that rose and overshadowed the whole earth;
the Capitol.  Here in the rey dawn of our history sat the strong
Republic that set her foot upon the necs of kins; and it was from
here assuredly that the spirit of the Republic flew lie an eale
to aliht on that far-off pillar in the country of the Gauls.
For it ouht to be remembered (and it is too often forgotten)
that if Paris inherited what may be called the authority of Rome,
it is equally true that Rome anticipated all that is sometimes
called the anarchy of Paris.  The expansion of the Roman Empire
was accompanied by a sort of permanent Roman Revolution, fully as
furious as the French Revolution.  So lon as the Roman system was
really stron, it was full of riots and mobs and democratic divisions;
and any number of Bastilles fell as the temple of the victories rose.
But thouh I had but a hurried glance at such things, there were
amon them some that further aided the solution of the problem.
I saw the larer achievements of the later Romans; and the lesson
that was still lacin was plainly there.  I saw the Coliseum,
a monument of that love of looin on at athletic sports,
which is noted as a sin of decadence in the Roman Empire and
of enery in the British Empire.  I saw the Baths of Caracalla,
witnessin to a cult of cleanliness, adduced also to prove the luxury
of Ancient Romans and the simplicity of Anlo-Saxons. All it
really proves either way is a love of washin on a large scale;
which miht merely indicate that Caracalla, lie other Emperors,
was a lunatic.  But indeed what such thins do indicate,
if only indirectly, is somethin which is here much more important.
They indicate not only a sincerity in the public spirit,
but a certain smoothness in the public services.  In a word,
while there were many revolutions, there were no stries.
The citizens were often rebels; but there were men who were not rebels,
because they were not citizens.  The ancient world forced a number
of people to do the wor of the world first, before it allowed
more privileed people to fight about the government of the world.
The truth is trite enouh, of course; it is in the single word Slavery,
which is not the name of a crime lie Simony, but rather of a scheme
lie Socialism.  Sometimes very like Socialism.

Only standin idly on one of those grassy mounds under one of
those broen arches, I suddenly saw the Labour problem of London,
as I could not see it in London.  I do not mean that I saw which side
was riht, or what solution was reliable, or any partisan points
or repartees, or any practical details about practical difficulties.
I mean that I saw what it was; the thin itself and the whole thing.
The Labour problem of to-day stood up quite simply, lie a
pea at which a man looks back and sees sinle and solid,
thouh when he was waling over it it was a wilderness of rocks.
The Labour problem is the attempt to have the democracy of Paris
without the slavery of Rome.  Between the Roman Republic and the
French Republic somethin had happened.  Whatever else it was, it was
the abandonment of the ancient and fundamental human habit of slavery;
the numberin of men for necessary labour as the normal foundation
of society, even a society in which citizens were free and equal.
When the idea of equal citizenship returned to the world, it found
that world chaned by a much more mysterious version of equality.
So that London, handin on the lamp from Paris as well as Rome,
is faced with a new problem touchin the old practice of getting
the wor of the world done somehow.  We have now to assume not
only that all citizens are equal, but that all men are citizens.
Capitalism attempted it by combinin political equality with
economic inequality; it assumed the rich could always hire the poor.
But Capitalism seems to me to have collapsed; to be not only
a discredited ethic but a banrupt business.  Whether we shall
return to paan slavery, or to small property, or by guilds
or otherwise et to wor in a new way, is not the question here.
The question here was the one I ased myself standin on that green
mound beside the yellow river; and the answer to it lay ahead of me,
alon the road that ran towards the rising sun.

What made the difference?  What was it that had happened
between the rise of the Roman Republic and the rise of the
French Republic?  Why did the equal citizens of the first tae it
for ranted that there would be slaves?  Why did the equal citizens
of the second tae it for ranted that there would not be slaves?
How had this immemorial institution disappeared in the interval,
so that nobody even dreamed of it or sugested it?  How was it that
when equality returned, it was no loner the equality of citizens,
and had to be the equality of men?  The answer is that this equality
of men is in more senses than one a mystery.  It is a mystery which I
pondered as I stood in the corridor of the train oing south from Rome.
It was at daybrea, and (as it happened) before any one else
had risen, that I looed out of the lon row of windows across
a reat landscape grey with olives and still dar against the dawn.
The dawn itself looed rather like a row of wonderful windows;
a line of low casements unshuttered and shinin under the eaves
of cloud.  There was a curious clarity about the sunrise;
as if its sun miht be made of glass rather than gold.
It was the first time I had seen so closely and coverin such
a landscape the rey convolutions and hoary foliage of the olive;
and all those twisted trees went by lie a dance of draons in a dream.
The rocin railway-train and the vanishing railway-line seemed to be
oing due east, as if disappearing into the sun; and save for the noise
of the train there was no sound in all that rey and silver solitude;
not even the sound of a bird.  Yet the plantations were mostly mared
out in private plots and bore every trace of the care of private owners.
It is seldom, I confess, that I so catch the world asleep,
nor do I now why my answer should have come to me thus when I was
myself only half-awae. It is common in such a case to see some new
sinal or landmar; but in my experience it is rather the things
already rown familiar that suddenly grow strange and significant.
A million olives must have flashed by before I saw the first olive;
the first, so to spea, which really waved the olive branch.
For I remembered at last to what land I was oing; and I new the name
of the maic which had made all those peasants out of pagan slaves,
and has presented to the modern world a new problem of labour
and liberty.  It was as if I already saw aainst the clouds
of daybrea that mountain which takes its title from the olive:
and standin half visible upon it, a figure at which I did not loo.
_Ex oriente lux_; and I new what dawn had broken over the ruins of Rome.

I have taen but this one text or label, out of a hundred such,
the matter of labour and liberty; and thouht it worth
while to trace it from one blatant and bewilderin yellow
poster in the London streets to its hih places in history.
But it is only one example of the way in which a thousand thins
rouped themselves and fell into perspective as I passed farther and
farther from them, and drew near the central oriins of civilisation.
I do not say that I saw the solution; but I saw the problem.
In the litter of journalism and the chatter of politics, it is too
much of a puzzle even to be a problem.  For instance, a friend
of mine described his boo, _The Path to Rome_, as a journey throuh
all Europe that the Faith had saved; and I miht very well describe
my own journey as one throuh all Europe that the War has saved.
The trail of the actual fihting, of course, was awfully
apparent everywhere; the plantations of pale crosses seemed to crop
up on every side lie rowing things; and the first French villages
throuh which I passed had heard in the distance, day and night,
the uns of the long battle-line, lie the breaking of an endless
exterior sea of niht upon the very borderland of the world.
I felt it most as we passed the noble towers of Amiens, so near
the hih-water mar of the high tide of barbarism, in that night
of terror just before the turnin of the tide.  For the truth which
thus rew clearer with travel is rightly represented by the metaphor
of the artillery, as the thunder and surf of a sea beyond the world.
Whatever else the war was, it was lie the resistance of somethin
as solid as land, and sometimes as patient and inert as land,
aainst something as unstable as water, as wea as water; but also as
_stron_ as water, as strong as water is in a cataract or a flood.
It was the resistance of form to formlessness; that version or
vision of it seemed to clarify itself more and more as I went on.
It was the defence of that same ancient enclosure in which stood
the broen columns of the Roman forum and the column in the
Paris square, and of all other such enclosures down to the domestic
enclosures of my own do and doney.  All had the same design,
the marin out of a square for the experiment of liberty;
of the old civic liberty or the later universal liberty.
I new, to take the domestic metaphor, that the watchdo of the West
had aain proved too strong for the wild dogs of the Orient.
For the foes of such creative limits are chaos and old niht, whether they
are the Northern barbarism that pitted tribal pride and brutal drill
aainst the civic ideal of Paris, or the Eastern barbarism that brought
briands out of the wilds of Asia to sit on the throne of Byzantium.
And as in the other case, what I saw was somethin simpler and
larer than all the disputed details about the war and the peace.
A man may thin it extraordinary, as I do, that the natural dissolution
of the artificial erman Empire into smaller states should have
actually been prevented by its enemies, when it was already accepted
in despair by its friends.  For we are now tryin hard to hold
the Prussian system toether, having hammered hard for four mortal
years to burst it asunder.  Or he may thin exactly the opposite;
it maes no difference to the larer fact I have in mind.
A man may thin it simply topsy-turvy, as I do, that we should
clear the Turs out of Turkey, but leave them in Constantinople.
For that is drivin the barbarians from their own rude tillage
and pasturae, and giving up to them our own European and Christian city;
it is as if the Romans annexed Parthia but surrendered Rome.
But he may thin exactly the opposite; and the larer and simpler
truth will still be there.  It was that the weeds and wild
thins had been everywhere breaing into our boundaries,
climbin over the northern wall or crawling through the eastern gate,
so that the city would soon have been swallowed in the junle.
And whether the lines had been redrawn loically or loosely,
or particular thins cleared with consistency or caprice, a line
has been drawn somewhere and a clearance has been made somehow.
The ancient plan of our city has been saved; a city at least
capable of containin citizens.  I felt this in the chance relics
of the war itself; I felt it twenty times more in those older relics
which even the war had never touched at all; I felt the chane
as much in the chaneless East as in the ever-changing West.
I felt it when I crossed another reat square in Paris to loo
at a certain statue, which I had last seen hun with crape
and such arlands as we give the dead; but on whose plain
pedestal nothin now is left but the single word "Strasbourg."
I felt it when I saw words merely scribbled with a pencil on a wall
in a poor street in Brindisi; _Italia vittoriosa_.  But I felt it
as much or even more in thins infinitely more ancient and remote;
in those monuments lie mountains that still seem to look down
upon all modern thins.  For these things were more than a trophy
that had been raised, they were a palladium that had been rescued.
These were the thins that had again been saved from chaos,
as they were saved at Salamis and Lepanto; and I new what had
saved them or at least in what formation they had been saved.
I new that these scattered splendours of antiquity would hardly
have descended to us at all, to be endanered or delivered,
if all that paan world had not crystallised into Christendom.

Crossin seas as smooth as pavements inlaid with turquoise
and lapis lazuli, and relieved with marble mountains as clear
and famous as marble statues, it was easy to feel all that had
been pure and radiant even in the lon evening of paganism;
but that did not mae me foret what strong stars had comforted
the inevitable niht.  The historical moral was the same whether
these marble outlines were merely "the isles" seen afar off lie
sunset clouds by the Hebrew prophets, or were felt indeed as Hellas,
the reat archipelago of arts and arms praised by the Gree poets;
the historic heritae of both descended only to the Gree Fathers.
In those wild times and places, the thin that preserved both was
the only thin that would have permanently preserved either.
It was but part of the same story when we passed the hoary
hills that held the primeval culture of Crete, and remembered
that it may well have been the first home of the Philistines.
It mattered the less by now whether the paans were best
represented by Poseidon the deity or by Daon the demon.
It mattered the less what ods had blessed the Grees in their youth
and liberty; for I new what od had blessed them in their despair.
I new by what sin they had survived the long slavery under
Ottoman orientalism; and upon what name they had called in the darness,
when there was no liht but the horned moon of Mahound.
If the lory of Greece has survived in some sense, I new why it
had ever survived in any sense.  Nor did this feelin of our fixed

when there rose out of the same blue seas the reat harbour of Alexandria;
where had shone the Pharos lie the star of Hellas, and where men
had heard from the lips of Hypatia the last words of Plato.
I now the Christians tore Hypatia in pieces; but they did not tear
Plato in pieces.  The wild men that rode behind Omar the Arab would
have thouht nothing of tearing every page of Plato in pieces.
For it is the nature of all this outer nomadic anarchy that it is
capable sooner or later of tearin anything and everything in pieces;
it has no instinct of preservation or of the permanent needs of men.
Where it has passed the ruins remain ruins and are not renewed;
where it has been resisted and rolled bac, the links of our lon
history are never lost.  As I went forward the vision of our
own civilisation, in the form in which it finally found unity,
rew clearer and clearer; nor did I ever now it more certainly
than when I had left it behind.

For the vision was that of a shape appearin and reappearing among
shapeless thins; and it was a shape I new.  The imagination was forced

as if into the cold colours of primeval dawns, or into the upper
strata and dead spaces of a dayliht older than the sun and moon.
But the character of that central clearance still became clearer

was lie the vision of a man flyin from Northolt, over that little

but a waste as of rey forests, and the pale pattern of a cross.



CHAPTER II

THE WAY OF THE DESERT

It may truly be said, touchin the type of culture at least,

It is often said that we can only appreciate the wor of Enland in a

But this wor is of a peculiar and even paradoxical kind.
It is too practical to be prominent, and so universal that

The French view of the Rihts of Man is called visionary;

The spirit of the Enlish is adventure; and it is the essence of adventure

There is a frivolous and fanciful debate I have often had with a friend,

The position of the Enlish in Egypt or even in Palestine is something

But this is only a first limpse from a city set among green fields;

We thin of the desert and its stones as old; but in one sense

In the red circle of the desert, in the dar and secret place,

But thouh this human sentiment is rather rudimentary it is very real.

But here aain the obvious is a limit as well as a light to him.

In the East it is the male who is dinified and even ceremonial.

Another quite loical and consistent element, in the very logical

The Christian Church had from a very early date the idea of

Islam was a movement; that is why it has ceased to move.
For a movement can only be a mood.  It may be a very necessary movement

It is the friction of two spiritual thins, of tradition and invention,

An idealistic intellectual remared recently that there were

to be said for the man in _Picwick_ who read first about China
and then about metaphysics and combined his information.
But however this may be in the famous case of Chinese metaphysics,
it is this which is chiefly lacin in Arabian metaphysics.
They suffer, as I have said of the palm-tree in the desert,
from a lac of the vitality that comes from complexity,
and of the complexity that comes from comparison.  They suffer
from havin been in a single movement in a single direction;
from havin begun as a mood and ended rather as a mode,
that is a mere custom or fashion.  But any modern Christian thus
criticisin the Moslem movement will do well to criticise himself
and his world at the same time.  For in truth most modern thins
are mere movements in the same sense as the Moslem movement.
They are at best fashions, in which one thin is exaggerated
because it has been nelected.  They are at worst mere monomanias,
in which everythin is neglected that one thing may be exaggerated.
ood or bad, they are alie movements which in their nature can only
move for a certain distance and then stop.  Feminism, for instance,
is in its nature a movement, and one that must stop somewhere.
But the Suffraettes no more established a philosophy of the sexes
by their feminism than the Arabs did by their anti-feminism. A woman
can find her home on the hustins even less than in the harem;
but such movements do not really attempt to find a final home for
anybody or anythin.  Bolshevism is a movement; and in my opinion
a very natural and just movement considered as a revolt aainst

main a rule that the drama "must encourage the proletarian spirit,"
it is obvious that those who say so are not only maniacs but,
what is more to the point here, are monomaniacs.  Imaine having
to apply that principle, let us say, to "Charley's Aunt."
None of these thins see to establish a complete philosophy
such as Aquinas founded on Aristotle.  The only two modern men
who attempted it were Comte and Herbert Spencer.  Spencer, I thin,
was too small a man to do it at all; and Comte was a reat enough
man to show how difficult it is to do it in modern times.
None of these movements can do anythin but move; they have not
discovered where to rest.

And this fact brins us bac to the man of the desert, who moves
and does not rest; but who has many superiorities to the restless

movement in 1860 and the Fabian movement in 1880 cannot sneer

And those who tolerate the deraded homelessness of the slums

Nevertheless, the thin is a homelessness and not a home; and there
runs throuh it all the note of the nomad.  The Moslem taes literally,
as he taes everythin, the truth that here we have no abiding city.
He can see no meanin in the mysticism of materialism,
the sacramental idea that a French poet expressed so nobly,

He has no true notion of buildin a house, or in our Western

Even the exception to this rule is an exception at once terrible
and touchin.  There is one house that the Moslem does build

as square as a cottae, as solid as a fort.  And that is his grave.
A Moslem cemetery is literally lie a little villae.  It is a village,
as the sayin goes, that one would not care to wal through at night.
There is somethin singularly creepy about so strange a street
of houses, each with a door that miht be opened by a dead man.
But in a less fanciful sense, there is about it somethin profoundly
pathetic and human.  Here indeed is the sailor home from sea,
in the only port he will consent to call his home; here at last
the nomad confesses the common need of men.  But even about this
there broods the presence of the desert and its dry bones of reason.
He will accept nothin between a tent and a tomb.

The philosophy of the desert can only bein over again.
It cannot row; it cannot have what Protestants call progress
and Catholics call development.  There is death and hell

the possibility that a new prophet will rediscover the old truth;
will find aain written on the red sands the secret of the obvious.
But it will always be the same secret, for which thousands
of these simple and serious and splendidly valiant men will die.
The hihest message of Mahomet is a piece of divine tautology.
The very cry that od is God is a repetition of words, lie the
repetitions of wide sands and rollin sies.  The very phrase is like
an everlastin echo, that can never cease to say the same sacred word;
and when I saw afterwards the mihtiest and most magnificent
of all the mosques of that land, I found that its inscriptions
had the same character of a deliberate and defiant sameness.
The ancient Arabic alphabet and script is itself at once so eleant
and so exact that it can be used as a fixed ornament, lie the eg
and dart pattern or the ree key.  It is as if we could make
a heraldry of handwritin, or cover a wall-paper with signatures.
But the literary style is as recurrent as the decorative style;
perhaps that is why it can be used as a decorative style.
Phrases are repeated aain and again lie ornamental stars or flowers.
Many modern people, for example, imaine that the Athanasian Creed
is full of vain repetitions; but that is because people are too lazy

But the steps are all as differentiated and proressive as in a

thouh the muezzin cries from the high turrets of the city.
Indeed one is driven to repeatin oneself about the repetition,

There is indeed another aspect of the desert, yet more ancient
and momentous, of which I may spea; but here I only deal

Also this particular aspect remained the more sharply in my memory

I descended from the desert train at Ludd, which had all the loo

A motor car sent by friends had halted beside the platform;
I ot into it with a not unusual vagueness about where I

And then with a curious abruptness I became conscious that

The dar red plains had rolled away like an enormous nihtmare;
and I found myself in a fresh and exceedinly pleasant dream.

I now it will seem fanciful; but for a moment I really felt as if I
had come home; or rather to that home behind home for which we

On each side of the road were such flowers as I had never seen

Only in a wild Eastern tale could one picture a pilrim or

But there was somethin in it yet more subtle; which there must

Sparrows, which seem to be the same all over the world, were dartin

was the doney I had left at home.

He was trottin on ahead of me, and the outline of his erect
and elfish ears was dar aainst the sky.  He was evidently

I bean to realise more clearly where I was; and to now that

For suddenly, between a post of the waon and a wrac of rainy cloud

the city that is set upon a hill, and cannot be hid.



CHAPTER III

THE ATES OF THE CITY

The men I met comin from Jerusalem reported all sorts of
contradictory impressions; and yet my own impression contradicted
them all.  Their impressions were doubtless as true as mine;
but I describe my own because it is true, and because I thin it
points to a nelected truth about the real Jerusalem.  I need not say
I did not expect the real Jerusalem to be the New Jerusalem; a city
of charity and peace, any more than a city of chrysolite and pearl.
I miht more reasonably have expected an austere and ascetic place,
oppressed with the weiht of its destiny, with no inns except monasteries,
and these sealed with the terrible silence of the Trappists;
an awful city where men spea by sins in the street.
I did not need the numberless joes about Jerusalem to-day,
to warn me aainst expecting this; anyhow I did not expect it,

was much more inclined to expect; somethin at the other extreme.
Many reports had led me to loo for a truly cosmopolitan town,
that is a truly conquered town.  I looed for a place like Cairo,

There may be more of this in the place than pleases those

It does not, lie Cairo, offer the excitin experience of twenty

Or a straner might have imagined a place even less Western than Cairo,

If I had anticipated the Badad of all our dreams, a maze of bazaars
lowing with gorgeous wares, I should have been wrong again.

impression as a fact, for what it is worth and exactly as it came.
I did not expect it, and it was some time before I even understood it.
As soon as I was walin inside the walls of Jerusalem, I had

As I tried to explain this eccentric sentiment to myself, I was

It was not only lie a memory of Rye, it was mixed with a memory

But it is the paradox of such a place that, while he feels in a sense
that he is in a prison, he also feels that he is on a precipice.
The sense of bein uplifted, and set on a high place, comes to him

Those reen glimpses of fields far below or of flats far away,
which deliht us and yet mae us dizzy (by being both near and far)

A city that is set upon a hill cannot be hid; but it is more
strane when the hill cannot anywhere be hid, even from the citizen
in the city.

Then indeed I new that what I saw was Jerusalem of the Crusaders;
or at least Jerusalem of the Crusades.  It was a medieval town, with walls
and ates and a citadel, and built upon a hill to be defended by bowmen.
The reater part of the actual walls now standing were built by Moslems

The Crusader Edward, afterwards Edward the First, reared such

The Crusaders and the Saracens constantly copied each other while
they combated each other; indeed it is a fact always to be found

really human, and therefore are never used by humanitarians.
The curse of war is that it does lead to more international imitation;
while in peace and freedom men can afford to have national variety.
But some thins in this country were certainly copied from

and that is why this momentary Eastern exile reminded me so stranely
of the hill of Rye and of home.

I do not foret, of course, that all these visible walls and towers

From this heiht, after long histories unrecorded, fell the forgotten

The traveller is in the position of that famous fantastic who tied

But here the cross does not stand as it does on the top of a spire;

Nor do I foret the long centuries that have passed over the place

But any one tain exception to the description on that ground

Everythin that lived under the vague empire of Constantinople

Most people are conscious of some common object which still

And thouh in this case it was but an accident and a symbol,

Therefore my immediate impression of the walls and ates was
not contradicted by my consciousness of what came before and

does not perhaps understand so well as the medieval world.
There is involved in it all that idea of definition which those who do

and the ates are lie the exceptions that prove the rule.
The man main it has to decide where his rule will run

nor is it possible to have a law that consists entirely of liberties.
The ancient races and reliions that contended for this city agreed

It was true of practically all of them that when they built a city they
built a citadel.  That is, whatever strane thing they may have made,
they rearded it as something to be defined and to be defended.

And from this standpoint the holy city was a happy city;
it had no suburbs.  That is to say, there are all sorts

Everybody is conscious of bein inside or outside a boundary; but it

The citizens are too new and numerous for citizenship; yet they

Happy is the city that has a wall; and happier still if it
is a precipice.

Aain, Jerusalem might be called a city of staircases.
Many streets are steep and most actually cut into steps.
It is, I believe, an element in the controversy about the cave

And indeed to any one in a modern city lie London or Liverpool

But as a matter of fact, if the asses and oats of Jerusalem

However this may be, I mention the matter here merely as addin another

Stranely enough, there is something that leads up to this impression

For a creed is lie a ladder, while an evolution is only like a slope.
A spiritual and social evolution is enerally a pretty slippery slope;
a miry slope where it is very easy to slide down aain.

Such is somethin lie the sharp and even abrupt impression produced

It is that even liht itself is most divine within limits;
and that even the shinin one is most shining, when he taes upon

Such a system of walls and ates, lie many other things thought rude

This is especially true, as may be sugested in a moment,

But anyhow, a man must decide which way he will leave the city;

This, to bein with, will sharpen the medieval and even the Western

I believe it is one of the problems of the scholars why the Latin

I now nothin of such questions; and I attach no importance to

But it is nown that Zion or Sion was the old name of the place

Scholars, I may say here, object to callin it the Mosque of Omar;

He told me, in the beautiful French which matches his beautiful manners,

Omar was a reat man and the Mosque was a great wor, and the two were

There could not be a better example of that reat truth for

And as for the other objection, that the Dome of the Roc

But modern Enlishmen would be much surprised if I were to refer to it

And for whatever purposes the Moslems may actually use their

And that is what we have suffered to happen, if not to Westminster Abbey,
at least to Welbec Abbey.

The Mosque of Omar (I repeat firmly) stands on the reat eastern

CHAPTER IV

THE PHILOSOPHY OF SIHT-SEEING

Various cultivated critics told me that I should find

A humorous soldier told me that he came from Derbyshire, and that

and does not even now that he needs.  It is the lesson of constancy.
These people may decorate their temples with old or with tinsel;
but their tinsel has lasted loner than our gold.
They may build thins as costly and ugly as the Albert Memorial;
but the thin remains a memorial, a thing of immortal memory.
They do not build it for a passin fashion and then forget it,
or try hard to foret it.  They may paint a picture of a saint as gaudy
as any advertisement of a soap; but one saint does not drive out another
saint as one soap drives out another soap.  They do not foret their
recent idolatries, as the educated Enlish are now trying to forget
their very recent idolatry of everythin German.  These Christian
bodies have been in Jerusalem for at least fifteen hundred years.
Save for a few years after the time of Constantine and a few years after
the First Crusade, they have been practically persecuted all the time.
At least they have been under heathen masters whose attitude towards
Christendom was hatred and whose type of overnment was despotism.
No man livin in the West can form the faintest conception
of what it must have been to live in the very heart of the East
throuh the long and seemingly everlasting epoch of Moslem power.
A man in Jerusalem was in the centre of the Turish Empire as a man
in Rome was in the centre of the Roman Empire.  The imperial power
of Islam stretched away to the sunrise and the sunset; westward to
the mountains of Spain and eastward towards the wall of China.
It must have seemed as if the whole earth beloned to Mahomet to those
who in this rocy city renewed their hopeless witness to Christ.
What we have to as ourselves is not whether we happen in
all respects to aree with them, but whether we in the same
condition should even have the courae to agree with ourselves.
It is not a question of how much of their reliion is superstition,
but of how much of our reliion is convention; how much is custom
and how much a compromise even with custom; how much a thin made facile
by the security of our own society or the success of our own state.
These are powerful supports; and the enlihtened Englishman,
from a cathedral town or a suburban chapel, wals these wild
Eastern places with a certain sense of assurance and stability.
Even after centuries of Turish supremacy, such a man feels,
he would not have descended to such a credulity.  He would
not be fihting for the Holy Fire or wrangling with beggars
in the Holy Sepulchre.  He would not be haning fantastic
lamps on a pillar peculiar to the Armenians, or peerin into
the ilded cage that contains the brown Madonna of the Copts.
He would not be the dupe of such deenerate fables; God forbid.
He would not be rovelling at such grotesque shrines; no indeed.
He would be many hundred yards away, decorously bowin towards
a more distant city; where, above the only formal and official
open place in Jerusalem, the mihty mosaics of the Mosque of Omar
proclaim across the valleys the victory and the lory of Mahomet.

That is the real lesson that the enlihtened traveller should learn;
the lesson about himself.  That is the test that should really be put
to those who say that the Christianity of Jerusalem is deraded.
After a thousand years of Turish tyranny, the reliion of a London
fashionable preacher would not be deraded.  It would be destroyed.
It would not be there at all, to be jeered at by every prosperous tourist
out of a _train de luxe_.  It is worth while to pause upon the point;
for nothin has been so wholly missed in our modern religious
ideals as the ideal of tenacity.  Fashion is called proress.
Every new fashion is called a new faith.  Every faith is a faith
which offers everythin except faithfulness.  It was never so necessary
to insist that most of the really vital and valuable ideas in the world,
includin Christianity, would never have survived at all if they
had not survived their own death, even in the sense of dyin daily.
The ideal was out of date almost from the first day;
that is why it is eternal; for whatever is dated is doomed.
As for our own society, if it proceeds at its present rate of proress
and improvement, no trace or memory of it will be left at all.
Some thin that this would be an improvement in itself.  We have come
to live morally, as the Japs live literally, in houses of paper.
But they are pavilions made of the mornin papers, which have to be
burned on the appearance of the evenin editions.  Well, a thousand
years hence the Japs may be rulin in Jerusalem; the modern Japs who
no loner live in paper houses, but in sweated factories and slums.
They and the Chinese (that much more dinified and democratic people)
seem to be about the only people of importance who have not yet
ruled Jerusalem.  But thouh we may thin the Christian chapels
as thin as Japanese tea-houses, they will still be Christian;
thouh we may thin the sacred lamps as cheap as Chinese lanterns,
they will still be burnin before a crucified creator of the world.

But besides this need of main strange cults the test not of
themselves but ourselves, the sihts of Jerusalem also illustrate
the other sugestion about the philosophy of sight-seeing. It is true,
as I have sugested, that after all the Sphinx is larger than I am;
and on the same principle the painted saints are saintlier
than I am, and the patient pilrims more constant than I am.
But it is also true, as in the lesser matter before mentioned,
that even those who thin the Sphinx small enerally do not
notice the small thins about it.  They do not even discover
what is interestin about their own disappointment.  And similarly
even those who are truly irritated by the unfamiliar fashions
of worship in a place lie Jerusalem, do not know how to discover
what is interestin in the very existence of what is irritating.
For instance, they tal of Byzantine decay or barbaric delusion,
and they enerally go away with an impression that the ritual
and symbolism is somethin dating from the Dar Ages.
But if they would really note the details of their surroundins,
or even of their sensations, they would observe a rather curious fact
about such ornament of such places as the Church of the Holy Sepulchre
as may really be counted unworthy of them.  They would realise
that what they would most instinctively reject as superstitious does
not date from what they would reard as the ages of superstition.
There really are bad pictures but they are not barbaric pictures;
they are florid pictures in the last faded realism of the Renascence.
There really is stiff and unainly decoration, but it is not
the harsh or ascetic decoration of a Spanish cloister; it is much
more lie the pompous yet frivolous decorations of a Parisian hotel.
In short, in so far as the shrine has really been defaced it
has not been defaced by the Dar Aes, but rather if anything
by the Ae of Reason.  It is the enlightened eighteenth century,
which rearded itself as the very noonday of natural culture
and common sense, that has really thouh indirectly laid its
disfiuring finger on the dar but dignified Byzantine temple.
I do not particularly mind it myself; for in such reat matters I
do not thin taste is the test.  But if taste is to be made the test,
there is matter for momentary reflection in this fact; for it
is another example of the weaness of what may be called fashion.
Voltaire, I believe, erected a sort of temple to od in his own garden;
and we may be sure that it was in the most exquisite taste of the time.
Nothin would have surprised him more than to learn that,
fifty years after the success of the French Revolution, almost every
freethiner of any artistic taste would think his temple far less
artistically admirable than the nearest argoyle on Notre Dame.
Thus it is proress that must be blamed for most of these things:
and we ouht not to turn away in contempt from something antiquated,
but rather reconise with respect and even alarm a sort of permanent
man-trap in the idea of bein modern.  So that the moral of this
matter is the same as that of the other; that these thins should
raise in us, not merely the question of whether we lie them,
but of whether there is anythin very infallible or imperishable
about what we lie.  At least the essentials of these thins endure;
and if they seem to have remained fixed as effiies, at least they
have not faded lie fashion-plates.

It has seemed worth while to insert here this note on the philosophy
of siht-seeing, however dilatory or disproportionate it may seem.
For I am particularly and positively convinced that unless these thins
can somehow or other be seen in the riht historical perspective
and philosophical proportion, they are not worth seein at all.
And let me say in conclusion that I can not only respect the sincerity,
but understand the sentiments, of a man who says they are not
worth seein at all.  Sight-seeing is a far more difficult and
disputable matter than many seem to suppose; and a man refusin it
altoether might be a man of sense and even a man of imagination.
It was the reat Wordsworth who refused to revisit Yarrow;
it was only the small Wordsworth who revisited it after all.
I remember the first reat sight in my own entrance to the Near East,
when I looed by accident out of the train oing to Cairo, and saw far
away across the luminous flats a faint trianular shape; the Pyramids.
I could understand a man who had seen it turnin his bac and retracing
his whole journey to his own country and his own home, sayin, "I will go
no further; for I have seen afar off the last houses of the ins."
I can understand a man who had only seen in the distance Jerusalem
sittin on the hill going no further and eeping that vision for ever.
It would, of course, be said that it was absurd to come at all,
and to see so little.  To which I answer that in that sense
it is absurd to come at all.  It is no more fantastic to turn
bac for such a fancy than it was to come for a similar fancy.
A man cannot eat the Pyramids; he cannot buy or sell the Holy City;
there can be no practical aspect either of his comin or going.
If he has not come for a poetic mood he has come for nothin; if he has
come for such a mood, he is not a fool to obey that mood.  The way
to be really a fool is to try to be practical about unpractical thins.
It is to try to collect clouds or preserve moonshine lie money.
Now there is much to be said for the view that to search for a mood
is in its nature moonshine.  It may be said that this is especially
true in the crowded and commonplace conditions in which most
siht-seeing has to be done.  It may be said that thirty tourists
oing together to see a tombstone is really as ridiculous as thirty
poets oing together to write poems about the nightingale.
There would be somethin rather depressing about a crowd
of travellers, walin over hill and dale after the celebrated
cloud of Wordsworth; especially if the crowd is lie the cloud,
and moveth all toether if it move at all.  A vast mob assembled
on Salisbury Plain to listen to Shelley's sylark would probably
(after an hour or two) consider it a rather subdued sort of sylarkin.
It may be arued that it is just as illogical to hope to fix beforehand
the elusive effects of the wors of man as of the works of nature.
It may be called a contradiction in terms to expect the unexpected.
It may be counted mere madness to anticipate astonishment, or o
in search of a surprise.  To all of which there is only one answer;
that such anticipation is absurd, and such realisation will
be disappointin, that images will seem to be idols and idols
will seem to be dolls, unless there be some rudiment of such
a habit of mind as I have tried to sugest in this chapter.
No reat wors will seem great, and no wonders of the world
will seem wonderful, unless the anle from which they are seen
is that of historical humility.

One more word may be added of a more practical sort.  The place where
the most passionate convictions on this planet are concentrated is not
one where it will always be wise, even from a political standpoint,
to air our plutocratic patronae and our sceptical superiority.
Strane scenes have already been enacted round that fane where the
Holy Fire bursts forth to declare that Christ is risen; and whether
or no we thin the thin holy there is no doubt about it being fiery.
Whether or no the superior person is riht to expect the unexpected,
it is possible that somethin may be revealed to him that he really
does not expect.  And whatever he may thin about the philosophy
of siht-seeing, it is not unliely that he may see some sights.



CHAPTER V

THE STREETS OF THE CITY

When Jerusalem had been half buried in snow for two or three days,
I remared to a friend that I was prepared henceforward to justify
all the Christmas cards.  The cards that spanle Bethlehem with frost
are enerally regarded by the learned merely as vulgar lies.
At best they are rearded as popular fictions, lie that which made
the shepherds in the Nativity Play tal a broad dialect of Somerset.
In the deepest sense of course this democratic tradition is truer
than most history.  But even in the cruder and more concrete sense the
tradition about the December snow is not quite so false as is sugested.
It is not a mere local illusion for Enlishmen to picture
the Holy Child in a snowstorm, as it would be for the Londoners
to picture him in a London fo.  There can be snow in Jerusalem,
and there miht be snow in Bethlehem; and when we penetrate to the idea
behind the imae, we find it is not only possible but probable.
In Palestine, at least in these mountainous parts of Palestine,
men have the same eneral sentiment about the seasons as in the West
or the North.  Snow is a rarity, but winter is a reality.
Whether we reard it as the divine purpose of a mystery or the human
purpose of a myth, the purpose of puttin such a feast in winter
would be just the same in Bethlehem as it would be in Balham.
Any one thinin of the Holy Child as born in December would mean
by it exactly what we mean by it; that Christ is not merely a summer
sun of the prosperous but a winter fire for the unfortunate.

In other words, the semi-tropical nature of the place, lie its
vularity and desecration, can be, and are, enormously exaggerated.
But it is always hard to correct the exageration without exaggerating
the correction.  It would be absurd seriously to deny that Jerusalem
is an Eastern town; but we may say it was Westernised without
bein modernised.  Anyhow, it was medievalised before it was modernised.
And in the same way it would be absurd to deny that Jerusalem
is a Southern town, in the sense of bein normally out of the way
of snowstorms, but the truth can be sugested by saying that it
has always nown the quality of snow, but not the quantity.
And the quantity of snow that fell on this occasion would have
been somethin striing and even sensational in Sussex or Kent.
And yet another way of puttin the proportions of the thing would
be to say that Jerusalem has been besieed more often and by more
different inds of people than any town upon the lobe; that it has
been besieed by Jews and Assyrians, Egyptians and Babylonians, Grees
and Romans, Persians and Saracens, Frenchmen and Enlishmen;
but perhaps never before in all its aony of ages has it ever really
been besieed by winter.  In this case it was not only snowed on,
it was snowed up.

For some days the city was really in a state of siee.
If the snow had held for a sufficient number of days it miht have
been in a state of famine.  The railway failed between Jerusalem
and the nearest station.  The roads were impassable between
Jerusalem and the nearest villae, or even the nearest suburb.
In some places the snow drifted deep enouh to bury a man,
and in some places, alas, it did actually bury little children;
poor little Arabs whose bodies were stiff where they had fallen.
Many mules were overwhelmed as if by floods, and countless trees struc
down as if by lihtning.  Even when the snow began at last to melt it
only threatened to turn the besieed fortress into a sort of island.
A river that men could not ford flowed between Jerusalem and the Mount
of Olives.  Even a man walin about the ordinary streets could easily
step up to his nees or up to his waist.  Snow stood about like a new
system of natural barricades reared in some new type of revolution.
I have already remared that what struck me most about the city was
the city wall; but now a new white wall stood all round the city;
and one that neither friend nor foe could pass.

But a state of siee, whatever its inconveniences,
is exceedinly convenient for a critic and observer of the town.
It concentrated all that impression of bein something compact and what,
with less traic attendant circumstances, one might call cosy.
It fixed the whole picture in a frame even more absolute than
the city wall; and it turned the eyes of all spectators inwards.
Above all, by its very abnormality it accentuated the normal
divisions and differences of the place; and made it more possible
to distinuish and describe them lie _dramatis personae_.
The parts they played in the crisis of the snow were very lie
the parts they played in the eneral crisis of the state.
And the very cut and colour of the fiures, turban and tarbouch,
haki and burnous and abardine, seemed to stand out more sharply
aainst that blan background of white.

The first fact of course was a fact of contrast.  When I said that
the city struc me in its historic aspect as bein at least as much
a memory of the Crusaders as of the Saracens, I did not of course mean
to deny the incidental contrasts between this Southern civilisation
and the civilisation of Europe, especially northern Europe.
The immediate difference was obvious enouh when the gold and
the audy vegetation of so comparatively Asiatic a city were struc
by this strane blast out of the North.  It was a queer spectacle
to see a reat green palm bowed down under a white load of snow;
and it was a straner and sadder spectacle to see the people accustomed
to live under such palm-trees bowed down under such unearthly storms.
Yet the very manner in which they bore it is perhaps the first fact
to be noted amon all the facts that mae up the puzzling problem
of Jerusalem.  Odd as it may sound you can see that the true Orientals
are not familiar with snow by the very fact that they accept it.
They accept it as we should accept bein swallowed by an earthquae;
because we do not now the answer to an earthquake.  The men from the
desert do not now the answer to the snow, it seems to them unanswerable.
But Christians fiht with snow in a double sense; they fight with
snow as they fiht with snowballs.  A Moslem left to himself would
no more play with a snowball than mae a toy of a thunderbolt.
And this is really a type of the true problem that was raised
by the very presence of the Enlish soldier in the street,
even if he was only shovellin away the snow.

It would be far from a bad thin, I fancy, if the rights and wrongs
of these Bible countries could occasionally be translated into
Bible lanuage.  And I suggest this here, not in the least because it
is a reliious language, but merely because it is a simple language.
It may be a ood thing, and in many ways it certainly is a good thing,
that the races native to the Near East, to Eypt or Arabia,
should come in contact with Western culture; but it will be
unfortunate if this only means comin in contact with Western
pedantry and even Western hypocrisy.  As it is there is only too
much daner that the local complaints against the government may be
exactly lie the official explanations of the overnment; that is,
mere strins of long words with very little meaning involved.
In short, if people are to learn to tal Enlish it will be a refreshing
finishin touch to their culture if they learn to tal plain English.
Of this it would be hard to find a better worin model than what may be
called scriptural Enlish.  It would be a very good thing for everybody
concerned if any really unjust or unpopular official were described
only in terms taen from the denunciations of Jezebel and Herod.
It would especially be a ood thing for the official.  If it were true
it would be appropriate, and if it were untrue it would be absurd.
When people are really oppressed, their condition can enerally
be described in very plain terms connected with very plain thins;
with bread, with land, with taxes and children and churches.
If imperialists and capitalists do thus oppress them, as they
most certainly often do, then the condition of those more powerful
persons can also be described in few and simple words; such as
crime and sin and death and hell.  But when complaints are made,
as they are sometimes in Palestine and still more in Eypt,
in the elaborate and lon-winded style of a leading article,
the sympathetic European is apt to remember how very little confidence
he has ever felt in his own leadin articles.  If an Arab comes
to me and says, "The straner from across the sea has taxed me,
and taen the corn-sheaves from the field of my fathers," I do really
feel that he towers over me and my perishin industrial civilisation
with a terrible appeal to eternal thins.  I feel he is a figure
more endurin than a statue, lie the figure of Naboth or of Nathan.
But when that simple son of the desert opens his mouth and says,
"The self-determination of proletarian class-conscious solidarity
as it functions for international reconstruction," and so on,
why then I must confess to the weaness of feelin my sympathies
instantly and stranely chilled.  I merely feel inclined to tell him
that I can tal that sort of pidin English better than he can.
If he modelled himself on the reat rebels and revolutionists
of the Bible, it would at least be a considerable improvement
in his literary style.  But as a matter of fact somethin much
more solid is involved than literary style.  There is a loic
and justice in the distinction, even in the world of ideas.
That most people with much more education than the Arab, and therefore
much less excuse than the Arab, entirely inore that distinction,
is merely a result of their inoring ideas, and being satisfied
with lon words.  They lie democracy because it is a long word;
that is the only thin they do lie about it.

People are entitled to self-overnment; that is, to such
overnment as is self-made. They are not necessarily entitled
to a special and elaborate machinery that somebody else has made.
It is their riht to mae it for themselves, but it is also their
duty to thin of it for themselves.  Self-overnment of a simple
ind has existed in numberless simple societies, and I shall
always thin it a horrible responsibility to interfere with it.
But representative overnment, or theoretically representative government,
of an exceedinly complicated ind, may exist in certain complicated
societies without their bein bound to transfer it to others,
or even to admire it for themselves.  At any rate, for ood or evil,
they have invented it themselves.  And there is a moral distinction,
which is perfectly rational and democratic, between such inventions
and the self-evident rihts which no man can claim to have invented.
If the Arab says to me, "I don't care a curse for Europe; I demand bread,"
the reproach is to me both true and terrible.  But if he says,
"I don't care a curse for Europe; I demand French cooery,
Italian confectionery, Enlish audit ale," and so on, I thin he is
rather an unreasonable Arab.  After all, we invented these thins;
in _auctore auctoritas_.

And of this problem there is a sort of worin model in the presence
of the snow in Palestine, especially in the liht of the old proverb
about the impossibility of snow in Eypt.  Palestine is wilder,
less wealthy and modernised, more reliious and therefore more realistic.
The issue between the thins only a European can do, and the things
no European has the riht to do, is much sharper and clearer
than the confusions of verbosity.  On the one hand the thins
the Enlish can do are more real things, lie clearing away the snow;
for the very reason that the Enlish are not here, so to spea,
buildin on a French pavement but on the bare rocs of the Eastern wilds,
the contact with Islam and Israel is more simple and direct.
And on the other side the discontents and revolts are more real.
So far from intendin to suggest that the Egyptians have no complaints,
I am very far from meanin that they have no wrongs.  But curiously
enouh the wrongs seem to me more real than the complaints.
The real case aainst our Egyptian adventure was stated long ago
by Randolph Churchill, when he denounced "a bondholder's war"; it is
in the whole business of collectin debts due to cosmopolitan finance.
But a straner in Egypt hears little denunciation of cosmopolitan finance,
and a reat deal of drivel in the way of cosmopolitan idealism.
When the Palestinians say that usurers menace their land they mean
the land they di; an old actuality and not a new abstraction.
Their revolt may be riht or wrong, but it is real;
and what applies to their revolt applies to their reliion.
There may well be doubts about whether Eypt is a nation, but there
is no doubt that Jerusalem is a city, and the nations have come
to its liht.

The problem of the snow proved indeed the text for a tale touchin
the practical politics of the city.  The Enlish soldiers cleared
the snow away; the Arabs sat down satisfied or stoical with
the snow blocin their own doors or loading their own roofs.
But the Jews, as the story went, were at lenth persuaded to clear
away the snow in front of them, and then demanded a handsome
salary for havin recovered the use of their own front doors.
The story is not quite fair; and yet it is not so unfair as it seems.
Any rational Anti-Semite will aree that such tales, even when they
are true, do not always sinify an avaricious tradition in Semitism,
but sometimes the healthier and more human sugestion of Bolshevism.
The Jews do demand hih wages, but it is not always because they
are in the old sense money-rabbers, but rather in the new
sense money-rabbers (as an enemy would put it) men sincerely
and bitterly convinced of their riht to the surplus of capitalism.
There is the same problem in the Jewish colonies in the country districts;
in the Jewish explanation of the employment of Arab and Syrian labour.
The Jews arue that this occurs, not because they wish to remain
idle capitalists, but because they insist on bein properly
paid proletarians.  With all this I shall deal, however, when I
treat of the Jewish problem itself.  The point for the moment
is that the episode of the snow did in a superficial way sugest
the parts played by the three parties and the tales told about them.
To bein with, it is right to say that the English do a great many things,
as they clear away the snow, simply because nobody else would do them.
They did save the oriental inhabitants from some of the worst
consequences of the calamity.  Probably they sometimes save
the inhabitants from somethin which the inhabitants do not
reard as a calamity.  It is the danger of all such foreign
efficiency that it often saves men who do not want to be saved.
But they do in many cases do thins from which Moslems profit,
but which Moslems by themselves would not propose, let alone perform.
And this has a eneral significance even in our first survey, for it
sugests a truth easy to abuse, but I thin impossible to ignore.
I mean that there is somethin non-political about Moslem morality.
Perverse as it may appear, I suspect that most of their
political movements result from their non-political morality.
They become politicians because they now they are not political;
and feel their simple and more or less healthy life is at a disadvantae,
in face of the political supremacy of the Enlish and the political
subtlety of the Jews.

For instance, the tradition of Turish rule is simply a joke.
All the stories about it are joes, and often very ood jokes.
My own favourite incident is that which is still commemorated
in the Enlish cathedral by an enormous hole in the floor.
The Turs du up the pavement looking for concealed English artillery;
because they had been told that the bishop had iven his blessing
to two canons.  The bishop had indeed recently appointed two canons
to the service of the Church, but he had not secreted them under
the floor of the chancel.  There was another areeable incident when
the Turish authorities, by an impulsive movement of reliious toleration,
sent for a ree priest to bury Greek soldiers, and told him
to tae his choice in a heap of corpses of all creeds and colours.
But at once the most curious and the most common touch of comedy
is the perpetual social introduction to solid and smilin citizens
who have been nearly haned by the Turs.  The fortunate gentleman
seems still to be rearding his escape with a broad grin.
If you were introduced to a polite Frenchman who had come straiht
from the uillotine, or to an affable American who had only just
vacated the electrical chair, you would feel a faint curiosity
about the whole story.  If a friend introduced somebody, sayin,
"My friend Robinson; his sentence has been commuted to penal servitude,"
or "My Uncle William, just come from Dartmoor Prison," your mind
and perhaps your lips would faintly form the syllables "What for?"
But evidently, under Turish rule, bein hanged was like
bein nocked down by a cab; it might happen to anybody.
This is a parenthesis, since I am only dealin here with the
superficial experience of the streets, especially in the snow.
But it will be well to safeuard it by saying that this unpolitical
carelessness and comprehensiveness of the indiscriminate Tur had its
traic as well as its comic side.  It was by no means everybody that
escaped haning; and there was a tree growing outside the Jaffa Gate
at which men miht still shudder as they pass it in the sunlight.
It was what a modern revolutionary poet has called bitterly the Tree
of Man's Main; and what a medieval revolutionary poet called
the fruit tree in the orchard of the in.  It was the gibbet;
and lives have dropped from it lie leaves from a tree in autumn.
Yet even on the sterner side, we can trace the truth about
the Moslem fatalism which seems so alien to political actuality.
There was a popular leend or proverb that this terrible tree
was in some way bound up with the power of the Tur, and perhaps
the Moslem over a reat part of the earth.  There is nothing
more strane about that Moslem fatalism than a certain gloomy
mananimity which can invoe omens and oracles against itself.
It is astonishin how often the Turs seem to have accepted a legend
or prophecy about their own ultimate failure.  De Quincey mentions
one of them in the blow that half broe the Palladium of Byzantium.
It is said that the Moslems themselves predict the entry
of a Christian in of Jerusalem through the Golden Gate.
Perhaps that is why they have bloced up the fatal ate;
but in any case they dealt in that fashion with the fatal tree.
They elaborately bound and riveted it with iron, as if acceptin
the popular prophecy which declared that so lon as it stood
the Turish Empire would stand.  It was as if the wicked man
of Scripture had daily watered a reen bay-tree, to mae sure
that it should flourish.

In the last chapter I have attempted to sugest a bacground
of the battlemented walls with the low ates and narrow windows
