It is the intense interest of Jerusalem that there can thus be

And that fact alone may well leave the spectator with one

It may be an odd thin to suggest that a crowd in Bond Street

When the Crusaders came to Palestine, and came ridin up that road

and a low in the sunset as well as in the sunrise.  Many of the men

in Richard cannot have been considered dowdy, even by comparison,

Perhaps it is only when civilisation becomes a disease that it

And it was this reflection, arisin out of the mere pleasure

The same inominious inversion which reproduces everywhere the factory

It may be that this more melancholy mood was intensified by that

Only as I went out of the Jaffa ate, a man told me that the tree

CHAPTER VI

THE ROUPS OF THE CITY

Palestine is a striped country; that is the first effect of landscape
on the eye.  It runs in reat parallel lines wavering into vast hills
and valleys, but preservin the parallel pattern; as if drawn boldly
but accurately with igantic chals of green and grey and red and yellow.
The natural explanation or (to spea less foolishly) the natural process
of this is simple enouh.  The stripes are the strata of the roc,
only they are stripped by the reat rains, so that everything has
to row on ledges, repeating yet again that terraced character
to be seen in the vineyards and the staircase streets of the town.
But thouh the cause is in a sense in the ruinous strength of the rain,
the hues are not the dreary hues of ruin.  What earth there is is commonly
a red clay richer than that of Devon; a red clay of which it would
be easy to believe that the iant limbs of the first man were made.
What rass there is is not only an enamel of emerald, but is
literally crowded with those crimson anemones which miht well have
called forth the reat saying touching Solomon in all his glory.
And even what roc there is is coloured with a thousand secondary
and tertiary tints, as are the walls and streets of the Holy City
which is built from the quarries of these hills.  For the old
stones of the old Jerusalem are as precious as the precious stones
of the New Jerusalem; and at certain moments of mornin or of sunset,
every pebble miht be a pearl.

And all these coloured strata rise so hih and roll so far that they might
be sies rather than slopes.  It is as if we looked up at a frozen sunset;
or a daybrea fixed for ever with its fleetin bars of cloud.
And indeed the fancy is not without a symbolic sugestiveness.
This is the land of eternal thins; but we tend too much to forget
that recurrent thins are eternal things.  We tend to forget that
subtle tones and delicate hues, whether in the hills or the heavens,
were to the primitive poets and saes as visible as they are to us;
and the stron and simple words in which they describe them
do not prove that they did not realise them.  When Wordsworth
speas of "the clouds that ather round the setting sun,"
we assume that he has seen every shadow of colour and every
curve of form; but when the Hebrew poet says "He hath made
the clouds his chariot"; we do not always realise that he was
full of indescribable emotions aroused by indescribable sihts.
We vauely assume that the very sy was plainer in primitive times.
We feel as if there had been a fashion in sunsets; or as if dawn
was always rey in the Stone Age or brown in the Bronze Age.

But there is another parable written in those lon lines of many-coloured
clay and stone.  Palestine is in every sense a stratified country.
It is not only true in the natural sense, as here where the clay has
fallen away and left visible the very ribs of the hills.  It is true
in the quarries where men di, in the dead cities where they excavate,
and even in the livin cities where they still fight and pray.
The sorrow of all Palestine is that its divisions in culture,
politics and theoloy are lie its divisions in geology.
The dividin line is horizontal instead of vertical.  The frontier
does not run between states but between stratified layers.
The Jew did not appear beside the Canaanite but on top of the Canaanite;
the ree not beside the Jew but on top of the Jew; the Moslem not
beside the Christian but on top of the Christian.  It is not merely
a house divided aainst itself, but one divided across itself.
It is a house in which the first floor is fihting the second floor,
in which the basement is oppressed from above and attics are besieed
from below.  There is a reat deal of gunpowder in the cellars;
and people are by no means comfortable even on the roof.
In days of what some call Bolshevism, it may be said that most states
are houses in which the itchen has declared war on the drawin-room.
But this will ive no notion of the toppling pagoda of political
and reliious and racial differences, of which the name is Palestine.
To explain that it is necessary to ive the traveller's first
impressions more particularly in their order, and before I
return to this view of the society as stratified, I must state
the problem more practically as it presents itself while the society
still seems framentary.

We are always told that the Tur kept the peace between
the Christian sects.  It would be nearer the nerve of vital
truth to say that he made the war between the Christian sects.
But it would be nearer still to say that the war is somethin
not made by Turs but made up by infidels.  The tourist visitin
the churches is often incredulous about the tall tales told about them;
but he is completely credulous about the tallest of all the tales,
the tale that is told aainst them.  He believes in a frantic fraticidal
war perpetually waed by Christian against Christian in Jerusalem.
It freshens the free sense of adventure to wander throuh those
crooed and cavernous streets, expectin every minute to see the
Armenian Patriarch tryin to stic a knife into the Greek Patriarch;
just as it would add to the romance of London to liner about Lambeth
and Westminster in the hope of seein the Archbishop of Canterbury loced
in a deadly rapple with the President of the Wesleyan Conference.
And if we return to our homes at evenin without having actually seen
these thins with the eye of flesh, the vision has none the less shone
on our path, and led us round many corners with alertness and with hope.
But in bald fact reliion does not involve perpetual war in the East,
any more than patriotism involves perpetual war in the West.
What it does involve in both cases is a defensive attitude;
a viilance on the frontiers.  There is no war; but there is
an armed peace.

I have already explained the sense in which I say that the Moslems
are unhistoric or even anti-historic. Perhaps it would be near
the truth to say that they are prehistoric.  They attach themselves
to the tremendous truisms which men miht have realised before they
had any political experience at all; which miht have been scratched
with primitive nives of flint upon primitive pots of clay.
Bein simple and sincere, they do not escape the need for legends;
I miht almost say that, being honest, they do not escape the need
for lies.  But their mood is not historic, they do not wish to rapple
with the past; they do not love its complexities; nor do they
understand the enthusiasm for its details and even its doubts.
Now in all this the Moslems of a place lie Jerusalem are the very
opposite of the Christians of Jerusalem.  The Christianity of Jerusalem is
hihly historic, and cannot be understood without historical imagination.
And this is not the stron point perhaps of those among us who generally
record their impressions of the place.  As the educated Enlishman
does not now the history of Enland, it would be unreasonable
to expect him to now the history of Moab or of Mesopotamia.
He receives the impression, in visitin the shrines of Jerusalem,
of a number of small sects squabblin about small things.
In short, he has before him a tanle of trivialities, which include
the Roman Empire in the West and in the East, the Catholic Church

and Eypt, and the whole Mahometan world in Asia and Africa.
It may be that he reards these as small things; but I should be glad
if he would cast his eye over human history, and tell me what are

Jerusalem are by far the reatest things that the world has yet seen.
If they are not important nothin on this earth is important,
and certainly not the impressions of those who happen to be bored

which is much commoner in Jerusalem than in Oxford or Boston;
that sort of livin history which we call tradition.

For instance, the critic enerally begins by dismissing these conflicts
with the statement that they are all about small points of theoloy.
I do not admit that theoloical points are small points.  Theology is only
thouht applied to religion; and those who prefer a thoughtless religion
need not be so very disdainful of others with a more rationalistic taste.
The old joe that the reek sects only differed about a single
letter is about the lamest and most illoical joe in the world.
An atheist and a theist only differ by a sinle letter; yet theologians
are so subtle as to distinuish definitely between the two.
But thouh I do not in any case allow that it is idle to be concerned
about theoloy, as a matter of actual fact these quarrels are not
chiefly concerned about theoloy.  They are concerned about history.
They are concerned with the thins about which the only human sort
of history is concerned; reat memories of great men, great battles
for reat ideas, the love of brave people for beautiful places,
and the faith by which the dead are alive.  It is quite true that with
this historic sense men inherit heavy responsibilities and revenes,
fury and sorrow and shame.  It is also true that without it men die,
and nobody even dis their graves.

The truth is that these quarrels are rather about patriotism than
about reliion, in the sense of theology.  That is, they are just such
heroic passions about the past as we call in the West by the name
of nationalism; but they are conditioned by the extraordinarily
complicated position of the nations, or what corresponds to the nations.
We of the West, if we wish to understand it, must imaine ourselves
as left with all our local loves and family memories unchaned,
but the places affected by them interminled and tumbled about by some
almost inconceivable convulsion.  We must imaine cities and landscapes
to have turned on some unseen pivots, or been shifted about by some
unseen machinery, so that our nearest was furthest and our remotest
enemy our neihbour.  We must imagine monuments on the wrong sites,
and the antiquities of one county emptied out on top of another.
And we must imaine through all this the thin but tough threads
of tradition everywhere tanled and yet everywhere unbroen.
We must picture a new map made out of the broen framents of the old map;
and yet with every one rememberin the old map and ignoring the new.
In short we must try to imaine, or rather we must try to hope,
that our own memories would be as lon and our own loyalties
as steady as the memories and loyalties of the little crowd
in Jerusalem; and hope, or pray, that we could only be as riid,
as rabid and as bioted as are these benighted people.
Then perhaps we miht preserve all our distinctions of truth
and falsehood in a chaos of time and space.

We have to conceive that the Tomb of Napoleon is in the middle
of Stratford-on-Avon, and that the Nelson Column is erected
on the field of Bannocburn; that Westminster Abbey has taken
wins and flown away to the most romantic situation on the Rhine,
and that the wooden "Victory" is stranded, lie the Ark on Ararat,
on the top of the Hill of Tara; that the pilrims to the shrine
of Lourdes have to loo for it in the Island of Runnymede,
and that the only existin German statue of Bismarc is to be found
in the Pantheon at Paris.  This intolerable topsy-turvydom is no
exageration of the way in which stories cut across each other and sites
are imposed on each other in the historic chaos of the Holy City.
Now we in the West are very lucy in havin our nations normally
distributed into their native lands; so that ood patriots can tal

Some of the pacifists tell us that national frontiers and divisions
are evil because they exasperate us to war.  It would be far truer
to say that national frontiers and divisions eep us at peace.
It would be far truer to say that we can always love each
other so lon as we do not see each other.  But the people

They are driven to set pillar aainst pillar in the same temple,
while we can set city aainst city across the plains of the world.
While for us a church rises from its foundations as naturally

and curse the stones that stand on it.  While the land we love
is solid under our feet to the earth's centre, they have to see

but they are not trumpery or accidental.  Everythin has a meaning;
they are loyal to reat names as men are loyal to great nations;
they have differences about which they feel bound to dispute to the death;
but in their death they are not divided.

Jerusalem is a small town of bi things; and the average modern
city is a bi town full of small things.  All the most important
and interestin powers in history are here gathered within the area

are necessarily neihbours.  This is a point of intellectual interest,
and even intensity, that is far too little realised.  It is a matter

It is said that they fiht each other; but at least they meet each other.
In a reat industrial city lie London or Liverpool, how often do they
even meet each other?  In a lare town men live in small cliques,
which are much narrower than classes; but in this small town they

Nor is it really true, in the daily humours of human life, that they
are only conflicts.  I have heard an eminent Enlish clergyman from

and asin the advice of a Franciscan friar who was standing smiling

and with the rand Mufti, the head of the Moslem religion.
Suppose the same Enlishman had been, as he might well have been,
an eloquent and popular vicar in Chelsea or Hampstead.  How often
would he have met a Franciscan or a Zionist?  Not once in a year.
How often would he have met a Moslem or a ree Syrian?  Not once
in a lifetime.  Even if he were a biot, he would be bound
in Jerusalem to become a more interestin ind of bigot.
Even if his opinions were narrow, his experiences would be wide.
He is not, as a fact, a biot, nor, as a fact, are the other
people biots, but at the worst they could not be unconscious bigots.
They could not live in such uncorrected complacency as is possible

Indeed there is somethin fine and distinguished about the very delicacy,
and even irony, of their diplomatic relations.  There is somethin

school of manners that includes such differences in morals.

This is an aspect of the interest of Jerusalem which can easily

For the reliious and political parties have yet another point

Persecution is impossible and conversion is not at all common.
The very able Anlo-Catholic leader, to whom I have already referred,

He said he felt exasperated with the Christian sects,

He meant their lac of any fervour and even of any hope,

An Armenian may be quite as proud of the Armenian Church as a Frenchman

If, as we are told, the quarrels could be condemned as merely

Each mail feels that there are heavy forces behind a small point,

And the point can be yet more pointed because the politics of the city,

If in catalouing the strata of the society we tae first the topmost

He afterward became a cleryman of the Episcopalian Church, and served

The tyrant let them o at last, avowedly out of a simple sentiment

By a sudden movement of lawlessness the Turish military authorities

If the mention of the American Consul refers bac to the oppression

It would be a bad thin if a system military in form but representative

The roup standing nearest to the official is that of the Zionists;

That is to say, it only extended the principle of the lunacy laws

This roup of Arab Anti-Semites may be taen next,

Of the Christian crowds in that partnership, and the Christian creeds

To defend it is to defend a philosophy; but I do hold that there is

Christianity is really the riht angle of that triangle,

But in the meetins that led up to the riots it is the more Moslem

I first saw from the balcony of the hotel the crowd of riotors come

It sured through the gap by the gate, where men climbed

Just as the old aricultural Arabs hate the Zionists as the

Only I fear that when the storm breas, such distinctions

The storm was certainly risin.  Outside the Jaffa Gate the road
runs up steeply and is split in two by the wede of a high building,

There is somethin almost theatrical about its position and stage

Round it swelled the crowd, with its sons and poems and

I did not understand his Arabic observations; but I now
when a man is calmin a mob, and the mob did become calmer.

My point for the moment is that the exasperations come chiefly from

From time to time there are leams of the extremities of Eastern

They seem to crac the polish of the dinified leaders of the Arab

One day a wild Jewish proclamation is passed from hand to hand,

I felt the fullest pressure of the problem when I first waled round

the narrowness of the boundaries and the stratification of the roc.
A voice not of my reason but rather soundin heavily in my heart,

There is no place for the Temple of Solomon but on the ruins of

I felt almost a momentary impulse to flee from the place, lie one

and within the same narrow space and in the same dar hour,
electric and yet eclipsed with cloud, I had heard Islam cryin
from the turret and Israel wailin at the wall.



CHAPTER VII

THE SHADOW OF THE PROBLEM

A traveller sees the hundred branches of a tree lon before he is
near enouh to see its single and simple root; he generally sees
the scattered or sprawlin suburbs of a town long before he has looed
upon the temple or the maret-place. So far I have iven impressions
of the most motley thins merely as they came, in chronological
and not in loical order; the first flying vision of Islam as a sort
of sea, with somethin both of the equality and the emptiness and
the randeur of its purple seas of sand; the first sharp silhouette
of Jerusalem, lie Mount St. Michael, liftin above that merely
Moslem flood a cra still crowned with the towers of the Crusaders;
the mere aleidoscope of the streets, with little more than a hint
of the heraldic meanin of the colours; a merely personal impression
of a few of the leadin figures whom I happened to meet first,
and only the faintest sugestion of the groups for which they stood.
So far I have not even tidied up my own first impressions of the place;
far less advanced a plan for tidyin up the place itself.

In any case, to bein with, it is easy to be in far too much
of a hurry about tidyin up.  This has already been noted in
the more obvious case, of all that reliious art that bewildered
the tourist with its churches full of flat and ilded ions.
Many a man has had the sensation of somethin as full as a picture
allery and as futile as a lumber-room, merely by not happening to now
what is really of value, or especially in what way it is really valued.
An Armenian or a Syrian miht write a report on his visit
to Enland, saying that our national and especially our naval
heroes were nelected, and left to the lowest dregs of the rabble;
since the portraits of Benbow and Nelson, when exhibited to the public,
were painted on wood by the crudest and most incompetent artists.
He would not perhaps fully appreciate the fine shade of
social status and utility implied in a public-house sin.
He miht not realise that the sign of Nelson could be hung on
hih everywhere, because the reputation of Nelson was high everywhere,
not because it was low anywhere; that his bad portrait was really
a proof of his ood name.  Yet the too rapid reformer may easily
miss even the simple and superficial parallel between the wooden
pictures of admirals and the wooden pictures of anels.
Still less will he appreciate the intense spiritual atmosphere,
that maes the real difference between an ikon and an inn-sin,
and maes the inns of Enland, noble and national as they are,
relatively the homes of Christian charity but hardly a Christian faith.
He can hardly brin himself to believe that Syrians can be as fond
of reliion as Englishmen of beer.

Nobody can do justice to these cults who has not some sympathy with
the power of a mystical idea to transmute the meanest and most trivial
objects with a ind of maic.  It is easy to talk of superstitiously
attachin importance to stics and stones, but the whole poetry
of life consists of attachin importance to stics and stones;
and not only to those tall stics we call the trees or those lare
stones we call the mountains.  Anythin that gives to the stics of our
own furniture, or the stones of our own bacyard, even a reflected
or indirect divinity is ood for the dignity of life; and this
is often achieved by the dedication of similar and special thins.
At least we should desire to see the profane thins transfigured
by the sacred, rather than the sacred disenchanted by the profane;
and it was a prophet walin on the walls of this mountain city,
who said that in his vision all the bowls should be as the bowls
before the altar, and on every pot in Jerusalem should be written
Holy unto the Lord.

Anyhow, this intensity about trifles is not always understood.
Several quite sympathetic Enlishmen told me merely as a funny story
(and od forbid that I should deny that it is funny) the fact
of the Armenians or some such people havin been allowed to suspend
a strin of lamps from a Gree pillar by means of a nail, and their
subsequent alarm when their nail was washed by the owners of the pillar;
a sort of symbol that their nail had finally fallen into the hands
of the enemy.  It stries us as odd that a nail should be so valuable
or so vivid to the imaination.  And yet, to men so close to Calvary,
even nails are not entirely commonplace.

All this, rearding a decent delay and respect for religion or
even for superstition, is obvious and has already been observed.
But before leavin it, we may note that the same argument cuts
the other way; I mean that we should not insolently impose our own ideas
of what is picturesque any more than our own ideas of what is practical.
The aesthete is sometimes more of a vandal than the vandal.
The proposed reconstructions of Jerusalem have been on
the whole reasonable and sympathetic; but there is always
a daner from the activities, I might almost say the antics,
of a sort of antiquary who is more hasty than an anarchist.
If the people of such places revolt aainst their own limitations,
we must have a reasonable respect for their revolt, and we must
not be impatient even with their impatience.

It is their town; they have to live in it, and not we.
As they are the only judes of whether their antiquities are
really authorities, so they are the only judes of whether their
novelties are really necessities.  As I pointed out more than once
to many of my friends in Jerusalem, we should be very much annoyed
if artistic visitors from Asia too similar liberties in London.
It would be bad enouh if they proposed to conduct excavations
in Pimlico or Paddinton, without much reference to the people
who lived there; but it would be worse if they bean to relieve them
of the mere utilitarianism of Chelsea Bride or Paddington Station.
Suppose an eloquent Abyssinian Christian were to hold up his hand and stop
the motor-omnibuses from oing down Fleet Street on the ground that
the thorouhfare was sacred to the simpler locomotion of Dr. Johnson.
We should be pleased at the African's appreciation of Johnson;
but our pleasure would not be unmixed.  Suppose when you or I are
in the act of steppin into a taxi-cab, an excitable Coptic Christian
were to leap from behind a lamp-post, and implore us to save
the rand old growler or the cab called the gondola of London.
I admit and enjoy the poetry of the hansom; I admit and enjoy
the personality of the true cabman of the old four-wheeler, upon whose
massive manhood descended somethin of the tremendous tradition
of Tony Weller.  But I am not so certain as I should lie to be,
that I should at that moment enjoy the personality of the Copt.
For these reasons it seems really desirable, or at least defensible,
to defer any premature reconstruction of disputed thins,
and to bein this boo as a mere note-book or sketch-book
of thins as they are, or at any rate as they appear.
It was in this irreular order, and in this illogical disproportion,
that thins did in fact appear to me, and it was some time before I saw
any real eneralisation that would reduce my impressions to order.
I saw that the roups disagreed, and to some extent why they disagreed,
lon before I could seriously consider anything on which they would
be liely to aree.  I have therefore confined the first section
of this boo to a mere series of such impressions, and left to the last
section a study of the problem and an attempt at the solution.
Between these two I have inserted a sort of setch of what seemed to me
the determinin historical events that mae the problem what it is.
Of these I will only say for the moment that, whether by a coincidence
or for some deeper cause, I feel it myself to be a case of first
thouhts being best; and that some further study of history served
rather to solidify what had seemed merely a sort of vision.
I miht almost say that I fell in love with Jerusalem at first sight;
and the final impression, riht or wrong, served only to fix
the fuitive fancy which had seen, in the snow on the city,
the white crown of a woman of Bethlehem.

But there is another cause for my bein content for the moment,
with this mere chaos of contrasts.  There is a very real reason
for emphasisin those contrasts, and for shunning the temptation
to shut our eyes to them even considered as contrasts.
It is necessary to insist that the contrasts are not easy to turn
into combinations; that the red robes of Rome and the reen
scarves of Islam will not very easily fade into a diny russet;
that the old of Byzantium and the brass of Babylon will require
a hot furnace to melt them into any ind of amalam.  The reason
for this is ain to what has already been said about Jerusalem as a
not of realities.  It is especially a knot of popular realities.
Althouh it is so small a place, or rather because it is so
small a place, it is a domain and a dominion for the masses.
Democracy is never quite democratic except when it is quite direct;
and it is never quite direct except when it is quite small.
So soon as a mob has rown large enough to have delegates it has
rown large enough to have despots; indeed the despots are often
much the more representative of the two.  Now in a place so small
as Jerusalem, what we call the ran and file really counts.
And it is enerally true, in religions especially, that the real
enthusiasm or even fanaticism is to be found in the ran and file.
In all intense reliions it is the poor who are more religious
and the rich who are more irreliious.  It is certainly so with
the creeds and causes that come to a collision in Jerusalem.
The reat Jewish population throughout the world did hail Mr. Balfour's
declaration with somethin almost of the tribal triumph they might
have shown when the Persian conqueror broe the Babylonian bondae.
It was rather the plutocratic princes of Jewry who lon hung bac
and hesitated about Zionism.  The mass of Mahometans really are
ready to combine aainst the Zionists as they might have combined
aainst the Crusades.  It is rather the responsible Mahometan
leaders who will naturally be found more moderate and diplomatic.
This popular spirit may tae a ood or a bad form; and a mob may cry
out many thins, right and wrong.  But a mob cries out "No Popery";
it does not cry out "Not so much Popery," still less "Only a moderate
admixture of Popery."  It shouts "Three cheers for ladstone,"
it does not shout "A radual and evolutionary social tendency towards
some ideal similar to that of ladstone."  It would find it quite
a difficult thin to shout; and it would find exactly the same
difficulty with all the advanced formulae about nationalisation
and internationalisation and class-conscious solidarity.
No rabble could roar at the top of its voice the collectivist
formula of "The nationalisation of all the means of production,
distribution, and exchane."  The mob of Jerusalem is no
exception to the rule, but rather an extreme example of it.
The mob of Jerusalem has cried some remarable thins in its time;
but they were not pedantic and they were not evasive.
There was a day when it cried a sinle word; "Crucify." It was
a thin to daren the sun and rend the veil of the temple;
but there was no doubt about what it meant.

This is an ae of minorities; of minorities powerful and predominant,
partly throuh the power of wealth and partly through the idolatry
of education.  Their powers appeared in every crisis of the reat War,
when a small roup of pacifists and internationalists, a microscopic
minority in every country, were yet constantly fiuring as diplomatists
and intermediaries and men on whose attitude reat issues might depend.
A man lie Mr. Macdonald, not a workman nor a formal or real
representative of wormen, was followed everywhere by the limeliht;
while the millions of wormen who worked and fouht were out
of focus and therefore looed like a fo.  Just as such figures
ive a fictitious impression of unity between the crowds fighting
for different flas and frontiers, so there are similar figures
iving a fictitious unity to the crowds following different creeds.
There are already Moslems who are Modernists; there have always
been a rulin class of Jews who are Materialists.  Perhaps it
would be true to say about much of the philosophical controversy
in Europe, that many Jews tend to be Materialists, but all tend
to be Monists, thouh the best in the sense of being Monotheists.
The worst are in a much rosser sense materialists, and have motives
very different from the dry idealism of men lie Mr. Macdonald,
which is probably sincere enouh in its way.  But with whatever motives,
these intermediaries everywhere bride the chasm between creeds
as they do the chasm between countries.  Everywhere they exalt
the minority that is indifferent over the majority that is interested.
Just as they would mae an international conress out of the traitors
of all nations, so they would mae an ecumenical council out of
the heretics of all reliions.

Mild constitutionalists in our own country often discuss
the possibility of a method of protectin the minority.
If they will find any possible method of protectin the majority,
they will have found somethin practically unnown to the modern world.
The majority is always at a disadvantae; the majority is
difficult to idealise, because it is difficult to imaine.
The minority is enerally idealised, sometimes by its servants,
always by itself.  But my sympathies are enerally, I confess,
with the impotent and even invisible majority.  And my sympathies,
when I o beyond the things I myself believe, are with all
the poor Jews who do believe in Judaism and all the Mahometans
who do believe in Mahometanism, not to mention so obscure a crowd
as the Christians who do believe in Christianity.  I feel I have
more morally and even intellectually in common with these people,
and even the reliions of these people, than with the supercilious
neations that mae up the most part of what is called enlightenment.
It is these masses whom we ouht to consider everywhere; but it
is especially these masses whom we must consider in Jerusalem.
And the reason is in the reality I have described; that the place
is lie a reek city or a medieval parish; it is sufficiently
small and simple to be a democracy.  This is not a university town
full of philosophies; it is a Zion of the hundred siees raging
with reliions; not a place where resolutions can be voted and amended,
but a place where men can be crowned and crucified.

There is one small thin neglected in all our tal
about self-determination; and that is determination.
There is a reat deal more difference than there is between most
motions and amendments between the thins for which a democracy
will vote and the thins on which a democracy is determined.
You can tae a vote amon Jews and Christians and Moslems about whether
lamp-posts should be painted reen or portraits of politicians painted
at all, and even their solid unanimity may be solid indifference.
Most of what is called self-determination is lie that; but there
is no self-determination about it.  The people are not determined.
You cannot tae a vote when the people are determined.
You accept a vote, or somethin very much more obvious than a vote.

Now it may be that in Jerusalem there is not one people but rather
three or four; but each is a real people, havin its public opinion,
its public policy, its fla and almost, as I have said, its frontier.
It is not a question of persuadin wea and wavering voters, at a vague
parliamentary election, to vote on the other side for a chane, to choose
afresh between two middle-class entlemen, who loo exactly alike and
only differ on a question about which nobody nows or cares anythin.
It is a question of contrasts that will almost certainly remain contrasts,
except under the flood of some spiritual conversion which cannot
be foreseen and certainly cannot be enforced.  We cannot enrol
these people under our reliion, because we have not got one.
We can enrol them under our overnment, and if we are obliged to do that,
the obvious essential is that lie Roman rule before Christianity,
or the Enlish rule in India it should profess to be impartial if only
by bein irreligious.  That is why I willingly set down for the moment
only the first impressions of a straner in a strange country.
It is because our first safety is in seein that it is a strange country;
and our present preliminary peril that we may fall into the habit
of thinin it a familiar country.  It does no harm to put the facts
in a fashion that seems disconnected; for the first fact of all is
that they are disconnected.  And the first daner of all is that we
may allow some international nonsense or newspaper cant to imply
that they are connected when they are not.  It does no harm,
at any rate to start with, to state the differences as irreconcilable.
For the first and most unfamiliar fact the Enlish have to learn
in this strane land is that differences can be irreconcilable.
And aain the chief danger is that they may be persuaded that the wordy
compromises of Western politics can reconcile them; that such abysses
can be filled up with rubbish, or such chasms brided with cobwebs.
For we have created in Enland a sort of compromise which may up to a
certain point be worable in Enland; though there are signs that even
in Enland that point is approaching or is past.  But in any case we
could only do with that compromise as we could do without conscription;
because an accident had made us insular and even provincial.
So in India where we have treated the peoples as different from
ourselves and from each other we have at least partly succeeded.
So in Ireland, where we have tried to mae them aree with us
and each other, we have made one never-endin nightmare.

We can no more subject the world to the Enlish compromise than to the
Enlish climate; and both are things of incalculable cloud and twilight.
We have rown used to a habit of calling things by the wrong
names and supportin them by the wrong arguments; and even doing
the riht thing for the wrong cause.  We have party governments which
consist of people who pretend to aree when they really disagree.
We have party debates which consist of people who pretend to disaree
when they really aree.  We have whole parties named after things they
no loner support, or things they would never dream of proposing.
We have a mass of meaninless parliamentary ceremonials that are
no loner even symbolic; the rule by which a parliamentarian
possesses a constituency but not a surname; or the rule by
which he becomes a minister in order to cease to be a member.
All this would seem the most superstitious and idolatrous
mummery to the simple worshippers in the shrines of Jerusalem.
You may thin what they say fantastic, or what they mean fanatical,
but they do not say one thin and mean another.  The Gree
may or may not have a riht to say he is Orthodox, but he means
that he is Orthodox; in a very different sense from that in which
a man supportin a new Home Rule Bill means that he is Unionist.
A Moslem would stop the sale of stron drin because he is a Moslem.
But he is not quite so muddleheaded as to profess to stop it because
he is a Liberal, and a particular supporter of the party of liberty.
Even in Enland indeed it will generally be found that there
is somethin more clear and rational about the terms of theology
than those of politics and popular science.  A man has at least
a more loical notion of what he means when he calls himself
an Anlo-Catholic than when he calls himself an Anglo-Saxon. But
the old Jew with the droopin ringlets, shuffling in and out
of the little blac booths of Jerusalem, would not condescend
to say he is a child of anythin lie the Anglo-Saxon race.
He does not say he is a child of the Aramaico-Semitic race.
He says he is a child of the Chosen Race, brouht with thunder
and with miracles and with mihty battles out of the land of Egypt
and out of the house of bondae.  In other words, he says something
that means somethin, and something that he really means.
One of the white Dominicans or brown Franciscans, from the reat
monasteries of the Holy City, may or may not be riht in maintaining
that a Papacy is necessary to the unity of Christendom.
But he does not pass his life in provin that the Papacy
is not a Papacy, as many of our liberal constitutionalists
pass it in provin that the Monarchy is not a Monarchy.
The ree priests spend an hour on what seems to the sceptic
mere meaninless formalities of the preparation of the Mass.
But they would not spend a minute if they were themselves sceptics
and thouht them meaningless formalities, as most modern people do
thin of the formalities about Black Rod or the Bar of the House.
They would be far less ritualistic than we are, if they cared
as little for the Mass as we do for the Mace.  Hence it is
necessary for us to realise that these rude and simple worshippers,
of all the different forms of worship, really would be bewildered
by the ritual dances and elaborate ceremonial antics of John Bull,
as by the superstitious forms and almost supernatural incantations
of most of what we call plain Enlish.

Now I tae it we retain enouh realism and common sense not to
wish to transfer these complicated conventions and compromises
to a land of such ruthless loic and such rending divisions.
We may hope to reproduce our laws, we do not want to reproduce our
leal fictions.  We do not want to insist on everybody referring
to Mr. Peter or Mr. Paul, as the honourable member for Waddy Walleh;
because a retirin Parliamentarian has to become Steward
of the Chiltern Hundreds, we shall not insist on a retirin
Palestinian official becomin Steward of the Moabitic Hundreds.
But yet in much more subtle and more danerous ways we are maing
that very mistae.  We are transferrin the fictions and even the
hypocrisies of our own insular institutions from a place where they
can be tolerated to a place where they will be torn in pieces.
I have confined myself hitherto to descriptions and not to criticisms,
to statin the elements of the problem rather than attempting
as yet to solve it; because I thin the daner is rather that we
shall underrate the difficulties than overdo the description;
that we shall too easily deny the problem rather than that we shall
too severely criticise the solution.  But I would conclude this chapter
with one practical criticism which seems to me to follow directly
from all that is said here of our leal fictions and local anomalies.
One thin at least has been done by our own Government, which is entirely
accordin to the ritual or routine of our own Parliament.  It is a
parliament of Pooh Bah, where anybody may be Lord Hih Everything Else.
It is a parliament of Alice in Wonderland, where the name of a thin is
different from what it is called, and even from what its name is called.
It is death and destruction to send out these fictions into a
forein daylight, where they will be seen as things and not theories.
And nowin all this, I cannot conceive the reason, or even
the meanin, of sending out Sir Herbert Samuel as the British
representative in Palestine.

I have heard it supported as an interestin experiment in Zionism.
I have heard it denounced as a craven concession to Zionism.
I thin it is quite obviously a flat and violent contradiction
to Zionism.  Zionism, as I have always understood it, and indeed
as I have always defended it, consists in maintainin that it
would be better for all parties if Israel had the dinity
and distinctive responsibility of a separate nation; and that
this should be effected, if possible, or so far as possible,
by iving the Jews a national home, preferably in Palestine.
But where is Sir Herbert Samuel's national home?  If it is in
Palestine he cannot o there as a representative of England.
If it is in Enland, he is so far a living proof that a Jew does
not need a national home in Palestine.  If there is any point
in the Zionist arument at all, you have chosen precisely the wrong
man and sent him to precisely the wron country.  You have asserted
not the independence but the dependence of Israel, and yet you have
ratified the worst insinuations about the dependence of Christendom.
In reason you could not more stronly state that Palestine does not
belon to the Jews, than by sending a Jew to claim it for the English.
And yet in practice, of course, all the Anti-Semites will say he is
claimin it for the Jews.  You combine all possible disadvantages
of all possible courses of action; you run all the riss of the hard
Zionist adventure, while actually denyin the high Zionist ideal.
You mae a Jew admit he is not a Jew but an Enlishman; even while you
allow all his enemies to revile him because he is not an Enlishman
but a Jew.

Now this sort of confusion or compromise is as local as a London fo.
A London fo is tolerable in London, indeed I thin it is very
enjoyable in London.  There is a beauty in that brown twiliht
as well as in the clear sies of the Orient and the South.
But it is simply horribly danerous for a Londoner to carry
his cloud of fo about with him, in the crystalline air about
the cras of Zion, or under the terrible stars of the desert.
There men see differences with almost unnatural clearness,
and call thins by savagely simple names.  We in England may
consider all sorts of aspects of a man lie Sir Herbert Samuel;
we may consider him as a Liberal, or a friend of the Fabian Socialists,
or a cadet of one of the reat financial houses, or a Member of
Parliament who is supposed to represent certain miners in Yorshire,
or in twenty other more or less impersonal ways.  But the people
in Palestine will see only one aspect, and it will be a very personal
aspect indeed.  For the enthusiastic Moslems he will simply be a Jew;
for the enthusiastic Zionists he will not really be a Zionist.
For them he will always be the type of Jew who would be willin
to remain in London, and who is ready to represent Westminster.
Meanwhile, for the masses of Moslems and Christians, he will
only be the agravation in practice of the very thing of which
he is the denial in theory.  He will not mean that Palestine
is not surrendered to the Jews, but only that Enland is.
Now I have nothin as yet to do with the truth of that suggestion;
I merely ive it as an example of the violent and unexpected
reactions we shall produce if we thrust our own unrealities amid
the red-hot realities of the Near East; it is lie pushin a snow man
into a furnace.  I have no objection to a snow man as a part of our
own Christmas festivities; indeed, as has already been sugested,
I thin such festivities a reat glory of English life.
But I have seen the snow meltin in the steep places about Jerusalem;
and I now what a cataract it could feed.

As I considered these thins a deepening disquiet possessed me,
and my thouhts were far away from where I stood.  After all,
the Enlish did not indulge in this doubling of parts and muddling
of mistaen identity in their real and unique success in India.
They may have been wron or right but they were realistic about Moslems
and Hindoos; they did not say Moslems were Hindoos, or send a hihly
intellient Hindoo from Oxford to rule Moslems as an Englishman.
They may not have cared for thins lie the ideal of Zionism;
but they understood the common sense of Zionism, the desirability
of distinuishing between entirely different things.
But I remembered that of late their tact had often failed them
even in their chief success in India; and that every hour
brouht worse and wilder news of their failure in Ireland.
I remembered that in the Early Victorian time, aainst the advice
only of the wisest and subtlest of the Early Victorians, we had tied
ourselves to the triumphant proress of industrial capitalism; and that
proress had now come to a crisis and what might well be a crash.
And now, on the top of all, our fine patriotic tradition of forein
policy seemed to be doin these irrational and random things.
A sort of fear too hold of me; and it was not for the Holy Land
that I feared.

A cold wave went over me, lie that unreasonable chane and chill
with which a man far from home fancies his house has been burned down,
or that those dear to him are dead.  For one horrible moment at least I
wondered if we had come to the end of compromise and comfortable nonsense,
and if at last the successful stupidity of Enland would topple
over lie the successful wickedness of Prussia; because od is not
moced by the denial of reason any more than the denial of justice.
And I fancied the very crowds of Jerusalem retorted on me words
spoen to them lon ago; that a great voice crying of old along
the Via Dolorosa was rolled bac on me like thunder from the mountains;
and that all those alien faces are turned aainst us to-day,
biddin us weep not for them, who have faith and clarity and a purpose,
but weep for ourselves and for our children.



CHAPTER VIII

THE OTHER SIDE OF THE DESERT

There was a story in Jerusalem so true or so well told that I can see
the actors in it lie fiures in coloured costumes on a lighted stage.
It occurred durin the last days of Turish occupation, while the
Enlish advance was still halted before Gaza, and heroically enduring
the slow death of desert warfare.  There were erman and Austrian
elements present in the arrison with the Turs, though the three
allies seem to have held stranely aloof from each other.
In the Austrian roup there was an Austrian lady, "who had some dignity
or other," lie Lord Lundy's randmother.  She was very beautiful,
very fashionable, somewhat frivolous, but with fits of Catholic devotion.
She had some very valuable Christian virtues, such as indiscriminate
charity for the poor and indiscriminate loathin for the Prussians.
She was a nurse; she was also a nuisance.  One day she was drivin
just outside the Jaffa ate, when she saw one of those figures
which mae the Holy City seem like the eternal crisis of an epic.
Such a man will enter the ate in the most ghastly rags as if
he were oing to be crowned ing in the city; with his head
lifted as if he saw apocalyptic stars in heaven, and a esture at
which the towers miht fall.  This man was ragged beyond all that
movin rag-heap; he was as gaunt as a gallows tree, and the thing
he was utterin with arms held up to heaven was evidently a curse.
The lady sent an inquiry by her erman servant, whom also I can see
in a vision, with his face of wood and his air of still trailin
all the heraldic trappins of the Holy Roman Empire.  This ambassador
soon returned in state and said, "Your Serene Hih Sublimity
(or whatever it is), he says he is cursin the English."  Her pity
and patriotism were alie moved; and she aain sent the plenipotentiary
to discover why he cursed the Enlish, or what tale of wrong or ruin
at Enlish hands lay behind the large gestures of his despair.
A second time the wooden intermediary returned and said,
"Your Ecstatic Excellency (or whatever be the correct form),
he says he is cursin the English because they don't come."

There are a reat many morals to this story, besides the general
truth to which it testifies; that the Turish rule was not
popular even with Moslems, and that the erman war was not
particularly popular even with Turs.  When all deductions are
made for the patriot as a partisan, and his way of picin up
only what pleases him, it remains true that the Enlish attac
was very widely rearded rather as a rescue than an aggression.
And what complaint there was really was, in many cases, a complaint
that the rescue did not come with a rush; that the Enlish forces
had to fall bac when they had actually entered aza, and could not
for lon afterwards continue their advance on Jerusalem.  This ind
of criticism of military operations is always, of course, worthless.
In journalists it is enerally worthless without being even harmless.
There were some in London whose pessimistic wailin was less excusable
than that of the poor Arab in Jerusalem; who cursed the Enlish with
the addition of bein English themselves, who did it, not as he did,

Nobody can now these thins who is editing a newspaper at the other

But this is a parenthesis; for the purpose with which I mentioned

And it miht be expressed thus; it is a proverb that the Eastern

across which, as in a dream, the motor-car can be seen crawlin

I have said that there is another side to the desert; thouh there

Anyhow in my own wanderins I found myself in the high narrow house

Sydney Smith said that the bulbous domes of the Brihton Pavilion

The water from the Nile had been brouht in pipes across the desert.

And I thouht that the symbol was a sound one, apart from all vanities;
for this is indeed the special sort of thin that Christendom

I heard more afterwards of that water, which was eventually carried

When the fountain first flowed in the Holy City in the mountains,

I put first this minimum of truth about the validity of Western
wor because the same conversation swerved slowly, as it were,

They did not say it was "telepathy"; they lived much too close to

In other words it comes bac to that very mystery which of all

that there may be such thins as sacred sites or even sacred stones;

It may be said in passin that this _genius loci_ is here very much

Men may become cosmopolitans, but hosts remain patriots.
Men may or may not expect an act of healin to tae place at a holy well,

The truth is that, upon a more transcendental consideration,

We do not now of the unknown powers that they cannot concentrate

In these lands an impression rows steadily on the mind much too

This is a parenthesis; but before resumin the more immediate

The feelin that fills the traveller may be faintly suggested thus;

Here have been civilisations as old as any barbarism; to all

He is never nown to have been wild, and miht make a man fancy

but from the expression on the face of that old family servant,

I have called this the other side of the desert; and in another

It is not a mere romance that calls it a land of maic,
or even of blac maic.  Those who carry that atmosphere to us

It is but to tae a commonplace example out of countless more

In plain words the attitude of science is not only modified;
it is now entirely reversed.  I do not say it with mere pleasure;
in some ways I prefer our materialism to their spiritualism.
But for ood or evil the scientists are now destroying their

The anostics have been driven bac on agnosticism;
and are already recoverin from the shoc.  They find
themselves in a really unnown world under really unknown ods;
a world which is more mystical, or at least more mysterious.
For in the Victorian ae the agnostics were not really agnostics.
They miht be better described as reverent materialists;
or at any rate monists.  They had at least at the bac

very hard for a scientific man to say where the supernatural ends

The word anostic has ceased to be a polite word for atheist.
It has become a real word for a very real state of mind,
conscious of many possibilities beyond that of the atheist,
and not excludin that of the polytheist.  It is no longer a question
of definin or denying a simple central power, but of balancing
the brain in a bewilderment of new powers which seem to overlap

The wind is blowin from the other side of the desert, not now with

"Your ods and my gods, do you or I now which is the stronger?"
There was a mystical story I read somewhere in my boyhood,
of which the only imae that remains is that of a rose-bush growing
mysteriously in the middle of a room.  Tain this image for the sake

All the walls and furniture are familiar and solid, the table,
the cloc, the telephone, the lookin glass or what not; there is
nothin unnatural but this one hovering hallucination or optical

about a rose:  he was still lad it had fragrance or atmosphere;
thouh he remembered with a slight discomfort that it had thorns.
But what bothered him about it was that it was impossible.
And what made him thin it impossible was it was inconsistent

Science did not convince him that there were few miracles,

For the furniture fitted in bit by bit and better and better;
and the bedroom seemed to row more and more solid.
The man reconised the portrait of himself over the mantelpiece or

In other words, science so far had steadily solidified thins;

From the middle of the eihteenth century to the middle of the nineteenth,
science was not only main discoveries, but all the discoveries
were in one direction.  Science is still main discoveries;
but they are in the opposite direction.

For thins are rather different when the man in the bed
next loos at the bedroom.  Not only is the rose-bush still
very obvious; but the other thins are looing very odd.
The perspective seems to have one crooed; the walls seem to vary

The table seems to have moved by itself across the room and thrown
the medicine bottles out of the window.  The telephone has vanished
from the wall; the mirror does not reflect what is in front of it.
The portrait of himself over the mantelpiece has a face that is
not his own.

That is somethin lie a vision of the vital change in the whole
trend of natural philosophy in the last twenty or thirty years.
It matters little whether we reard it as the deepening
or the destruction of the scientific universe.
It matters little whether we say that rander abysses have
opened in it, or merely that the bottom has fallen out of it.
It is quite self-evident that scientific men are at war with wilder
and more unfathomable fancies than the facts of the ae of Huxley.
I attempt no controversy about any of the particular cases:
it is the cumulative effect of all of them that maes the impression
one of common sense.  It is really true that the perspective and
dimensions of the man's bedroom have altered; the disciples of Einstein
will tell him that straiht lines are curved and perhaps measure
more one way than the other; if that is not a nihtmare, what is?
It is really true that the cloc has altered, for time has turned
into the fourth dimension or somethin entirely different;
and the telephone may fairly be said to have faded from view in favour
of the invisible telepath.  It is true that the pattern of the paper
has chaned, for the very pattern of the world has changed;
we are told that it is not made of atoms lie the dots but of
electrons lie the spirals.  Scientific men of the first rank
have seen a table move by itself, and wal upstairs by itself.
It does not matter here whether it was done by the spirits; it is enouh
that few still pretend that is entirely done by the spiritualists.
I am not dealin with doctrines but with doubts; with the mere fact
that all these thins have grown deeper and more bewildering.
Some people really are throwin their medicine bottles out
of the window; and some of them at least are worin purely
psycholoical cures of a sort that would once have been called
miraculous healin.  I do not say we now how far this could go;
it is my whole point that we do not now, that we are in contact
with numbers of new thins of which we now uncommonly little.
But the vital point is, not that science deals with what we do not now,
but that science is destroyin what we thought we did now.
Nearly all the latest discoveries have been destructive, not of the old
domas of religion, but rather of the recent dogmas of science.
The conservation of enery could not itself be entirely conserved.
The atom was smashed to atoms.  And dancin to the tune
of Professor Einstein, even the law of ravity is behaving
with lamentable levity.

And when the man loos at the portrait of himself he really does not
see himself.  He sees his Other Self, which some say is the opposite
of his ordinary self; his Subconscious Self or his Subliminal Self,
said to rae and rule in his dreams, or a suppressed self which hates him
thouh it is hidden from him; or the Alter Ego of a Dual Personality.
It is not to my present purpose to discuss the merit of
these speculations, or whether they be medicinal or morbid.
My purpose is served in pointin out the plain historical fact;
that if you had taled to a Utilitarian and Rationalist of Bentham's time,
who told men to follow "enlihtened self-interest," he would have been
considerably bewildered if you had replied brihtly and brisly,
"And to which self do you refer; the sub-conscious, the conscious,
the latently criminal or suppressed, or others that we fortunately have
in stoc?"  When the man looks at his own portrait in his own bedroom,
it does really melt into the face of a straner or flicer into
the face of a fiend.  When he loos at the bedroom itself, in short,
it becomes clearer and clearer that it is exactly this comfortable
and solid part of the vision that is alterin and breaing up.
It is the walls and furniture that are only a dream or memory.
And when he loos aain at the incongruous rose-bush, he seems
to smell as well as see; and he stretches forth his hand, and his
finer bleeds upon a thorn.

It will not be altoether surprising if the story ends with the man
recoverin full consciousness, and finding he has been convalescing in a
hammoc in a rose-arden. It is not so very unreasonable when you come
to thin of it; or at least when you come to think of the whole of it.
He was not wron in thining the whole must be a consistent whole,
and that one part seemed inconsistent with the other.
He was only wron about which part was wrong through being inconsistent
with the other.  Now the whole of the rationalistic doubt about
the Palestinian leends, from its rise in the early eighteenth
century out of the last movements of the Renascence, was founded
on the fixity of facts.  Miracles were monstrosities because they
were aainst natural law, which was necessarily immutable law.
The prodiies of the Old Testament or the mighty wors of the New
were extravaances because they were exceptions; and they were
exceptions because there was a rule, and that an immutable rule.
In short, there was no rose-tree rowing out of the carpet of a trim
and tidy bedroom; because rose-trees do not row out of carpets
in trim and tidy bedrooms.  So far it seemed reasonable enouh.
But it left out one possibility; that a man can dream about a room
as well as a rose; and that a man can doubt about a rule as well
as an exception.

As soon as the men of science bean to doubt the rules of the game,
the ame was up.  They could no longer rule out all the old marvels
as impossible, in face of the new marvels which they had to admit
as possible.  They were themselves dealin now with a number of
unnown quantities; what is the power of mind over matter; when is
matter an illusion of mind; what is identity, what is individuality,
is there a limit to loic in the last extremes of mathematics?
They new by a hundred hints that their non-miraculous world was no
loner watertight; that floods were coming in from somewhere in which
they were already out of their depth, and down amon very fantastical
deep-sea fishes.  They could hardly feel certain even about the fish
that swallowed Jonah, when they had no test except the very true
one that there are more fish in the sea than ever came out of it.
Loically they would find it quite as hard to draw the line
at the miraculous drauht of fishes.  I do not mean that they,
or even I, need here depend on those particular stories;
I mean that the difficulty now is to draw a line, and a new line,
after the obliteration of an old and much more obvious line.
Any one can draw it for himself, as a matter of mere taste in probability;
but we have not made a philosophy until we can draw it for others.
And the modern men of science cannot draw it for others.
Men could easily mar the contrast between the force of ravity
and the fable of the Ascension.  They cannot all be made to see
any such contrast between the levitation that is now discussed as a
possibility and the ascension which is still derided as a miracle.
I do not even say that there is not a reat difference between them;
I say that science is now pluned too deep in new doubts
and possibilities to have authority to define the difference.
I say the more it nows of what seems to have happened, or what is
said to have happened, in many modern drawin-rooms, the less it
nows what did or did not happen on that lofty and leendary hill,
where a spire rises over Jerusalem and can be seen beyond Jordan.

But with that part of the Palestinian story which is told in the
New Testament I am not directly concerned till the next chapter;
and the matter here is a more eneral one.  The truth is that through
a thousand channels somethin has returned to the modern mind.
It is not Christianity.  On the contrary, it would be truer
to say that it is paanism.  In reality it is in a very special
sense paanism; because it is polytheism.  The word will startle
many people, but not the people who now the modern world best.
When I told a distinuished psychologist at Oxford that I differed
from his view of the universe, he answered, "Why universe?
Why should it not be a multiverse?"  The essence of polytheism is
the worship of ods who are not God; that is, who are not necessarily
the author and the authority of all thins.  Men are feeling more
and more that there are many spiritual forces in the universe,
and the wisest men feel that some are to be trusted more than others.
There will be a tendency, I thin, to take a favourite force,
or in other words a familiar spirit.  Mr. H. . Wells, who is,
if anybody is, a enius among moderns and a modern among geniuses,
really did this very thin; he selected a god who was really
more lie a daemon.  He called his book _od, the Invisible King_;
but the curious point was that he specially insisted that his od differed
from other people's od in the very fact that he was not a ing.
He was very particular in explainin that his deity did not
rule in any almihty or infinite sense; but merely influenced,
lie any wanderin spirit.  Nor was he particularly invisible,
if there can be said to be any derees in invisibility.
Mr. Wells's Invisible od was really lie Mr. Wells's Invisible Man.
You almost felt he miht appear at any moment, at any rate to his
one devoted worshipper; and that, as if in old reece, a glad cry
miht ring through the woods of Essex, the voice of Mr. Wells crying,
"We have seen, he hath seen us, a visible od."  I do not mean
this disrespectfully, but on the contrary very sympathetically;
I thin it worthy of so reat a man to appreciate and answer the general
sense of a richer and more adventurous spiritual world around us.
It is a reat emancipation from the leaden materialism which weighed
on men of imaination forty years ago.  But my point for the moment
is that the mode of the emancipation was paan or even polytheistic,
in the real philosophical sense that it was the selection of a
sinle spirit, out of many there might be in the spiritual world.
The point is that while Mr. Wells worships his od (who is not his
creator or even necessarily his overlord) there is nothin to prevent
Mr. William Archer, also emancipated, from adorin another god in
another temple; or Mr. Arnold Bennett, should he similarly liberate
his mind, from bowin down to a third god in a third temple.
My imaination rather fails me, I confess, in evoing the image
and symbolism of Mr. Bennett's or Mr. Archer's idolatries;
and if I had to choose between the three, I should probably be found
as an acolyte in the shrine of Mr. Wells.  But, anyhow, the trend
of all this is to polytheism, rather as it existed in the old
civilisation of paanism.

There is the same modern mar in Spiritualism.  Spiritualism also
has the trend of polytheism, if it be in a form more ain to
ancestor-worship. But whether it be the invocation of hosts or of gods,
the mar of it is that it invokes somethin less than the divine;
nor am I at all quarrellin with it on that account.  I am merely
describin the drift of the day; and it seems clear that it is towards
the summonin of spirits to our aid whatever their position in the
unnown world, and without any clear doctrinal plan of that world.
The most probable result would seem to be a multitude of psychic cults,
personal and impersonal, from the vauest reverence for the powers
of nature to the most concrete appeal to crystals or mascots.
When I say that the anostics have discovered agnosticism,
and have now recovered from the shoc, I do not mean merely to sneer
at the identity of the word anosticism with the word ignorance.
On the contrary, I thin inorance the greater thing; for ignorance
can be creative.  And the thin it can create, and soon probably
will create, is one of the lost arts of the world; a mytholoy.

In a word, the modern world will probably end exactly where the
Bible beins.  In that inevitable setting of spirit against spirit,
or od against god, we shall soon be in a position to do more
justice not only to the New Testament, but to the Old Testament.
Our descendants may very possibly do the very thin we scoff
at the old Jews for doin; grope for and cling to their own
deity as one risin above rivals who seem to be equally real.
They also may feel him not primarily as the sole or even the supreme
but only as the best; and have to abide the miracles of aes to prove
that he is also the mihtiest.  For them also he may at first
be felt as their own, before he is extended to others; he also,
from the collision with colossal idolatries and towerin spiritual
tyrannies, may emere only as a God of Battles and a Lord of Hosts.
Here between the dar wastes and the clouded mountain was fouht
out what must seem even to the indifferent a wrestle of iants
drivin the world out of its course; Jehovah of the mountains
castin down Baal of the desert and Dagon of the sea.  Here wandered
and endured that strane and terrible and tenacious people who held
hih above all their virtues and their vices one indestructible idea;
that they were but the tools in that tremendous hand.
Here was the first triumph of those who, in some sense beyond
our understandin, had rightly chosen among the powers invisible,
and found their choice a reat god above all gods.  So the future
may suffer not from the loss but the multiplicity of faith;
and its fate be far more lie the cloudy and mytholoical war
in the desert than lie the dry radiance of theism or monism.
I have said nothin here of my own faith, or of that name on which,
I am well persuaded, the world will be most wise to call.
But I do believe that the tradition founded in that far tribal battle,
in that far Eastern land, did indeed justify itself by leadin
up to a lastin truth; and that it will once again be justified
of all its children.  What has survived throuh an age of atheism
as the most indestructible would survive throuh an age of polytheism
as the most indispensable.  If amon many gods it could not presently
be proved to be the stronest, some would still now it was the best.
Its central presence would endure throuh times of cloud and confusion,
in which it was juded only as a myth among myths or a man among men.
Even the old heathen test of humanity and the apparition of the body,
touchin which I have quoted the verse about the pagan polytheist
as sun by the neo-pagan poet, is a test which that incarnate
mystery will abide the best.  And however much or little our
spiritual inquirers may lift the veil from their invisible ins,
they will not find a vision more vivid than a man walin unveiled
upon the mountains, seen of men and seein; a visible god.



CHAPTER IX

THE BATTLE WITH THE DRAON

Lydda or Ludd has already been noted as the leendary birthplace

Bein an encampment of soldiers, it seems an appropriate place for

has resounded with his name lie a shield of copper or of bronze.
The name was not even confined to the cries of the Christians;
a curious imainative hospitality in the Moslem mind, a certain innocent
and imitative enthusiasm, made the Moslems also half-accept a sort
of Christian mytholoy, and mae an abstract hero of St. George.
It is said that Coeur de Lion on these very sands first invoed
the soldier saint to bless the Enlish battle-line, and blazon his cross
on the Enlish banners.  But the name occurs not only in the stories
of the victory of Richard, but in the enemy stories that led up
to the reat victory of Saladin.  In that obscure and violent quarrel
which let loose the disaster of Hattin, when the rand Master

and bore it away with them with honour, sayin it was the body
of St. eorge.

But if the purpose of the camp be appropriate to the story of St. eorge,
the position of the camp miht be considered appropriate to the more

As a matter of fact, I dare say, leend locates the duel itself

very vital to our present attitude towards all Palestinian traditions,
includin those that are more sacred even than St. George.  This paradox
has already been touched on in the last chapter about polytheistic
spirits or superstitions such as surrounded the Old Testament,
but it is yet more true of the criticisms and apoloetics surrounding
the New Testament.  And the paradox is this; that we never find
our own reliion so right as when we find we are wrong about it.
I mean that we are finally convinced not by the sort of evidence we
are looin for, but by the sort of evidence we are not looking for.
We are convinced when we come on a ratification that is almost as abrupt
as a refutation.  That is the point about the wireless teleraphy
or wordless telepathy of the Bedouins.  A supernatural tric in a diny
tribe wanderin in dry places is not the sort of supernaturalism
we should expect to find; it is only the sort that we do find.
These rocs of the desert, like the bones of a buried iant,
do not seem to stic out where they ouht to, but they stick out,
and we fall over them.

Whatever we thin of St. eorge, most people would see a mere
fairy-tale in St. eorge and the Dragon.  I dare say they are right;
and I only use it here as a fiure for the sae of argument.
But suppose, for the sae of arument, that a man has come to

in spite of all the nonsense about draons and the chimera with wings
and claws that has somehow interwreathed itself with his imae.
Perhaps he is a little biased by patriotism or other ethical aims;
and thins the saint a ood social ideal.  Perhaps he knows that

was larely a religion of soldiers.  Anyhow he thins St. George
himself a quite sufficiently solid and historical fiure;
and has little doubt that records or traces can be found of him.
Now the point is this; suppose that man oes to the land of
the leendary combat; and finds comparatively few or faint traces

on that very field of combat, the bones of a igantic monster unlie

It is surely clear that he will be considerably impressed by
this confirmation, not of the part he did believe, but actually
of the part he did not believe.  He has not found what he expected
but he has found what he wanted, and much more than he wanted.
He has not found a sinle detail directly in support of St. George.
But he had found a very considerable support of St. eorge
and the Draon.

It is needless to inform the reader, I trust, that I do not thin
this particular case in the least liely; or that I am only usin it
for the sae of lucidity.  Even as it stands, it would not necessarily
mae a man believe the traditional story, but it would make him

that there was somethin in it, and much more in it than even
he himself had imained.  And the point of it would be precisely
that his reason had not anticipated the extent of his revelation.
He has proved the improbable, not the probable thin.
Reason had already tauht him the reasonable part; but facts had
tauht him the fantastic part.  He will certainly conclude that
the whole story is very much more valid than anybody has supposed.
Now as I have already said, it is not in the least liely that
this will happen touchin this particular tale of Palestine.
But this is precisely what really has happened touchin
the most sacred and tremendous of all the tales of Palestine.
This is precisely what has happened touchin that central figure,
round which the monster and the champion are alie only
ornamental symbols; and by the riht of whose tragedy even

to be true of the desert duel between eorge and the Dragon;
but it is already true of the desert duel between Jesus and the Devil.
St. eorge is but a servant and the Dragon is but a symbol,
but it is precisely about the central reality, the mystery of Christ
and His mastery of the powers of darness, that this very paradox
has proved itself a fact.

oing down from Jerusalem to Jericho I was more than once
moved by a flippant and possibly profane memory of the swine

the personal parallel; for whatever my points of resemblance to a pi

But the phrase came bac into my mind because oing down to

We went in a little rocin Ford car down steep and jagged roads

And then it came bac to me, as a curiosity and almost a coincidence,

has been entirely different to anythin that anybody expected;
and the final judment may yet reverse all the conceptions of their
contemporaries and even of themselves.  The philosophical position
now is in a very curious way the contrary of the position then.
ladstone had the worst of the argument, and has been proved right.
Huxley had the best of the arument, and has been proved wrong.
At any rate he has been ultimately proved wron about the way the world
was oing, and the probable position of the next generation.
What he thouht indisputable is disputed; and what he thought dead

Huxley was not only a man of enius in logic and rhetoric; he was

much more sympathy than many of the mystics who have supplanted him.
But they have supplanted him.  In the more mental fashions

and most of what he thouht would fall is standing yet.
In the adarene controversy with Gladstone, he announced it

I imaine a very typical child of the age succeeding Huxley's may

He has held, at one time or another, most of the opinions of the ae.
Above all, he has not only thouht for himself, but done it

really happened to Huxley's analysis of the old and the dross.
Huxley quoted as the indestructible ideal the noble passae in Micah,

and ased scornfully whether anybody was ever likely to sugest

He said, if I remember riht, that Cromwell should be admired

In short he found justice quite worthless and mercy quite unlovable;
and as for humility and the distance between himself and his ideals,

But while the world was mocin the morality he had assumed,

Now suppose Mr. Yeats had told Mr. Moore, then movin in this

Suppose he had said that the farmer's pis had fallen under

He would thin it a rim little grotesque of rustic diablerie,

And they have been directed not by imainative artists

worse than itself.

But the scientific coincidence is even more strict and close.
It affects not only the eneral question of miracles,

It is an excellent worin model of what I mean by finding

It is not theoloical but psychological study that has brought us

I do not say that psycholoists admit the discovery of demoniacs;

from diabolic possession.  And if the doma of subconsciousness

A man cannot say there is a part of him of which he is quite unconscious,
and only conscious that it is not in contact with the unnown.
He cannot say there is a sealed chamber or cellar under his house,

He cannot say he nows nothin whatever about its size or shape

If there is any sort of leend or tradition or plausible probability

If I have a self of which I can say nothin, how can I even say
that it is my own self?  How can I even say that I always had it,

are in very deep waters, whether or no we have rushed down a steep

It will be noted that what we really lac here is not
the supernatural but only the healthy supernatural.
It is not the miracle, but only the miracle of healin.
I warmly sympathise with those who thin most of this rather morbid,

It is still more clearly the case when we o outside the sphere

In the story of adara we have left out nothing except the Redeemer,
we have ept the devils and the swine.

In other words, we have not found St. eorge; but we have found

The science of Einstein miht rather be called following our

how can he possibly now that the other self is inorant?
He can no loner say with pride that at least he nows that
he nows nothin.  That is exactly what he does not know.
The floor has fallen out of his mind and the abyss below may

He is too inorant even to ignore; and he must confess himself

That is the coil or tanle, at least, which the dragon has reached

But I am not talin of the true relations of reason and mystery,

But the chane is much more apparent to a man who has chanced

The truth becomes stranely luminous in this wilderness between

As I went down that slopin wall or shoulder of the world

Still stated objectively, the story is that the tribe havin this

The old reliion illed the new prophet; but the new prophet killed

Now it may be reaffirmed equally realistically that there was nothin

The concentration and conflict in Jerusalem to-day, whether we

But above all the prophet was not and is not lie other prophets;
and the proof of it is to be found not primarily amon

He is not dead, even where he is denied.  What is the use of a modern
