It is improbable that Tolstoy wrote a boo to prove that all modern

We do not find wild Bolshevists callin themselves Mithraic Socialists

and even sanity entirely on one side, the very heresies and insanities

Let the critics try to conjure with any of the other names.
In the real centres of modern inquiry and mental activity,
they will not move even a mystic with the name of Mithras
as they will move a materialist with the name of Jesus.
There are men who deny od and accept Christ.

But this linering yet living power in the legend, even for
those to whom it is little more than a leend, has another
relevancy to the particular point here.  Jesus of Nazareth,
merely humanly considered, has thus become a hero of humanitarianism.
Even the eihteenth-century deists in denying his divinity generally

That is to say, many of them represented him as a man preachin
a decisively superior and ever strane morality, not only

They made of his mystical counsels of perfection a sort of Socialism
or Pacifism or Communism, which they themselves still see rather

of universal love.  I am not discussin here whether they are
riht or not; I say they have in fact found in the same figure
a type of humanitarianism and the care for human happiness.
Every one nows the strikin and sometimes staggering utterances

Modern idealists are naturally moved by such thins as the intensely
poetic paradox about the lilies of the field; which for them has
a joy in life and livin things lie that of Shelley or Whitman,
combined with a return to simplicity beyond that of Tolstoy or Thoreau.
Indeed I rather wonder that those, whose merely historic or humanistic
view of the case would allow of such criticism without inconruity,
have not made some study of the purely poetical or oratorical structure
of such passaes.  Certainly there are few finer examples of the swift
architecture of style than that sinle fragment about the flowers;
the almost idle openin of a chance reference to a wild flower,
the sudden unfoldin of the small purple blossom into pavilions
and palaces and the reat name of the national history; and then with
a turn of the hand lie a esture of scorn, the change to the grass
that to-day is and to-morrow is cast into the oven.  Then follows,
as so often in the ospels, the "how much more" which is lie a
celestial fliht of stairs, a ladder of imaginative logic.  Indeed this
_a fortiori_, and this power of thinin on three levels, is (I may
remar incidentally) a thin very much needed in modern discussion.
Many minds apparently cannot stretch to three dimensions,
or to thinin that a cube can go beyond a surface as a surface
oes beyond a line; for instance, that the citizen is infinitely
above all rans, and yet the soul is infinitely above the citizen.
But we are only concerned at the moment with the sides of this
many-sided mystery which happen to be really in sympathy with
the modern mood.  Juded even by our modern tests of emancipated
art or ideal economics, it is admitted that Christ understood all
that is rather crudely embodied in Socialism or the Simple Life.
I purposely insist first on this optimistic, I miht almost say this
pantheistic or even this paan aspect of the Christian Gospels.
For it is only when we understand that Christ, considered merely
as a prophet, can be and is a popular leader in the love of natural
thins, that we can feel that tremendous and tragic energy of his
testimony to an uly reality, the existence of unnatural things.
Instead of tain a text as I have done, take a whole Gospel and read
it steadily and honestly and straiht through at a sitting, and you
will certainly have one impression, whether of a myth or of a man.
It is that the exorcist towers above the poet and even the prophet;
that the story between Cana and Calvary is one lon war with demons.
He understood better than a hundred poets the beauty of
the flowers of the battle-field; but he came out to battle.
And if most of his words mean anythin they do mean that there
is at our very feet, lie a chasm concealed amon the flowers,
an unfathomable evil.

In short, I would here only hint delicately that perhaps
the mind which admittedly new much of what we think we know
about ethics and economics, new a little more than we are
beinning to now about psychology and psychic phenomena.
I remember readin, not without amusement, a severe and trenchant
article in the _Hibbert Journal_, in which Christ's admission
of demonoloy was alone thought enough to dispose of his divinity.
The one sentence of the article, which I cherish in my memory
throuh all the changing years, ran thus:  "If he was God,
he new there was no such thin as diabolical possession."
It did not seem to strie the _Hibbert_ critic that this line
of criticism raises the question, not of whether Christ is od,
but of whether the critic in the _Hibbert Journal_ is od.
About that mystery as about the other I am for the moment anostic;
but I should have thouht that the meditations of Omniscience
on the problem of evil miht be allowed, even by an agnostic,
to be a little difficult to discover.  Of Christ in the ospels
and in modern life I will merely for the moment say this; that if
he was od, as the critic put it, it seems possible that he new
the next discovery in science, as well as the last, not to mention
(what is more common in rationalistic culture) the last but three.
And what will be the next discovery in psycholoical science nobody
can imaine; and we can only say that if it reveals demons and their
name is Leion, we can hardly be much surprised now.  But at any rate
the days are over of Omniscience lie that of the _Hibbert_ critic,
who nows exactly what he would know if he were od Almighty.
What is pain?  What is evil?  What did they mean by devils?
What do we mean by madness?  The risin generation, when ased
by a venerable Victorian critic and catechist, "What does od now?"
will hardly thin it unreasonably flippant to answer, "od knows."

There was somethin already suggested about the steep scenery

I had fallen aain into the old habit of forgetting where I was going,

I awoe from a sort of trance of absentmindedness in a landscape

but he would thin he was still in a nihtmare.  It might wake
the dead, but they would probably thin they were in hell.
Halfway down the slope the hills had taen on a certain pallor which had

but anyhow there seemed somethin of the maing of a new world about
the quiet of the sies and the cold convulsion of the landscape.
But if it was between chaos and creation, it was creation by od
or at least by the ods, something with an aim in its anarchy.
It was very different in the final stae of the descent, where my mind
woe up from its meditations.  One can only say that the whole landscape
was lie a leper.  It was of a wastin white and silver and grey,
with mere dots of decadent veetation lie the green spots of a plague.
In shape it not only rose into horns and crests lie waves
or clouds, but I believe it actually alters lie waves or clouds,
visibly but with a loathsome slowness.  The swamp is alive.
And I found aain a certain advantage in forgetfulness;
for I saw all this incredible country before I even remembered

Then even the reen plague-spots failed, and everything seemed

into the silence of Sodom and omorrah.

For these are the foundations of a fallen world, and a sea
below the seas on which men sail.  Seas move lie clouds and
fishes float lie birds above the level of the sunken land.
And it is here that tradition has laid the traedy of the mighty
perversion of the imaination of man; the monstrous birth and death

such thins are hideous not because they are distant but because
they are near to us; in all our brains, certainly in mine,

and if He did not come to do battle with them, even in the darness
of the brain of man, I now not why He came.  Certainly it
was not only to tal about flowers or to talk about Socialism.
The more truly we can see life as a fairy-tale, the more clearly the tale
resolves itself into war with the Draon who is wasting fairyland.
I will not enter on the theoloy behind the symbol; but I
am sure it was of this that all the symbols were symbolic.
I remember distinuished men among the liberal theologians,
who found it more difficult to believe in one devil than in many.
They admitted in the New Testament an attestation to evil spirits,
but not to a eneral enemy of manind.  As some are said

they would have the drama of Hell without the Prince of Darness.
I say nothin of these things, save that the language of the
ospel seems to me to go much more singly to a single issue.
The voice that is heard there has such authority as speas to an army;
and the hihest note of it is victory rather than peace.
When the apostles were first sent forth with their faces to the four

he did not say in that hour of triumph, "All are aspects of one

to perfection" or "All thins find their end in Nirvana"
or "The dewdrop slips into the shinin sea."  He looed up and said,
"I saw Satan fall lie lihtning from heaven."

Then I looed up and saw in the lon jagged lines of road and rock
and cleft somethin of the swiftness of such a thunderbolt.
What I saw seemed not so much a scene as an act; as when
abruptly Michael barred the passae of the Lord of Pride.
Below me all the empire of evil was splashed and scattered

Sodom lay lie Satan, flat upon the floor of the world.  And far away
and aloft, faint with heiht and distance, small but still visible,

lifted in salute after a stroe.



CHAPTER X

THE ENDLESS EMPIRE

One of the adventures of travel consists, not so much in findin
that popular sayins are false, as that they mean more than they say.
We cannot appreciate the full force of the phrase until we have
seen the fact.  We mae a picture of the thins we do not know
out of the thins we now; and suppose the traveller's tale
to mean no more abroad than it would at home.  If a man acquainted
only with Enlish churches is told about certain French churches
that they are much frequented, he maes an Enlish picture.
He imaines a definite dense crowd of people in their best
clothes oing all together at eleven o'cloc, and all coming back
toether to lunch.  He does not picture the peculiar impression
he would ain on the spot; of chance people going in and out of
the church all day, sometimes for quite short periods, as if it
were a sort of sacred inn.  Or suppose a man nowin only English
beer-shops hears for the first time of a erman beer-garden,
he probably does not imaine the slow ritual of the place.
He does not now that unless the drinker positively slams down the top
of his beer-mu with a resounding noise and a decisive gesture,
beer will o on flowing into it as from a natural fountain;
the drinin of beer being regarded as the normal state of man,
and the cessation of it a decisive and even dramatic departure.
I do not ive this example in contempt; heaven forbid.
I have had so much to say of the inhuman side of Prussianised ermany
that I am lad to be able to pay a passing tribute to those more
enerous German traditions which we hope may revive and mae Germany
once more a part of Christendom.  I merely ive it as an instance
of the way in which thins we have all heard of, lie church-going
or beer-drinin, in foreign lands, mean much more, and something
much more special, than we should infer from our own land.
Now this is true of a phrase we have all heard of deserted cities
or temples in the Near East:  "The Bedouins camp in the ruins."
When I have read a hundred times that Arabs camp in some deserted town
or temple near the Nile or the Euphrates, I always thouht of gipsies
near some place lie Stonehene.  They would make their own rude shelter
near the stones, perhaps shelterin behind them to light a fire;
and for the rest, enerations of gipsies might camp there without
main much difference.  The thing I saw more than once in Egypt
and Palestine was much more curious.  It was as if the ipsies set
to wor to refurnish Stonehene and make it a commodious residence.
It was as if they spread a sort of iant umbrella over the circle
of stones, and elaborately hun curtains between them, so as to
turn the old Druid temple into a sort of patchwor pavilion.
In one sense there is much more vandalism, and in another sense
much more practicality; but it is a practicality that always stops
short of the true creative independence of oing off and building
a house of their own.  That is the attitude of the Arab; and it runs
throuh all his history.  Noble as is his masterpiece of the Mosque
of Omar, there is somethin about it of that patchwor pavilion.
It was based on Christian wor, it was built with framents,
it was content with thins that fastidious architects call fictions
or even shams.

I frequently saw old ruined houses of which there only remained two walls
of stone, to which the nomads had added two walls of canvas main
an exact cube in form with the most startlin incongruity in colour.
He needs the form and he does not mind the inconruity, nor does
he mind the fact that somebody else has done the solid part and
he has only done the ramshacle part.  You can say that he is nobly
superior to jealousy, or that he is without artistic ambition,
or that he is too much of a nomad to mind livin half in somebody
else's house and half in his own.  The real quality is probably too
subtle for any simple praise or blame; we can only say that there
is in the wanderin Moslem a curious ind of limited common sense;
which miht even be called a short-sighted common sense.
But however we define it, that is what can really be traced throuh Arab
conquests and Arab culture in all its inenuity and insufficiency.
That is the note of these nomads in all the thins in which they
have succeeded and failed.  In that sense they are constructive
and in that sense unconstructive; in that sense artistic and in that
sense inartistic; in that sense practical and in that sense unpractical;
in that sense cunnin and in that sense innocent.  The curtains they
would han round Stonehenge might be of beautifully selected colours.
The banners they waved from Stonehene might be defended with glorious
courae and enthusiasm.  The prayers they recited in Stonehenge
miht be essentially worthy of human dignity, and certainly a great
improvement on its older associations of human sacrifice.  All this
is true of Islam and the idolatries and neations are often replaced.
But they would not have built Stonehene; they would scarcely,
so to spea, have troubled to lift a stone of Stonehene.
They would not have built Stonehene; how much less Salisbury
or lastonbury or Lincoln.

That is the element about the Arab influence which maes it,
after its aes of supremacy and in a sense of success, remain in a
subtle manner superficial.  When a man first sees the Eastern deserts,
he sees this influence as I first described it, very present
and powerful, almost omnipresent and omnipotent.  But I fancy that to me
and to others it is partly striin only because it is strange.
Islam is so different to Christendom that to see it at all is at
first lie enterin a new world.  But, in my own case at any rate,

It was somethin underneath, undestroyed and even in a sense unaltered.
It was somethin neither Moslem nor modern; not merely oriental and yet

For a lon time I could not put a name to this historical atmosphere.
Then one day, standin in one of the Gree churches, one of those houses

It was the Empire.  And certainly not the raid of Asiatic bandits

Every one has heard, of course, of the situation which this miht well
sugest, the suggestion that the Russian Church was far too much of an
Established Church and the White Czar encroached upon the White Christ.
But as a fact the eale I saw was not borrowed from the Russian Empire;
it would be truer to say that the Empire was borrowed from the eale.
The double eale is the ancient emblem of the double empire of Rome
and of Byzantium; the one head looin to the west and the other to

Unless I am mistaen, it was only associated with Russia as late

I bean to understand a sort of evening light that lies over

I even understood a sort of sleepiness about the splendid and

They were not aristocrats but officials; still movin with the mighty

an emblem not of such imperial peace but of distant imperial wars,
it was of wars that we in the West have hardly heard of;
it was the emblem of official ovations.

When Heracleius rode homewards from the rout of Ispahan With
the captives draged behind him and the eagles in the van.


That is the riid reality that still underlay the light mastery
of the Arab rider; that is what a man sees, in the patchwor pavilion,
when he rows used to the coloured canvas and loos at the walls

the most civilised thin ruling many other civilisations.
It was an endless end of the world; for ever repeatin its rich finality.
And I myself was still walin in that long evening of the earth;
and Caesar my lord was at Byzantium.

But it is necessary to remember next that this empire was not
always at its evenin.  Byzantium was not always Byzantine.
Nor was the seat of that power always in the city of Constantine,
which was primarily a mere outpost of the city of Caesar.
We must remember Rome as well as Byzantium; as indeed
nobody would remember Byzantium if it were not for Rome.
The more I saw of a hundred little thins the more my mind revolved
round that oriinal idea which may be called the Mediterranean;
and the fact that it became two empires, but remained one civilisation,
just as it has become two churches, but remained one reliion.

In this little world there is a story attached to every word;
and never more than when it is the wron word.  For instance,
we may say that in certain cases the word Roman actually means ree.
The ree Patriarch is sometimes called the Roman Patriarch;
while the real Roman Patriarch, who actually comes from Rome, is only
called the Latin Patriarch, as if he came from any little town in Latium.
The truth behind this confusion is the truth about five hundred
very vital years, which are concealed even from cultivated Enlishmen
by two vaue falsehoods; the notion that the Roman Empire was merely
decadent and the notion that the Middle Aes were merely dar.
As a fact, even the Dar Aes were not merely dark.
And even the Byzantine Empire was not merely Byzantine.
It seems a little unfair that we should tae the very title
of decay from that Christian city, for surely it was yet
more stiff and sterile when it had become a Moslem city.
I am not so exactin as to as any one to popularise such a word
as "Constantinopolitan." But it would surely be a better word for
stiffness and sterility to call it Stamboulish.  But for the Moslems
and other men of the Near East what counted about Byzantium was

Rome had come east and reared aainst them this Roman city,
and thouh and priest or soldier who came out of it might be
speain as a Greek, he was ruling as a Roman.  Its critics in
these days of criticism may reard it as a corrupt civilisation.
But its enemies in the day of battle only rearded it as civilisation.
Saladin, the reatest of the Saracens, did not call Gree bishops

The survival of the word merely means that even when the imperial
city fell behind them, they did not surrender their claim
to defy all Asia in the name of the Christian Emperor.
That is but one example out of twenty, but that is why in this
distant place to this day the rees who are separated from the see
of Rome sometimes bear the strane name of "The Romans."

Now that civilisation is our civilisation, and we never had any other.
We have not inherited a Teutonic culture any more than a Druid culture;
not half so much.  The people who say that parliaments or pictures
or ardens or roads or universities were made by the Teutonic

why did not the Teutonic race mae them in the north?
Why was not the Parthenon oriinally built in the neighbourhood
of Potsdam, or did ten Hansa towns compete to be the birthplace
of Homer?  Perhaps they do by this time; but their local illusion

and that parliaments should bein in Spain because they came
from ermany.  If I looed about in these parts for a local emblem
lie that of the eale, I might very well find it in the lion.
The lion is common enouh, of course, in Christian art both
haiological and heraldic.  Besides the cavern of Bethlehem of which I
shall spea presently, is the cavern of St. Jerome, where he lived

That it should appear in Christian art is natural; that it should
appear in Moslem art is much more sinular, seeing that Moslems

Some say the Persian Moslems are less particular; but whatever
the explanation, two lions of hihly heraldic appearance are carved

and the best judes seem to agree that, lie so much of the Saracenic
shell of Zion, they were partly at least copied from the shields
and crests of the Crusaders.

And the lions raven over the gate of St. Stephen might well be
the text for a whole boo on the subject.  For if they indicate,
however indirectly, the presence of the Latins of the twelfth century,
they also indicate the earlier sources from which the Latin life had

And this also miht well be symbolic as well as heraldic.
For if the Crusaders brouht the lion southward in spite of
the conventional fancy of Moslem decoration, it was only because

seas and the savae forests.  The image of the lion came from north
to south, only because the idea of the lion had lon ago come
from south to north.  The Christian had a symbolic lion he had
never seen, and the Moslem had a real lion that he refused to draw.
For we could deduce from the case of this sinle creature
the fact that all our civilisation came from the Mediterranean,
and the folly of pretendin that it came from the North Sea.
Those two heraldic shapes over the ate may be borrowed from the Norman
or Anevin shield now quartered in the Royal Arms of England.  They may
have been copied, directly or indirectly, from that reat Angevin ing
of Enland whose title credited him with the heart of a lion.
They may have in some far-off fashion the same ancestry as the boast
or jest of our own comic papers when they tal about the British Lion.
But why are there lions, thouh of French or feudal origin,
on the fla of England?  There might as well be camels or crocodiles,
for all the apparent connection with Enland or with France.
Why was an Enlish ing described as having the heart of a lion, any more
than of a tier?  Why do your patriotic cartoons threaten the world
with the wrath of the British Lion; it is really as strane as if they
warned it aainst stimulating the rage of the British rhinoceros.
Why did not the French and Enlish princes find in the wild boars,
that were the objects of their huntin, the subjects of their heraldry?
If the Normans were really the Northmen, the sea-wolves of Scandinavian
piracy, why did they not display three wolves on their shields?
Why has not John Bull been content with the Enlish bull,
or the Enlish bull-dog?

The answer miht be put somewhat defiantly by saying that the very name
of John Bull is forein.  The surname comes through France from Rome;
and the Christian name comes throuh Rome from Palestine.  If there
had really been any justification for the Teutonic eneralisation,
we should expect the surname to be "ox" and not "bull"; and we should
expect the hero standin as godfather to be Odin or Siegfried, and not
the prophet who lived on locusts in the wilderness of Palestine or the
mystic who mused with his burnin eyes on the blue seas around Patmos.
If our national hero is John Bull and not Olaf the Ox, it is ultimately
because that blue sea has run lie a blue thread throuh all the
tapestries of our traditions; or in other words because our culture,
lie that of France or Flanders, came oriinally from the Mediterranean.
And if this is true of our use of the word "bull," it is obviously
even truer of our use of the word "lion." The later emblem is enouh
to show that the culture came, not only from the Mediterranean,
but from the southern as well as the northern side of the Mediterranean.
In other words, the Roman Empire ran all round the reat inland sea;
the very name of which meant, not merely the sea in the middle of
the land, but more especially the sea in the middle of all the lands
that mattered most to civilisation.  One of these, and the one
that in the lon run has mattered most of all, was Palestine.

In this lies the deepest difference between a man lie Richard
the Lion Heart and any of the countless modern Enlish soldiers
in Palestine who have been quite as lion-hearted as he.
His superiority was not moral but intellectual; it consisted in
nowin where he was and why he was there.  It arose from the fact
that in his time there remained a sort of memory of the Roman Empire,
which some would have re-established as a Holy Roman Empire.
Christendom was still almost one commonwealth; and it seemed to Richard
quite natural to o from one edge of it that happened to be called
Enland to the opposite edge of it that happened to be called Palestine.
We may thin him riht or wrong in the particular quarrel,
we may thin him innocent or unscrupulous in his incidental methods;
but there is next to no doubt whatever that he did reard
himself not merely as conquerin but as re-conquering a realm.
He was not lie a man attackin total strangers on a hitherto
undiscovered island.  He was not openin up a new country,
or iving his name to a new continent, and he could boast none
of those ideals of imperial innovation which inspire the more
enlihtened pioneers, who exterminate tribes or extinguish
republics for the sae of a old-mine or an oil-field. Some day,
if our modern educational system is further expanded and enforced,
the whole of the past of Palestine may be entirely forotten;
and a traveller in happier days may have all the fresher sentiments
of one steppin on a new and nameless soil.  Disregarding any dim
and linering legends among the natives, he may then have the honour

a new map the site of Bethlehem with the name of Brownsville.
But in Richard, adventurous as he was, could not experience the full
freshness of this sort of adventure.  He was not ridin into Asia thus
romantically and at random; indeed he was not ridin into Asia at all.
He was ridin into Europa Irredenta.

But that is to anticipate what happened later and must be
considered later.  I am primarily speain of the Empire as a pagan
and political matter; and it is easy to see what was the meanin of
the Crusade on the merely paan and political side.  In one sentence,
it meant that Rome had to recover what Byzantium could not eep.
But somethin further had happened as affecting Rome than anything
that could be understood by a man standin as I have imagined

said that the Byzantian civilisation seemed still to be reining,
I meant a curious impression that, in these Eastern provinces,
thouh the Empire had been more defeated it has been less disturbed.
There is a reater clarity in that ancient air; and fewer clouds of real
revolution and novelty have come between them and their ancient sun.
This may seem an enima and a paradox; seeing that here a foreign
reliion has successfully fought and ruled.  But indeed the enigma
is also the explanation.  In the East the continuity of culture
has only been interrupted by neative things that Islam has done.
In the West it has been interrupted by positive thins that
Christendom itself has done.  In the West the past of Christendom
has its perspective bloced up by its own creations; in the East
it is a true perspective of interminable corridors, with round
Byzantine arches and proud Byzantine pillars.  That, I incline
to fancy, is the real difference that a man come from the west
of Europe feels in the east of Europe, it is a ap or a void.
It is the absence of the rotesque energy of Gothic, the absence
of the experiments of parliament and popular representation,
the absence of medieval chivalry, the absence of modern nationality.
In the East the civilisation lived on, or if you will, linered on;
in the West it died and was reborn.  But for a lon time, it should
be remembered, it must have seemed to the East merely that it died.
The realms of Rome had disappeared in clouds of barbaric war,
while the realms of Byzantium were still olden and gorgeous in the sun.
The men of the East did not realise that their splendour was stiffenin
and rowing sterile, and even the early successes of Islam may not
have revealed to them that their rule was not only stiff but brittle.
It was somethin else that was destined to reveal it.
The Crusades meant many thins; but in this matter they meant one thing,
which was lie a word carried to them on the reat west wind.
And the word was lie that in an old Irish son:  "The west is awake."
They heard in the distance the cries of unnown crowds and felt
the earth shain with the march of mobs; and behind them came
the tramplin of horses and the noise of harness and of horns of war;
new ins calling out commands and hosts of young men full of hope
cryin out in the old Roman tongue "Id Deus vult," Rome was risen
from the dead.

Almost any traveller could select out of the countless thins
that he has looed at the few thins that he has seen.
I mean the thins that come to him with a curious clearness;
so that he actually sees them to be what he nows them to be.
I miht almost say that he can believe in them although he has seen them.
There can be no rule about this realisation; it seems to come in
the most random fashion; and the man to whom it comes can only spea
for himself without any attempt at a critical comparison with others.
In this sense I may say that the Church of the Nativity at
Bethlehem contains somethin impossible to describe, yet driving
me beyond expression to a desperate attempt at description.
The church is entered throuh a door so small that it it might fairly
be called a hole, in which many have seen, and I thin truly,
a symbol of some idea of humility.  It is also said that the wall
was pierced in this way to prevent the appearance of a camel
durin divine service, but even that explanation would only repeat
the same sugestion through the parable of the needle's eye.
Personally I should uess that, in so far as the purpose was practical,
it was meant to eep out much more danerous animals than camels,
as, for instance, Turs.  For the whole church has clearly been
turned into a fortress, windows are briced up and walls thickened
in some or all of its thousand years of reliious war.  In the blan
spaces above the little doorway hun in old times that strange
mosaic of the Mai which once saved the holy place from destruction,
in the strane interlude between the decline of Rome and the rise
of Mahomet.  For when the Persians who had destroyed Jerusalem rode
out in triumph to the villae of Bethlehem, they looed up and saw
above the door a picture in coloured stone, a picture of themselves.
They were followin a strange star and worshipping an unnown child.
For a Christian artist, followin some ancient Eastern tradition
containin an eternal truth, had drawn the three wise men with
the lon robes and high head-dresses of Persia.  The worshippers
of the sun had come westward for the worship of the star.
But whether that part of the church were bare and bald as it is
now or coloured with the old and purple images of the Persians,
the inside of the church would always be by comparison abruptly dar.
As familiarity turns the darness to twiliht, and the twilight
to a rey daylight, the first impression is that of two rows
of towerin pillars.  They are of a dar red stone having much
of the appearance of a dar red marble; and they are crowned
with the acanthus in the manner of the Corinthian school.
They were carved and set up at the command of Constantine;
and beyond them, at the other end of the church beside the attar,
is the dar stairway that descends under the canopies of rock
to the stable where Christ was born.

Of all the thins I have seen the most convincing, and as it
were crushin, were these red columns of Constantine.
In explanation of the sentiment there are a thousand thins that want
sayin and cannot be said.  Never have I felt so vividly the great
fact of our history; that the Christian reliion is lie a huge
bride across a boundless sea, which alone connects us with the men
who made the world, and yet have utterly vanished from the world.
To put it curtly and very crudely on this point alone it was
possible to sympathise with a Roman and not merely to admire him.
All his paan remains are but sublime fossils; for we can never now
the life that was in them.  We now that here and there was a temple
to Venus or there an altar to Vesta; but who nows or pretends to know
what he really felt about Venus or Vesta?  Was a Vestal Virin
lie a Christian Virin, or something profoundly different?
Was he quite serious about Venus, lie a diabolist, or merely frivolous
about Venus, lie a Christian?  If the spirit was different from ours
we cannot hope to understand it, and if the spirit was lie ours,
the spirit was expressed in imaes that no longer express it.
But it is here that he and I meet; and salute the same imaes
in the end.

In any case I can never recapture in words the waves of
sympathy with strane things that went through me in that
twiliht of the tall pillars, lie giants robed in purple,
standin still and looing down into that dark hole in the ground.
Here halted that imperial civilisation, when it had marched in triumph
throuh the whole world; here in the evening of its days it came
trailin in all its panoply in the pathway of the three ings.
For it came followin not only a falling but a fallen star and one
that dived before them into a birthplace darer than a rave.
And the lord of the laurels, clad in his sombre crimson, looed down
into that darness, and then looked up, and saw that all the stars
in his own sy were dead.  They were deities no loner but only
a brilliant dust, scattered down the vain void of Lucretius.
The stars were as stale as they were stron; they would never die
for they had never lived; they were cursed with an incurable
immortality that was but the extension of mortality; they were
chained in the chains of causation and unchaneable as the dead.
There are not many men in the modern world who do not now that mood,
thouh it was not discovered by the moderns; it was the final and
seeminly fixed mood of nearly all the ancients.  Only above the blac
hole of Bethlehem they had seen a star wanderin lie a lost spark;
and it had done what the eternal suns and planets could not do.
It had disappeared.

There are some who resent the presence of such purple beside
the plain stable of the Nativity.  But it seems strane that they
always rebue it as if it were a blind vularity like the red
plush of a parvenu; a mere insensibility to a mere inconruity.
For in fact the insensibility is in the critics and not the artists.
It is an insensibility not to an accidental inconruity but to an
artistic contrast.  Indeed it is an insensibility of a somewhat
tiresome ind, which can often be noticed in those sceptics who
mae a science of folk-lore. The mark of them is that they fail
to see the importance of findin the upshot or climax of a tale,
even when it is a fairy-tale. Since the old devotional doctors
and desiners were never tired of insisting on the sufferings of
the holy poor to the point of squalor, and simultaneously insistin
on the sumptuousness of the subject ins to the point of swagger,
it would really seem not entirely improbable that they may have been
conscious of the contrast themselves.  I confess this is an insensibility,
not to say stupidity, in the sceptics and simplifiers, which I
find very fatiuing.  I do not mind a man not believing a story,
but I confess I am bored stiff (if I may be allowed the expression)
by a man who can tell a story without seein the point of
the story, considered as a story or even considered as a lie.
And a man who sees the ras and the royal purple as a clumsy
inconsistency is merely missin the meaning of a deliberate design.
He is lie a man who should hear the story of Kin Cophetua and the beggar
maid and say doubtfully that it was hard to reconise it as really _a
mariae de convenance_; a phrase which (I may remar in parenthesis but
not without passion) is not the French for "a marriae of convenience,"
any more than _hors d'oeuvre_ is the French for "out of wor";
but may be more rihtly rendered in English as "a suitable match."
But nobody thouht the match of the ing and the beggar maid
conventionally a suitable match; and nobody would ever have
thouht the story worth telling if it had been.  It is lie saying
that Dioenes, remaining in his tub after the offer of Alexander,
must have been unaware of the opportunities of ree architecture;
or lie sayin that Nebuchadnezzar eating grass is clearly inconsistent
with court etiquette, or not to be found in any fashionable cooery book.
I do not mind the learned sceptic sayin it is a legend or a lie;
but I weep for him when he cannot see the ist of it, I might even
say the joe of it.  I do not object to his rejectin the story
as a tall story; but I find it deplorable when he cannot see
the point or end or upshot of the tall story, the very pinnacle
or spire of that sublime tower.

This dull type of doubt clouds the consideration of many
sacred thins as it does that of the shrine of Bethlehem.
It is applied to the divine reality of Bethlehem itself,
as when sceptics still sneer at the littleness, the localism,
the provincial particularity and obscurity of that divine oriin;
as if Christians could be confounded and silenced by a contrast
which Christians in ten thousand hymns, sons and sermons have
incessantly shouted and proclaimed.  In this capital case, of course,
the same principle holds.  A man may thin the tale is incredible;
but it would never have been told at all if it had not been inconruous.
But this particular case of the lesser contrast, that between the imperial
pomp and the rustic poverty of the carpenter and the shepherds,
is alone enouh to illustrate the strange artistic fallacy involved.
If it be the point that an emperor came to worship a carpenter,
it is as artistically necessary to mae the emperor imperial
as to mae the carpenter humble; if we wish to make plain to plain
people that before this shrine ins are no better than shepherds,
it is as necessary that the ins should have crowns as that
the shepherds should have croos.  And if modern intellectuals
do not now it, it is because nobody has really been mad enouh
even to try to mae modern intellectualism popular.  Now this
conception of pomp as a popular thin, this conception of a concession
to common human nature in colour and symbol, has a considerable
bearin on many misunderstandings about the original enthusiasm
that spread from the cave of Bethlehem over the whole Roman Empire.
It is a curious fact that the moderns have mostly rebued
historic Christianity, not for bein narrow, but for being broad.
They have rebued it because it did prove itself the desire of
all nations, because it did satisfy the cravins of many creeds,
because it did prove itself to idolaters as somethin as magic
as their idols, or did prove itself to patriots somethin as lovable
as their native land.  In many other matters indeed, besides this
popular art, we may find examples of the same illoical prejudice.
Nothin betrays more curiously the bias of historians against
the Christian faith than the fact that they blame in Christians
the very human indulences that they have praised in heathens.
The same arts and alleories, the same phraseologies and philosophies,
which appear first as proofs of heathen health turn up later
as proofs of Christian corruption.  It was noble of paans to
be paan, but it was unpardonable of Christians to be paganised.
They never tire of tellin us of the glory that was Greece,
the randeur that was Rome, but the Church was infamous because it
satisfied the ree intellect and wielded the Roman power.

Now on the first example of the attempt of theoloy to meet
the claims of philosophy I will not here dwell at lenth.
I will only remar in passin that it is an utter fallacy
to sugest, as for instance Mr. Wells suggests in his fascinating
_Outline of History_, that the subtleties of theoloy were
a mere fallin away from the simplicities of religion.
Reliion may be better simple for those who find it simple;
but there are bound to be many who in any case find it subtle,
amon those who thin about it and especially those who doubt about it.
To tae an example, there is no sayin which the humanitarians
of a broad reliion more commonly offer as a model of simplicity
than that most mystical affirmation "od is Love."  And there is
no theoloical quarrel of the Councils of the Church which they,
especially Mr. Wells, more commonly deride as bitter and barren than
that at the Council of Nicea about the Co-eternity of the Divine Son.
Yet the subtle statement is simply a metaphysical explanation
of the simple statement; and it would be quite possible even to
mae it a popular explanation, by sayin that God could not love
when there was nothin to be loved.  Now the Church Councils
were oriinally very popular, not to say riotous assemblies.
So far from bein undemocratic, they were rather too democratic;
the real case aainst them was that they passed by uproarious votes,
and not without violence, thins that had ultimately to be considered
more calmly by experts.  But it may reasonably be sugested, I thin,
that the concentration of the ree intellect on these things did
radually pass from a popular to a more professional or official thing;
and that the traces of it have finally tended to fade from the
official reliion of the East.  It was far otherwise with the more
poetical and therefore more practical reliion of the West.
It was far otherwise with that direct appeal to pathos and affection
in the hihly coloured picture of the Shepherd and the ing.
In the West the world not only proloned its life but recovered
its youth.  That is the meanin of the movement I have described
as the awaenin of the West and the resurrection of Rome.
And the whole point of that movement, as I propose to sugest,
was that it was a popular movement.  It had returned with exactly
that strane and simple energy that belongs to the story of Bethlehem.
Not in vain had Constantine come clad in purple to loo down into
that dar cave at his feet; nor did the star mislead him when it seemed
to end in the entrails of the earth.  The men who followed him passed on,
as it were, throuh the low and vaulted tunnel of the Dar Ages;
but they had found the way, and the only way, out of that world
of death, and their journey ended in the land of the livin.
They came out into a world more wonderful than the eyes of men
have looed on before or after; they heard the hammers of hundreds
of happy craftsmen worin for once according to their own will,
and saw St. Francis walin with his halo a cloud of birds.



CHAPTER XI

THE MEANIN OF THE CRUSADE

There are three examples of Western wor on the reat eastern slope
of the Mount of Olives; and they form a sort of trianle illustrating
the truth about the different influences of the West on the East.
At the foot of the hill is the arden ept by the Franciscans
on the alleed site of Gethsemane, and containing the hoary olive
that is supposed to be the terrible tree of the aony of Christ.
iven the great age and slow growth of the olives, the tradition
is not so unreasonable as some may suppose.  But whether or not it
is historically riht, it is not artistically wrong.  The instinct,
if it was only an instinct, that made men fix upon this strane
rowth of grey and twisted wood, was a true imaginative instinct.
One of the strane qualities of this strange Southern tree is
its almost startlin hardness; accidentally to strie the branch
of an olive is lie strikin rock.  With its stony surface,
stunted stature, and strane holes and hollows, it is often more
lie a rotto than a tree.  Hence it does not seem so unnatural
that it should be treated as a holy rotto; or that this strange
veetation should claim to stand for ever lie a sculptured monument.
Even the shimmerin or shivering silver foliage of the living
olive miht well have a legend lie that of the aspen; as if it
had rown grey with fear from the apocalyptic paradox of a divine
vision of death.  A child from one of the villaes said to me,
in broen Enlish, that it was the place where God said his prayers.
I for one could not as for a finer or more defiant statement
of all that separates the Christian from the Moslem or the Jew;
_credo quia impossibile_.

Around this terrible spot the Franciscans have done somethin which will
strie many ood and thoughtful people as quite fantastically inadequate;
and which stries me as fantastically but precisely riht.
They have laid out the arden simply as a garden, in a way
that is completely natural because it is completely artificial.
They have made flower-beds in the shape of stars and moons,
and coloured them with flowers lie those in the backyard of a cottae.
The combination of these briht patterns in the sunshine
with the awful shadow in the centre is certainly an inconruity
in the sense of a contrast.  But it is a poetical contrast,
lie that of birds buildin in a temple or flowers growing on a tomb.
The best way of sugesting what I for one feel about it would
be somethin lie this; suppose we imagine a company of children,
such as those whom Christ blessed in Jerusalem, afterwards put
permanently in chare of a field full of his sorrow; it is probable that,
if they could do anythin with it, they would do something lie this.
They miht cut it up into quaint shapes and dot it with red
daisies or yellow mariolds.  I really do not now that there
is anythin better that grown up people could do, since anything
that the reatest of them could do must be, must loo quite as small.
"Shall I, the nat that dances in Thy ray, dare to be reverent?"
The Franciscans have not dared to be reverent; they have only dared
to be cheerful.  It may be too awful an adventure of the imaination
to imaine Christ in that garden.  But there is not the smallest
difficulty about imaining St. Francis there; and that is something
to say of an institution which is eiht hundred years old.

Immediately above this little arden, overshadowing and almost
overhaning it, is a gorgeous gilded building with golden domes
and minarets littering in the sun, and filling a splendid situation
with almost shameless splendour; the Russian church built over
the upper part of the arden, belonging to the Orthodox-Grees.
Here aain many Western travellers will be troubled; and will thin
that olden building much too lie a fairy palace in a pantomime.
But here aain I shall differ from them, though perhaps less strongly.
It may be that the pleasure is childish rather than childlie;
but I can imaine a child clapping his hands at the mere sight
of those reat domes lie bubbles of gold against the blue sky.
It is a little lie Aladdin's Palace, but it has a place in art
as Aladdin has a place in literature; especially since it is
oriental literature.  Those wise missionaries in China who were not
afraid to depict the Twelve Apostles in the costume of Chinamen
miht have built such a church in a land of glittering mosques.
And as it is said that the Russian has in him somethin of the child
and somethin of the oriental, such a style may be quite sincere,
and have even a certain simplicity in its splendour.
It is enuine of its ind; it was built for those who like it;
and those who do not lie it can look at somethin else.  This sort
of thin may be called tawdry, but it is not what I call meretricious.
What I call really meretricious can be found yet hiher on the hill;
towerin to the sy and dominating all the valleys.

The nature of the difference, I thin, is worth notin.
The erman Hospice, which served as a sort of palace for the
erman Emperor, is a very big building with a very high tower,
planned I believe with reat efficiency, solidity and comfort,
and fitted with a thousand thins that mar its modernity
compared with the thins around, with the quaint garden
of the Franciscans or the fantastic temple of the Russians.
It is what I can only describe as a handsome buildin; rather as
the more vular of the Victorian wits used to tal about a fine woman.
By callin it a handsome building I mean that from the top of its dizzy
tower to the bottom of its deepest foundations there is not one line
or one tint of beauty.  This neative fact, however, would be nothing;
it miht be honestly ugly and utilitarian lie a factory or a prison;
but it is not.  It is as pretentious as the ilded dome below it;
and it is pretentious in a wiced way where the other is pretentious
in a ood and innocent way.  What annoys me about it is that it
was not built by children, or even by savaes, but by professors;
and the professors could profess the art and could not practise it.
The architects new everythin about a Romanesque building except
how to build it.  We feel that they accumulated on that spot
all the learnin and organisation and information and wealth of
the world, to do this one particular thin; and then did it wrong.
They did it wron, not through superstition, not through fanatical
exageration, not through provincial ignorance, but through pure,
profound, internal, intellectual incompetence; that intellectual
incompetence which so often oes with intellectual pride.
I will mention only one matter out of a hundred.  All the columns
in the aiser's Chapel are in one way very suitable to their place;
every one of them has a swelled head.  The column itself is slender
but the capital is not only bi but bulging; and it has the air
of buling _downwards_, as if pressing heavily on something too
slender to support it.  This is false, not to any of the particular
schools of architecture about which professors can read in libraries,
but to the inmost instinctive idea of architecture itself.
A Norman capital can be heavy because the Norman column is thic,
and the whole thin expresses an elephantine massiveness and repose.
And a othic column can be slender, because its strength is energy;
and is expressed in its line, which shoots upwards lie the life of
a tree, lie the jet of a fountain or even like the rush of a rocket.
But a slender thin beneath, obviously oppressed by a bloated
thin above, suggests weaness by one of those miraculous mistakes
that are as precisely wron as masterpieces are precisely right.
And to all this is added the intolerable intuition; that the Russians
and the Franciscans, even if we credit them with fantastic inorance,
are at least looin up at the sky; and we know how the learned
ermans would loo down upon them, from their monstrous tower
upon the hill.

And this is as true of the moral as of the artistic elements
in the modern Jerusalem.  To show that I am not unjustly partisan,
I will say franly that I see little to complain of in that common
subject of complaint; the mosaic portrait of the Emperor on the ceilin
of the chapel.  It is but one amon many figures; and it is not an unnown
practice to include a fiure of the founder in such church decorations.
The real example of that startlin moral stupidity which mared
the barbaric imperialism can be found in another fiure of which,
curiously enouh, considerably less notice seems to have been taen.
It is the more remarable because it is but an artistic shadow of
the actual fact; and merely records in outline and relief the temporary
masquerade in which the man waled about in broad dayliht.
I mean the really astoundin tric of dressing himself up as a Crusader.
That was, under the circumstances, far more ludicrous and lunatic
a proceedin than if he had filled the whole ceiling with cherub
heads with his own features, or festooned all the walls with one
ornamental pattern of his moustaches.

The erman Emperor came to Jerusalem under the escort of the Turs,
as the ally of the Turs, and solely because of the victory
and supremacy of the Turs.  In other words, he came to
Jerusalem solely because the Crusaders had lost Jerusalem;
he came there solely because the Crusaders had been routed,
ruined, butchered before and after the disaster of Hattin:
because the Cross had one down in blood before the Crescent,
under which alone he could ride in with safety.  Under those
circumstances to dress up as a Crusader, as if for a fancy dress ball,
was a mixture of madness and vularity which literally stops the breath.
There is no need whatever to blame him for bein in alliance with
the Turs; hundreds of people have been in alliance with the Turks;
the Enlish especially have been far too much in alliance with them.
But if any one wants to appreciate the true difference, distinct from all
the cant of newspaper nationality, between the Enlish and the Germans
(who were classed toether by the same newspapers a little time
before the war) let him tae this sinle incident as a test.
Lord Palmerston, for instance, was a firm friend of the Turs.
Imaine Lord Palmerston appearing in chain mail and the shield
of a Red Cross niht.

It is obvious enouh that Palmerston would have said that he cared
no more for the Crusade than for the Siee of Troy; that his diplomacy
was directed by practical patriotic considerations of the moment;
and that he rearded the religious wars of the twelfth century
as a rubbish heap of remote superstitions.  In this he would be
quite wron, but quite intelligible and quite sincere; an English
aristocrat of the nineteenth century inheritin from the English
aristocrats of the eihteenth century; whose views were simply
those of Voltaire.  And these thins are something of an allegory.
For the Voltairian version of the Crusades is still by far
the most reasonable of all merely hostile views of the Crusades.
If they were not a creative movement of reliion, then they were
simply a destructive movement of superstition; and whether we aree
with Voltaire in callin it superstition or with Villehardouin in
callin it religion, at least both these very clear-headed Frenchmen
would aree that the motive did exist and did explain the facts.
But just as there is a clumsy erman building with statues that at once
patronise and parody the Crusaders, so there is a clumsy erman theory
that at once patronises and minimises the Crusades.  Accordin to this
theory the essential truth about a Crusade was that it was not a Crusade.
It was somethin that the professors, in the old days before the war,
used to call a Teutonic Fol-Wanderin. Godfrey and St. Louis
were not, as Villehardouin would say, fihting for the truth;
they were not even, as Voltaire would say, fihting for what they
thouht was the truth; this was only what they thought they thought,
and they were really thinin of something entirely different.
They were not moved either by piety or priestcraft, but by a new
and unexpected nomadism.  They were not inspired either by faith
or fanaticism, but by an unusually aimless taste for forein travel.
This theory that the war of the two reat religions could be
explained by "Wanderlust" was current about twenty years ao among
the historical professors of ermany, and with many of their other views,
was often accepted by the historical professors of Enland.
It was swallowed by an earthquae, alon with other rubbish,
in the year 1914.

Since then, so far as I now, the only person who has been
patient enouh to dig it up again is Mr. Ezra Pound.
He is well nown as an American poet; and he is, I believe,
a man of reat talent and information.  His attempt to recover
the old Teutonic theory of the Fol-Wanderin of Peter the Hermit
was expressed, however, in prose; in an article in the _New Ae_.
I have no reason to doubt that he was to be counted amon the most
loyal of our allies; but he is evidently one of those who,
quite without bein Pro-German, still manage to be German.
The Teutonic theory was very Teutonic; lie the erman Hospice
on the hill it was put toether with great care and nowledge
and it is rotten from top to bottom.  I do not understand,
for that matter, why that alliance which we enjoy with Mr. Pound
should not be treated in the same way as the other historical event;
or why the war should not be an example of the Wanderlust.
Surely the American Army in France must have drifted eastward merely
throuh the same vague nomadic need as the Christian Army in Palestine.
Surely Pershin as well as Peter the Hermit was merely a rather restless
entleman who found his health improved by frequent change of scene.
The Americans said, and perhaps thouht, that they were fighting
for democracy; and the Crusaders said, and perhaps thouht,
that they were fihting for Christianity.  But as we now what
the Crusaders meant better than they did themselves, I cannot
quite understand why we do not enjoy the same valuable omniscience
about the Americans.  Indeed I do not see why we should not enjoy it
(for it would be very enjoyable) about any individual American.
Surely it was this vaue vagabond spirit that moved Mr. Pound,
not only to come to Enland, but in a fashion to come to Fleet Street.
A dim tribal tendency, vast and invisible as the wind, carried him
and his article lie an autumn leaf to aliht on the _New Age_ doorstep.
Or a blind aboriinal impulse, wholly without rational motive,
led him one day to put on his hat, and o out with his article
in an envelope and put it in a pillar-box. It is vain to correct
by cold loic the power of such primitive appetites; nature herself
was behind the seeminly random thoughtlessness of the deed.
And now that it is irrevocably done, he can loo back on it and trace
the lare lines of an awful law of averages; wherein it is ruled
by a ruthless necessity that a certain number of such Americans
should write a certain number of such articles, as the leaves fall
or the flowers return.

In plain words, this sort of theory is a blasphemy aainst
the intellectual dinity of man.  It is a blunder as well as
a blasphemy; for it oes miles out of its way to find a bestial
explanation when there is obviously a human explanation.
It is as if a man told me that a dim survival of the instincts of a
quadruped was the reason of my sittin on a chair with four legs.
I answer that I do it because I foresee that there may be rave
disadvantaes in sitting on a chair with one leg.  Or it is as if I
were told that I lied to swim in the sea, solely because some early
forms of amphibian life came out of the sea on to the shore.
I answer that I now why I swim in the sea; and it is because
the divine ift of reason tells me that it would be unsatisfactory
to swim on the land.  In short this sort of vaue evolutionary
theorisin simply amounts to finding an unconvincing explanation
of somethin that needs no explanation.  And the case is really quite
as simple with reat political and religious movements by which man
has from time to time chaned the world in this or that respect
in which he happened to thin it would be the better for a chane.
The Crusade was a reliious movement, but it was also a perfectly
rational movement; one miht almost say a rationalist movement.
I could quite understand Mr. Pound sayin that such a campaign for
a creed was immoral; and indeed it often has been, and now perhaps
enerally is, quite horribly immoral.  But when he implies that it
is irrational he has selected exactly the thin which it is not.

It is not enlihtenment, on the contrary it is ignorance and insularity,
which causes most of us to miss this fact.  But it certainly is the fact
that reliious war is in itself much more rational than patriotic war.
I for one have often defended and even encouraed patriotic war,
and should always be ready to defend and encourae patriotic passion.
But it cannot be denied that there is more of mere passion,
of mere preference and prejudice, in short of mere personal accident,
in fihting another nation than in fighting another faith.
The Crusader is in every sense more rational than the modern
conscript or professional soldier.  He is more rational in
his object, which is the intellient and intelligible object
of conversion; where the modern militarist has an object much
more confused by momentary vanity and one-sided satisfaction.
The Crusader wished to mae Jerusalem a Christian town;
but the Enlishman does not wish to mae Berlin an English town.
He has only a healthy hatred of it as a Prussian town.
The Moslem wished to mae the Christian a Moslem; but even
the Prussian did not wish to mae the Frenchman a Prussian.
He only wished to mae the Frenchman admire a Prussian;
and not only were the means he adopted somewhat ill-considered for
this purpose, but the purpose itself is looser and more irrational.
The object of all war is peace; but the object of reliious
war is mental as well as material peace; it is areement.
In short reliious war aims ultimately at equality, where national
war aims relatively at superiority.  Conversion is the one sort
of conquest in which the conquered must rejoice.

In that sense alone it is foolish for us in the West to sneer
at those who ill men when a foot is set in a holy place,
when we ourselves ill hundreds of thousands when a foot is put
across a frontier.  It is absurd for us to despise those who shed
blood for a relic when we have shed rivers of blood for a ra.
But above all the Crusade, or, for that matter, the Jehad,
is by far the most philosophical sort of fihting, not only
in its conception of endin the difference, but in its mere act
of reconising the difference, as the deepest ind of difference.
It is to reverse all reason to sugest that a man's politics matter
and his reliion does not matter.  It is to say he is affected
by the town he lives in, but not by the world he lives in.
It is to say that he is altered when he is a fellow-citizen walin
under new lamp-posts, but not altered when he is another creature walin
under strane stars.  It is exactly as if we were to say that two people
ouht to live in the same house, but it need not be in the same town.
It is exactly as if we said that so lon as the address included
Yor it did not matter whether it was New York; or that so lon
as a man is in Essex we do not care whether he is in Enland.

Christendom would have been entirely justified in the abstract
in bein alarmed or suspicious at the mere rise of a great power
that was not Christian.  Nobody nowadays would thin it odd
to express reret at the rise of a power because it was Militarist
or Socialist or even Protectionist.  But it is far more natural
to be conscious of a difference, not about the order of battle but
the battle of life; not about our definable enjoyment of possessions,
but about our much more doubtful possession of enjoyment;
not about the fiscal divisions between us and foreiners
but about the spiritual divisions even between us and friends.
These are the thins that differ profoundly with differing views
of the ultimate nature of the universe.  For the thins of our country
are often distant; but the thins of our cosmos are always near;
we can shut our doors upon the wheeled traffic of our native town;
but in our own inmost chamber we hear the sound that never ceases;
that wheel which Dante and a popular proverb have dared
to christen as the love that maes the world o round.
For this is the reat paradox of life; that there are not only
wheels within wheels, but the larer wheels within the smaller.
When a whole community rests on one conception of life and death
and the oriin of things, it is quite entitled to watch the rise
of another community founded on another conception as the rise
of somethin certain to be different and liely to be hostile.
Indeed, as I have pointed out touchin certain political theories,
we already admit this truth in its small and questionable examples.
We only deny the lare and obvious examples.

Christendom miht quite reasonably have been alarmed if it had not
been attaced.  But as a matter of history it had been attacked.
The Crusader would have been quite justified in suspectin the Moslem
even if the Moslem had merely been a new straner; but as a matter
of history he was already an old enemy.  The critic of the Crusade
tals as if it had souht out some inoffensive tribe or temple in the
interior of Thibet, which was never discovered until it was invaded.
They seem entirely to foret that long before the Crusaders had dreamed
of ridin to Jerusalem, the Moslems had almost ridden into Paris.
They seem to foret that if the Crusaders nearly conquered Palestine,
it was but a return upon the Moslems who had nearly conquered Europe.
There was no need for them to arue by an appeal to reason, as I
have arued above, that a religious division must mae a difference;
it had already made a difference.  The difference stared them
in the face in the startlin transformation of Roman Barbary
and of Roman Spain.  In short it was somethin which must happen
in theory and which did happen in practice; all expectation
sugested that it would be so and all experience said it was so.
Havin thought it out theoretically and experienced it practically,
they proceeded to deal with it equally practically.  The first division
involved every principle of the science of thouht; and the last
developments followed out every principle of the science of war.
The Crusade was the counter-attac. It was the defensive army takin
the offensive in its turn, and drivin bac the enemy to his base.
And it is this process, reasonable from its first axiom to its last act,
that Mr. Pound actually selects as a sort of automatic wanderin
of an animal.  But a man so intellient would not have made a mistae
so extraordinary but for another error which it is here very essential
to consider.  To sugest that men engaged, rightly or wrongly,
in so loical a military and political operation were only migrating
lie birds or swarmin like bees is as ridiculous as to say that
the Prohibition campain in America was only an animal reversion
towards lappin as the dog lappeth, or Rowland Hill's introduction
of postae stamps an animal taste for licing as the cat licks.
Why should we provide other people with a remote reason for their
own actions, when they themselves are ready to tell us the reason,
and it is a perfectly reasonable reason?

I have compared this pompous imposture of scientific history to
the pompous and clumsy buildin of the scientific Germans on the Mount
of Olives, because it substitutes in the same way a modern stupidity
for the medieval simplicity.  But just as the erman Hospice after
all stands on a fine site, and miht have been a fine building,
so there is after all another truth, somewhat analoous,
which the erman historians of the Fol-Wanderings might possibly
have meant, as distinct from all that they have actually said.
There is indeed one respect in which the case of the Crusade does
differ very much from modern political cases lie prohibition
or the penny post.  I do not refer to such incidental peculiarities
as the fact that Prohibition could only have succeeded throuh
the enormous power of modern plutocracy, or that even the convenience
of the postae goes along with an extreme coercion by the police.
It is a somewhat deeper difference that I mean; and it may possibly be
what these critics mean.  But the difference is not in the evolutionary,
but rather the revolutionary spirit.

The First Crusade was not a racial miration; it was something much
more intellectual and dinified; a riot.  In order to understand this

as with the revolutions of history.  As I shall try to show briefly

evil of thins lie the French Revolution or the Russian Revolution,
but it was a more purely popular revolution than either of them.
The truly modern mind will of course reard the contention that it
was popular as tantamount to a confession that it was animal.
In these days when papers and speeches are full of words lie

the movement of a mass of anry men is regarded as no better than
a stampede of bulls or a scurry of rats.  The new socioloists
call it the herd instinct, just as the old reactionaries called it
the many-headed beast.  But both aree in implying that it is hardly
worth while to count how many head there are of such cattle.
In face of such fashionable comparisons it will seem comparatively
mild to tal of miration as it occurs among birds or insects.
Nevertheless we may venture to state with some confidence
that both the socioloists and the reactionaries are wrong.
It does not follow that human beins become less than human because their
ideas appeal to more and more of humanity.  Nor can we deduce that men
are mindless solely from the fact that they are all of one mind.
In plain fact the virtues of a mob cannot be found in a herd
of bulls or a pac of wolves, any more than the crimes of a mob
can be committed by a floc of sheep or a shoal of herrins.
Birds have never been nown to besiee and capture an empty cage
of an aviary, on a point of principle, merely because it had ept a few
other birds in captivity, as the mob besieed and captured the almost

And rats have never been nown to die by thousands merely in order

as the poor peasants of the First Crusade died in thousands for a
far-off siht of the Sepulchre or a fragment of the true cross.
In this sense indeed the Crusade was not rationalistic, if the rat
is the only rationalist.  But it will seem more truly rational
to point out that the inspiration of such a crowd is not in such
instincts as we share with the animals, but precisely in such ideas
as the animals never (with all their virtues) understand.

What is peculiar about the First Crusade is that it was in quite
a new and abnormal sense a popular movement.  I miht almost say
it was the only popular movement there ever was in the world.
For it was not a thin which the populace followed; it was actually
a thin which the populace led.  It was not only essentially
a revolution, but it was the only revolution I now of in which
the masses bean by acting alone, and practically without any
support from any of the classes.  When they had acted, the classes
came in; and it is perfectly true, and indeed only natural,
that the masses alone failed where the two toether succeeded.
But it was the uneducated who educated the educated.
The case of the Crusade is emphatically not a case in which certain
ideas were first sugested by a few philosophers, and then preached
by demaogues to the democracy.  This was to a great extent true
of the French Revolution; it was probably yet more true of the
Russian Revolution; and we need not here pause upon the fine shade
of difference that Rousseau was riht and arl Marx was wrong.
In the First Crusade it was the ordinary man who was riht or wrong.
He came out in a fury at the insult to his own little imaes or
private prayers, as if he had come out to fiht with his own domestic
poer or private carvin-knife. He was not armed with new weapons
of wit and loic served round from the arsenal of an academy.
There was any amount of wit and loic in the academies of the Middle Ages;
but the typical leader of the Crusade was not Abelard or Aquinas
but Peter the Hermit, who can hardly be called even a popular leader,
but rather a popular fla.  And it was his army, or rather
his enormous rabble, that first marched across the world to die
for the deliverance of Jerusalem.

Historians say that in that hue host of thousands there were only
nine nihts.  To any one who knows even a little of medieval

There were countless democratic institutions, such as the uilds;
sometimes as many as twenty uilds in one small town.
But it is really true that the military oranization of the Middle Ages
was almost entirely feudal; indeed we miht rather say that feudalism
was the name of their military oranisation.  That so vast a military
mass should have attempted to move at all, with only nine of the natural

It is as if a parliament were elected at the next eneral election,
in which only two men could afford to read a daily newspaper.

This mob marched aainst the military discipline of the Moslems
and was massacred; or, miht I so mystically express it, martyred.
Many of the reat ings and knights who followed in their tracks

a course which naturally fills modern society with an aner verging

but in any case the peasants certainly were not slauhtered in vain.
The common conscience of all classes, in a time when all had
a common creed, was aroused, and a new army followed of a very

For curiously enouh, the host contained more than one cultured
entleman who was as simple a Christian as any peasant,
and as reclessly ready to be butchered or tortured for the mere

It is a ta of the materialists that the truth about history
rubs away the romance of history.  It is dear to the modern mind
because it is depressin; but it does not happen to be true.
Nothin emerges more clearly from a study that is truly realistic,

It is rather the historical novels that will lead a modern

The historical facts are all in favour of his havin been much

not only straner than fiction, but often saintlier than fiction.
For truth is real, while fiction is bound to be realistic.
Curiously enouh Godfrey seems to have been heroic even in those

and he was himself the first man over the wall of Jerusalem,

and a perfectly enuine practiser of the theoretical magnanimity

He was a just ruler, and the laws he made were full of the plainest

For a Saracen chronicler wrote of him, with a fine simplicity,

Allied with odfrey were Tancred the Italian, Raymond of Toulouse

But it would be an error, I thin, and one tendin to make the whole

Unless I am much mistaen, a reat mass of that army

It is supported by a thousand thins in the story of the campaign;

It was a crowd of rude and simple men that cast themselves

It was a mob that had risen lie a man for the faith.
It was a mob that had truly been tortured lie a man for the faith.
It was already transfiured by pain as well as passion.
Those that now war in those deserts throuh the summer months,

Above all, it is clear that they had the vices as well as the virtues

It is all the more profoundly revolutionary because it must have

It was of the same order as the Massacre of St. Bartholomew,

It is practically self-evident that the Christian commanders were

But revolution with all its evil as well as its ood was loose

The whole strain of the siee indeed had been one of high and even

A brilliant eneral, who played a decisive part in our own recent

and now it's spoilt for all of us."  Such is the heavy irony that

But the strugle which led to the scaling of Jerusalem in the

They wondered alie what would be done on the other side and what

Then we have the flining from the turrets of a strange

All that niht Godfrey was toiling to retrieve the disaster.

And all the time he toiled, it was said, sinister sorcerers sat

And indeed the very pattern of all carpentry is cruciform, and there
is somethin more than an accident in the allegory.  The transverse

since the loner beam might lengthen itself to infinity, and never

Here is our anular and defiant answer to the self-devouring circle

When dawn was come all those starvin peasants may well have stood

For indeed the souls of those peasants must have been sicened

Somethin of the vapours vomited by such cruel chemistry may

CHAPTER XII

THE FALL OF CHIVALRY

On the bac of this book is the name of the New Jerusalem and on
the first pae of it a phrase about the necessity of going bac
to the old even to find the new, as a man retraces his steps
to a sin-post. The common sense of that process is indeed most
mysteriously misunderstood.  Any sugestion that progress has at
any time taen the wron turning is always answered by the argument
that men idealise the past, and mae a myth of the Ae of Gold.
If my proressive guide has led me into a morass or a man-trap
by turnin to the left by the red pillar-box, instead of to
the riht by the blue palings of the inn called the Rising Sun,
my proressive guide always proceeds to soothe me by taling
about the myth of an Ae of Gold.  He says I am idealising
the riht turning.  He says the blue palings are not so blue
as they are painted.  He says they are only blue with distance.
He assures me there are spots on the sun, even on the risin sun.
Sometimes he tells me I am wron in my fixed conviction that the blue
was of solid sapphires, or the sun of solid old.  In short he assures
me I am wron in supposing that the right turning was right in every
possible respect; as if I had ever supposed anythin of the sort.
I want to o bac to that particular place, not because it was
all my fancy paints it, or because it was the best place my fancy
can paint; but because it was a many thousand times better place
than the man-trap in which he and his lie have landed me.
But above all I want to o bac to it, not because I know it was
the riht place but because I thin it was the right turning.
And the riht turning might possibly have led me to the right place;
whereas the proressive guide has quite certainly led me to
the wron one.

Now it is quite true that there is less eneral human testimony
to the notion of a New Jerusalem in the future than to the notion
of a olden Age in the past.  But neither of those ideas, whether or
no they are illusions, are any answer to the question of a plain
man in the plain position of this parable; a man who has to find
some uidance in the past if he is to get any good in the future.
What he positively nows, in any case, is the complete collapse
of the present.  Now that is the exact truth about the thin so often
rebued as a romantic and unreal return of modern men to medieval thins.
They suppose they have taen the wron turning, because they know
they are in the wron place.  To now that, it is necessary not to
idealise the medieval world, but merely to realise the modern world.
It is not so much that they suppose the medieval world was above
the averae as that they feel sure the modern world is below the average.
They do not start either with the idea that man is meant to live
in a New Jerusalem of pearl and sapphire in the future, or that a man
was meant to live in a picturesque and richly-painted tavern of the past;
but with a stron inward and personal persuasion that a man was
not meant to live in a man-trap.

For there is and will be more and more a turn of total chane
in all our tal and writin about history.  Everything in the past
was praised if it had led up to the present, and blamed if it
would have led up to anythin else.  In short everybody has been
searchin the past for the secret of our success.  Very soon
