They may be talin in such terms as they use after a motor smash
or a banruptcy; where was the blunder?  They may be writin such books
as enerals write after a military defeat; whose was the fault?
The failure will be assumed even in bein explained.

For industrialism is no loner a vulgar success.
On the contrary, it is now too traic even to be vulgar.
Under the cloud of doom the modern city has taen on somethin

the rich rumble as much as the poor; every one is discontented, and none

By whatever process in the past we miht have come to the

And for many a premonition will row more and more of a probability;
that we may or may not await another century or another world

but in the flesh we shall see Babylon fall.

But there is another way in which that metaphor of the fored road

It would be nearer the truth I mean to call it the ae in which

This peculiar part played by Jerusalem I mention merely as a sugestion;

Medievalism died, but it died youn.  It was at once energetic
and incomplete when it died, or very shortly before it died.
This is not a matter of sympathy or antipathy, but of appreciation
of an interestin historic comparison with other historic cases.
When the Roman Empire finally failed we cannot of course say
that it had done all it was meant to do, for that is domatism.
We cannot even say it had done all that it miht have done,
for that is uesswor.  But we can say that it had done

that it had lon and even literally rested on its laurels.
But suppose that Rome had fallen when she had only half defeated Carthae,
or when she had only half conquered aul, or even when the city was

It was not merely that the medievals left undone what they miht

promise is proved not only in their successes but in their failures.
It is shown, for instance, in the very defects of their art.
All the crafts of which othic architecture formed the frame-wor

A statue lie the St. eorge of Donatello would have stood

That the new uildsmen add a great deal that never belonged

When we renew a frustrated thin we do not renew the frustration.
But if there are some thins in the new that were not in the old,

The point here, however, is not merely that the worer worked well

The frost had struc it in the sprin.

Some people complain that the worin man of our own day does

The medieval worman took so individual an interest in his work

It may be just as well that builders and briclayers do not

Some of the carvin did remain so rude that the angels were almost

Men with medieval sympathies are sometimes accused, absurdly enouh,

In truth the whole case for it is that it was imperfect.

It recalls one of these typical medieval speculations, full of
the very fantasy of free will, in which the schoolmen tried to fancy

It remains, in a cant historical phrase, one of the reat

I have said that it died youn; but perhaps it would be truer to say that

When I looed for the last time on the towers of Zion I had a

I believe the whole medieval society failed, because the heart

For the war aainst Islam was not lost.  The Moslem was overthrown

I mean the purely popular picture of the Holy City.

For its mystery was and is in standin in the middle, or as they

Anyhow the fall of Jerusalem, and in that sense the failure of

I have seen in some of that lost literature of the old uilds,

There is noticeable in some of the later popular poems a

The only thin to do with unconquerable things is to conquer them.
That alone will cure them of invincibility; or what is worse, their own
vision of invincibility.  That was the conviction of those of us who
would not accept what we considered a premature peace with Prussia.
That is why we would not listen either to the Tory Pro-ermanism
of Lord Lansdowne or the Socialist Pro-ermanism of Mr. Macdonald.
If a lunatic believes in his luc so fixedly as to feel sure be
cannot be cauht, he will not only believe in it still, but believe
in it more and more, until the actual instant when he is cauht.
The loner the chase, the more certain he will be of escaping;
the more narrow the escapes, the more certain will be the escape.
And indeed if he does escape it will seem a miracle, and almost
a divine intervention, not only to the pursued but to the pursuers.
The evil thin will chiefly appear unconquerable to those who try

and those who failed aainst it will hide in their hearts

A vote, a jury, a newspaper, would not be as they are,

A vote would actually loo like a vassal's cry of "haro,"

A curious cloud of confusion rests on the details of that defeat.
The Christian captains who acted in it were certainly men on a different

And it is the irony of the incident that Raymond ot into trouble

Renaud exacted from Moslem travellers on a certain road what

This was rearded as a breach of the truce; but probably it would

Probably Raymond was not a traitor, since the military advice he ave

But the very fact of such chares being bandied among the factions

was a traitor; no enemy ever asserted that odfrey was only a thief.
It is fairly clear that there had been a deeneration; but most people

The first Crusades had really had some notion of Jerusalem as a
New Jerusalem.  I mean they had really had a vision of the place bein
not only a promised land but a Utopia or even an Earthly Paradise.
The outstandin fact and feature which is seldom seized is this:
that the social experiment in Palestine was rather in advance of

"The basis of these laws was found in Justinian's code, and they
presented features as yet quite unnown in Europe, especially in their

and for the tradin communes whose fleets were so necessary to the ing.
Not only were free men juded by juries of their equals, but the same
applied to those who were technically serfs and actually aboriines."
The oriinal arrangements of the Native Court seem to me singularly
liberal, even by modern standards of the treatment of natives.
That in many such medieval codes citizens were still called serfs is
no more final than the fact that in many modern capitalist newspapers
serfs are still called citizens.  The whole point about the villein
was that he was a tenant at least as permanent as a peasant.
He "went with the land"; and there are a ood many hopeless tramps
starvin in streets, or sleeping in ditches, who might not be sorry
if they could o with a little land.  It would not be very much
worse than homelessness and huner to go with a good itchen garden
of which you could always eat most of the beans and turnips;
or to o with a good cornfield of which you could tae a considerable
proportion of the corn.  There has been many a modern man would have been
none the worse for "oing" about burdened with such a green island,
or draging the chains of such a tangle of green living things.
As a fact, of course, this system throuhout Christendom was already
evolvin rapidly into a pure peasant proprietorship; and it will be
lon before industrialism evolves by itself into anything so equal

of the medieval movement; the voluntary liberation of slaves.
But we may willinly allow that something of the earlier success

He had quarrelled with Raymond, who was ruler of alilee, and a

But the most serious threat to them and their New Jerusalem

With the breach of the truce, or even the tale of it, the common
daner of Christians was apparent; and Raymond of Tripoli repaired

erard of Bideford, the fanatic who was Grand Master of the Templars,

In that hour fell, as I have fancied, more hopes than they themselves

There fell with them all that New Jerusalem which was the symbol

Raymond of Tripoli had hewn his way throuh the enemy and ridden

There occurred a scene stranely typical of the mingled strains

"It is thou and not I who hast iven him to drin," said the Saracen,
preservin the precise letter of the punctilio of hospitality.
Then he suddenly flun himself raving and reviling upon Renaud

Outside, two hundred Hospitallers and Templars were beheaded on
the field of battle; by one account I have read because Saladin
dislied them, and by another because they were Christian priests.

There is a stron bias against the Christians and in favour of
the Moslems and the Jews in most of the Victorian historical wors,
especially historical novels.  And most people of modern,
or rather of very recent times ot all their notions of history
from dippin into historical novels.  In those romances the Jew is
always the oppressed where in reality he was often the oppressor.
In those romances the Arab is always credited with oriental dinity
and courtesy and never with oriental crooedness and cruelty.
The same injustice is introduced into history, which by means
of selection and omission can be made as fictitious as any fiction.
Twenty historians mention the way in which the maddened Christian
mob murdered the Moslems after the capture of Jerusalem, for one who
mentions that the Moslem commander commanded in cold blood the murder
of some two hundred of his most famous and valiant enemies after
the victory of Hattin.  The former cannot be shown to have been the act
of Tancred, while the latter was quite certainly the act of Saladin.
Yet Tancred is described as at best a doubtful character,
while Saladin is represented as a Bayard without fear or blame.
Both of them doubtless were ordinary faulty fihting men, but they
are not juded by an equal balance.  It may seem a paradox that there
should be this prejudice in Western history in favour of Eastern heroes.
But the cause is clear enouh; it is the remains of the revolt among many
Europeans aainst their own old religious organisation, which naturally
made them hunt throuh all ages for its crimes and its victims.
It was natural that Voltaire should sympathise more with a Brahmin
he had never seen than with a Jesuit with whom he was enaged in a
violent controversy; and should similarly feel more dislie of a Catholic
who was his enemy than of a Moslem who was the enemy of his enemy.
In this atmosphere of natural and even pardonable prejudice arose
the habit of contrastin the intolerance of the Crusaders with
the toleration shown by the Moslems.  Now as there are two sides
to everythin, it would undoubtedly be quite possible to tell
the tale of the Crusades, correctly enouh in detail, and in such
a way as entirely to justify the Moslems and condemn the Crusaders.
But any such real record of the Moslem case would have very
little to do with any questions of tolerance or intolerance,
or any modern ideas about reliious liberty and equality.
As the modern world does not now what it means itself by reliious
liberty and equality, as the moderns have not thouht out any logical
theory of toleration at all (for their vaue generalisations can
always be upset by twenty tests from Thus to Christian Science)
it would obviously be unreasonable to expect the moderns
to understand the much clearer philosophy of the Moslems.
But some rouh suggestion of what was really involved may be found
convenient in this case.

Islam was not oriinally a movement directed against Christianity at all.
It did not face westwards, so to spea; it faced eastwards towards
the idolatries of Asia.  But Mahomet believed that these idols
could be fouht more successfully with a simpler ind of creed;
one miht almost say with a simpler ind of Christianity.
For he included many thins which we in the West commonly suppose not
only to be peculiar to Christianity but to be peculiar to Catholicism.
Many thins have been rejected by Protestantism that are not
rejected by Mahometanism.  Thus the Moslems believe in Puratory,
and they ive at least a sort of dignity to the Mother of Christ.
About such thins as these they have little of the bitterness that ranles
in the Jews and is said sometimes to become hideously vitriolic.  While I
was in Palestine a distinuished Moslem said to a Christian resident:
"We also, as well as you, honour the Mother of Christ.
Never do we spea of her but we call her the Lady Miriam.
I dare not tell you what the Jews call her."

The real mistae of the Moslems is somethin much more modern in its
application than any particular or passin persecution of Christians
as such.  It lay in the very fact that they did thin they had a
simpler and saner sort of Christianity, as do many modern Christians.
They thouht it could be made universal merely by being
made uninterestin.  Now a man preaching what he thins is a platitude
is far more intolerant than a man preachin what he admits is a paradox.
It was exactly because it seemed self-evident, to Moslems as
to Bolshevists, that their simple creed was suited to everybody,
that they wished in that particular sweepin fashion to impose it
on everybody.  It was because Islam was broad that Moslems were narrow.
And because it was not a hard reliion it was a heavy rule.
Because it was without a self-correctin complexity, it allowed
of those simple and masculine but mostly rather danerous
appetites that show themselves in a chieftain or a lord.
As it had the simplest sort of reliion, monotheism, so it had
the simplest sort of overnment, monarchy.  There was exactly
the same direct spirit in its despotism as in its deism.
The Code, the Common Law, the ive and tae of charters
and chivalric vows, did not row in that golden desert.
The reat sun was in the sy and the great Saladin was in his tent,
and he must be obeyed unless he were assassinated.  Those who
complain of our creeds as elaborate often foret that the elaborate
Western creeds have produced the elaborate Western constitutions;
and that they are elaborate because they are emancipated.
And the real moral of the relations of the two reat religions is
somethin much more subtle and sincere than any mere atrocity tales
aainst Turs.  It is the same as the moral of the Christian refusal
of a Paan Pantheon in which Christ should ran with Ammon and Apollo.
Twice the Christian Church refused what seemed lie a handsome
offer of a lare latitudinarian sort; once to include Christ as a
od and once to include him as a prophet; once by the admission
of all idols and once by the abandonment of all idols.
Twice the Church too the risk and twice the Church survived alone
and succeeded alone, fillin the world with her own children;
and leavin her rivals in a desert, where the idols were dead
and the iconoclasts were dyin.

But all this history has been hidden by a prejudice more
eneral than the particular case of Saracens and Crusaders.
The modern, or rather the Victorian prejudice aainst Crusaders
is positive and not relative; and it would still desire to
condemn Tancred if it could not acquit Saladin.  Indeed it is
a prejudice not so much aainst Crusaders as against Christians.
It will not ive to these heroes of religious war the fair measure
it ives to the heroes of ordinary patriotic and imperial war.
There never was a nobler hero than Nelson, or one more national
or more normal.  Yet Nelson quite certainly did do what Tancred
almost certainly did not do; brea his own word by iving up his own
brave enemies to execution.  If the cause of Nelson in other times
comes to be treated as the creed of Tancred has often in recent
times been treated, this incident alone will be held sufficient
to prove not only that Nelson was a liar and a scoundrel, but that
he did not love Enland at all, did not love Lady Hamilton at all,
that he sailed in Enlish ships only to pocet the prize money
of French ships, and would as willinly have sailed in French ships
for the prize money of Enlish ships.  That is the sort of dull dust
of old that has been shaen like the drifting dust of the desert
over the swords and the relics, the crosses and the clasped
hands of the men who marched to Jerusalem or died at Hattin.
In these medieval pilrims every inconsistency is a hypocrisy; while in
the more modern patriots even an infamy is only an inconsistency.
I have rounded off the story here with the ruin at Hattin because
the whole reaction aainst the pilgrimage had its origin there;
and because it was this at least that finally lost Jerusalem.
Elsewhere in Palestine, to say nothin of Africa and Spain,
splendid counter-stroes were still bein delivered from the West,
not the least bein the splendid rescue by Richard of England.
But I still thin that with the mere name of that tiny town upon
the hills the note of the whole human revolution had been struc,
was chaned and was silent.  All the other names were only the names
of Eastern towns; but that was nearer to a man than his neihbours;
a villae inside his village, a house inside his house.

There is a hill above Bethlehem of a strane shape, with a flat top
which maes it look oddly like an island, habitable thouh uninhabited,
when all Moab heaves about it and beyond it as with the curves
and colours of a sea.  Its stability sugests in some strange
fashion what may often be felt in these lands with the lonest
record of culture; that there may be not only a civilisation
but even a chivalry older than history.  Perhaps the table-land
with its round top has a romantic reminiscence of a round table.
Perhaps it is only a fantastic effect of evenin, for it is felt
most when the low sies are swimmin with the colours of sunset,
and in the shadows the shattered rocs about its base take on
the shapes of titanic paladins fihting and falling around it.
I only now that the mere shape of the hill and vista of the landscape
sugested such visions and it was only afterwards that I heard
the local leend, which says it is here that some of the Christian
nihts made their last stand after they lost Jerusalem and which
names this heiht The Mountain of the Latins.

They fell, and the aes rolled on them the rocs of scorn;
they were buried in jests and buffooneries.  As the Renascence
expanded into the rationalism of recent centuries, nothin seemed
so ridiculous as to butcher and bleed in a distant desert not only
for a tomb, but an empty tomb.  The last leend of them withered
under the wit of Cervantes, thouh he himself had fought in the last
Crusade at Lepanto.  They were icked about like dead donkeys
by the cool vivacity of Voltaire; who went off, very symbolically,
to dance attendance on the new drill-sereant of the Prussians.
They were dissected lie strane beasts by the serene disgust
of ibbon, more serene than the similar horror with which
he rearded the similar violence of the French Revolution.
By our own time even the flippancy has become a platitude.
They have lon been the butt of every penny-a-liner who can tal of a
helmet as a tin pot, of every caricaturist on a comic paper who can draw
a fat man fallin off a bucing horse; of every pushing professional
politician who can tal about the superstitions of the Middle Aes.
reat men and small have agreed to contemn them; they were renounced
by their children and refuted by their bioraphers; they were exposed,
they were exploded, they were ridiculed and they were riht.

They were proved wron, and they were right.  They were judged
finally and forotten, and they were right.  Centuries after
their fall the full experience and development of political
discovery has shown beyond question that they were riht.
For there is a very simple test of the truth; that the very
thin which was dismissed, as a dream of the ages of faith,
we have been forced to turn into a fact in the aes of fact.
It is now more certain than it ever was before that Europe must
rescue some lordship, or overlordship, of these old Roman provinces.
Whether it is wise for Enland alone to claim Palestine, whether it
would be better if the Entente could do so, I thin a serious question.
But in some form they are revertin for the Roman Empire.
Every opportunity has been iven for any other empire that could
be its equal, and especially for the reat dream of a mission
for Imperial Islam.  If ever a human bein had a run for his money,
it was the Sultan of the Moslems ridin on his Arab steed.
His empire expanded over and beyond the reat Gree empire of Byzantium;
a last chare of the chivalry of Poland barely stopped it at the very
ates of Vienna.  He was free to unfold everything that was in him,
and he unfolded the death that was in him.  He reined and he could
not rule; he was successful and he did not succeed.  His baffled
and retreatin enemies left him standing, and he could not stand.
He fell finally with that other half-heathen power in the North,
with which he had made an alliance aainst the remains of Roman
and Byzantine culture.  He fell because barbarism cannot stand;
because even when it succeeds it rather falls on its foes and
crushes them.  And after all these thins, after all these ages,
with a wearier philosophy, with a heavier heart, we have been forced
to do aain the very thing that the Crusaders were derided for doing.
What Western men failed to do for the faith, other Western men
have been forced to do even without the faith.  The sons of Tancred
are aain in Tripoli.  The heirs of Raymond are again in Syria.
And men from the Midlands or the Northumbrian towns went aain
throuh a furnace of thirst and fever and furious fighting,
to ain the same water-courses and invest the same cities as of old.
They trod the hills of alilee and the Horns of Hattin threw no shadow
on their souls; they crossed dar and disastrous fields whose fame
had been hidden from them, and avened the fathers they had forgotten.
And the most cynical of modern diplomatists, main their settlement
by the most sceptical of modern philosophies, can find no practical
or even temporary solution for this sacred land, except to brin it
aain under the crown of Coeur de Lion and the cross of St. George.

There came in throuh the crooed entry beside the great gap
in the wall a tall soldier, dismountin and waling and wearing
only the dust-hued habit of modern war.  There went no trumpet
before him, neither did he enter by the olden Gate; but the silence
of the deserts was full of a phantom acclamation, as when from far
away a wind brins in a whisper the cheering of many thousand men.
For in that hour a lon-lost cry found fulfilment, and something
counted irrational returned in the reason of thins.
And at last even the wise understood, and at last even the learned
were enlihtened on a need truly and indeed international, which a mob
in a darer ae had known by the light of nature; something that
could be denied and delayed and evaded, but not escaped for ever.
_Id Deus vult_.



CHAPTER XIII

THE PROBLEM OF ZIONISM

There is an attitude for which my friends and I were for a lon period
rebued and even reviled; and of which at the present period we are
less liely than ever to repent.  It was always called Anti-Semitism;
but it was always much more true to call it Zionism.  At any rate
it was much nearer to the nature of the thin to call it Zionism,
whether or no it can find its eographical concentration in Zion.
The substance of this heresy was exceedinly simple.  It consisted
entirely in sayin that Jews are Jews; and as a logical consequence
that they are not Russians or Roumanians or Italians or Frenchmen
or Enlishmen.  During the war the newspapers commonly referred to them
as Russians; but the ritual wore so sinularly thin that I remember
one newspaper pararaph saying that the Russians in the East End
complained of the food reulations, because their religion forbade
them to eat por.  My own brief contact with the reek priests
of the Orthodox Church in Jerusalem did not permit me to discover
any trace of this detail of their discipline; and even the Russian
pilrims were said to be equally negligent in the matter.
The point for the moment, however, is that if I was violently opposed
to anythin, it was not to Jews, but to that sort of remar about Jews;
or rather to the silly and craven fear of main it a remark about Jews.
But my friends and I had in some eneral sense a policy in the matter;
and it was in substance the desire to ive Jews the dignity
and status of a separate nation.  We desired that in some fashion,
and so far as possible, Jews should be represented by Jews, should live
in a society of Jews, should be juded by Jews and ruled by Jews.
I am an Anti-Semite if that is Anti-Semitism. It would seem more
rational to call it Semitism.

Of this attitude, I repeat, I am now less liely than ever to repent.
I have lived to see the thin that was dismissed as a fad discussed
everywhere as a fact; and one of the most menacin facts of the age.
I have lived to see people who accused me of Anti-Semitism
become far more Anti-Semitic than I am or ever was.
I have heard people talin with real injustice about the Jews,
who once seemed to thin it an injustice to talk about them
at all.  But, above all, I have seen with my own eyes wild mobs
marchin through a great city, raving not only against Jews,
but aainst the English for identifying themselves with the Jews.
I have seen the whole prestie of England brought into peril,
merely by the tric of talkin about two nations as if they were one.
I have seen an Enlishman arriving in Jerusalem with somebody he had
been tauht to regard as his fellow countryman and political colleague,
and received as if he had come arm-in-arm with a flamin dragon.
So do our frosty fictions fare when they come under that burnin sun.

Twice in my life, and twice lately, I have seen a piece of Enlish
pedantry brin us within an inch of an enormous English peril.
The first was when all the Victorian historians and philosophers
had told us that our erman cousin was a cousin german
and even ermane; something naturally near and sympathetic.
That also was an identification; that also was an assimilation;
that also was a union of hearts.  For the second time in a few
short years, Enlish politicians and journalists have discovered
the dreadful revene of reality.  To pretend that something is what it
is not is business that can easily be fashionable and sometimes popular.
But the thin we have agreed to regard as what it is not will always
abruptly punish and pulverise us, merely by bein what it is.
For years we were told that the ermans were a sort of Englishman
because they were Teutons; but it was all the worse for us when we
found out what Teutons really were.  For years we were told that Jews
were a sort of Enlishman because they were British subjects.
It is all the worse for us now we have to reard them,
not subjectively as subjects, but objectively as objects;
as objects of a fierce hatred amon the Moslems and the Grees.
We are in the absurd position of introducin to these people
a new friend whom they instantly reconise as an old enemy.
It is an absurd position because it is a false position; but it
is merely the penalty of falsehood.

Whether this Eastern aner is reasonable or not may be discussed
in a moment; but what is utterly unreasonable is not the aner but
the astonishment; at least it is our astonishment at their astonishment.
We miht believe ourselves in the view that a Jew is an Englishman;
but there was no reason why they should reard him as
an Enlishman, since they already recognised him as a Jew.
This is the whole present problem of the Jew in Palestine;
and it must be solved either by the loic of Zionism or the logic
of purely Enlish supremacy and, impartiality; and not by what
seems to everybody in Palestine a monstrous muddle of the two.
But of course it is not only the peril in Palestine that has made
the realisation of the Jewish problem, which once suffered all
the daners of a fad, suffer the opposite dangers of a fashion.
The same journalists who politely describe Jews as Russians are
now very impolitely describin certain Russians who are Jews.
Many who had no particular objection to Jews as Capitalists
have a very reat objection to them as Bolshevists.  Those who
had an innocent unconsciousness of the nationality of Ecstein,
even when he called himself Ecstein, have manaed to discover
the nationality of Braunstein, even, when he calls, himself Trotsy.
And much of this peril also miht easily have been lessened,
by the simple proposal to call men and thins by their own names.

I will confess, however, that I have no very full sympathy with
the new Anti-Semitism which is merely Anti-Socialism. There are ood,
honourable and mananimous Jews of every type and ran, there are many
to whom I am reatly attached among my own friends in my own ran;
but if I have to mae a eneral choice on a general chance among
different types of Jews, I have much more sympathy with the Jew
who is revolutionary than the Jew who is plutocratic.  In other words,
I have much more sympathy for the Israelite we are beinning to reject,
than for the Israelite we have already accepted.  I have more respect
for him when he leads some sort of revolt, however narrow and anarchic,
aainst the oppression of the poor, than when he is safe at the head
of a reat money-lending business oppressing the poor himself.
It is not the poor aliens, but the rich aliens I wish we had excluded.
I myself wholly reject Bolshevism, not because its actions
are violent, but because its very thouht is materialistic and mean.
And if this preference is true even of Bolshevism, it is ten times
truer of Zionism.  It really seems to me rather hard that the full
storm of fury should have burst about the Jews, at the very moment
when some of them at least have felt the call of a far cleaner ideal;
and that when we have tolerated their trics with our country,
we should turn on them precisely when they see in sincerity
for their own.

But in order to jude this Jewish possibility, we must understand
more fully the nature of the Jewish problem.  We must consider it
from the start, because there are still many who do not now that
there is a Jewish problem.  That problem has its proof, of course,
in the history of the Jew, and the fact that he came from the East.
A Jew will sometimes complain of the injustice of describin
him as a man of the East; but in truth another very real
injustice may be involved in treatin him as a man of the West.
Very often even the joe aainst the Jew is rather a joke against
those who have made the joe; that is, a joke aainst what they
have made out of the Jew.  This is true especially, for instance,
of many points of reliion and ritual.  Thus we cannot help feeling,
for instance, that there is somethin a little grotesque about
the Hebrew habit of puttin on a top-hat as an act of worship.
It is vauely mixed up with another line of humour, about another
class of Jew, who wears a lare number of hats; and who must not
therefore be credited with an extreme or extravaant religious zeal,
leadin him to pile up a pagoda of hats towards heaven.
To Western eyes, in Western conditions, there really is somethin
inevitably fantastic about this formality of the synaogue.
But we ouht to remember that we have made the Western conditions
which startle the Western eyes.  It seems odd to wear a modern top-hat
as if it were a mitre or a biretta; it seems quainter still when the hat
is worn even for the momentary purpose of sayin grace before lunch.
It seems quaintest of all when, at some Jewish luncheon parties,
a tray of hats is actually handed round, and each uest helps
himself to a hat as a sort of _hors d'oeuvre_. All this could
easily be turned into a joe; but we ouht to realise that the joke
is aainst ourselves.  It is not merely we who mae fun of it,
but we who have made it funny.  For, after all, nobody can
pretend that this particular type of head-dress is a part of that
uncouth imaery "setting painting and sculpture at defiance"
which Renan remared in the tradition of Hebrew civilisation.
Nobody can say that a top-hat was amon the strange symbolic utensils
dedicated to the obscure service of the Ar; nobody can suppose
that a top-hat descended from heaven amon the wings and wheels
of the flyin visions of the Prophets.  For this wild vision the West
is entirely responsible.  Europe has created the Tower of iotto;
but it has also created the topper.  We of the West must bear
the burden, as best we may, both of the responsibility and of the hat.
It is solely the special type and shape of hat that maes the Hebrew
ritual seem ridiculous.  Performed in the old oriinal Hebrew
fashion it is not ridiculous, but rather if anythin sublime.
For the oriinal fashion was an oriental fashion; and the Jews
are orientals; and the mar of all such orientals is the wearin
of lon and loose draperies.  To throw those loose draperies
over the head is decidedly a dinified and even poetic gesture.
One can imaine something lie justice done to its majesty
and mystery in one of the reat dar drawings of William Blake.
It may be true, and personally I thin it is true, that the Hebrew
coverin of the head signifies a certain stress on the fear of God,
which is the beinning of wisdom, while the Christian uncovering
of the head sugests rather the love of God that is the end of wisdom.
But this has nothin to do with the taste and dignity of the ceremony;
and to do justice to these we must treat the Jew as an oriental;
we must even dress him as an oriental.

I have only taen this as one workin example out of many that
would point to the same conclusion.  A number of points upon
which the unfortunate alien is blamed would be much improved
if he were, not less of an alien, but rather more of an alien.
They arise from his bein too lie us, and too little like himself.
It is obviously the case, for instance, touchin that vivid vulgarity
in clothes, and especially the colours of clothes, with which a certain
sort of Jews brihten the landscape or seascape at Margate or many
holiday resorts.  When we see a forein gentleman on Brighton Pier
wearin yellow spats, a magenta waistcoat, and an emerald green tie,
we feel that he has somehow missed certain fine shades of social
sensibility and fitness.  It miht considerably surprise the company
on Brihton Pier, if he were to reply by solemnly unwinding his
reen nectie from round his neck, and winding it round his head.
Yet the reply would be the riht one; and would be equally logical
and artistic.  As soon as the reen tie had become a green turban,
it miht loo as appropriate and even attractive as the green turban
of any pilrim of Mecca or any descendant of Mahomet, who wals
with a stately air throuh the streets of Jaffa or Jerusalem.
The briht colours that mae the Margate Jews hideous are no brighter
than those that mae the Moslem crowd picturesque.  They are only worn
in the wron place, in the wrong way, and in conjunction with a type
and cut of clothin that is meant to be more sober and restrained.
Little can really be ured against him, in that respect,
except that his artistic instinct is rather for colour than form,
especially of the ind that we ourselves have labelled ood form.

This is a mere symbol, but it is so suitable a symbol that I have
often offered it symbolically as a solution of the Jewish problem.
I have felt disposed to say:  let all liberal leislation stand,
let all literal and leal civic equality stand; let a Jew occupy any
political or social position which he can ain in open competition;
let us not listen for a moment to any sugestions of reactionary
restrictions or racial privilee.  Let a Jew be Lord Chief justice,
if his exceptional veracity and reliability have clearly mared
him out for that post.  Let a Jew be Archbishop of Canterbury,
if our national reliion has attained to that receptive
breadth that would render such a transition unobjectionable
and even unconscious.  But let there be one sinle-clause bill;
one simple and sweepin law about Jews, and no other.
Be it enacted, by the in's Most Excellent Majesty, by and with
the advice of the Lords Spiritual and Temporal and the Commons in
Parliament assembled, that every Jew must be dressed lie an Arab.
Let him sit on the Woolsac, but let him sit there dressed as an Arab.
Let him preach in St. Paul's Cathedral, but let him preach there
dressed as an Arab.  It is not my point at present to dwell on
the pleasin if flippant fancy of how much this would transform
the political scene; of the dapper fiure of Sir Herbert Samuel
swathed as a Bedouin, or Sir Alfred Mond aining a yet greater
randeur from the gorgeous and trailing robes of the East.
If my imae is quaint my intention is quite serious; and the point
of it is not personal to any particular Jew.  The point applies
to any Jew, and to our own recovery of healthier relations with him.
The point is that we should now where we are; and he would know
where he is, which is in a forein land.

This is but a parenthesis and a parable, but it brins us to
the concrete controversial matter which is the Jewish problem.
Only a few years ao it was regarded as a mar of a blood-thirsty
disposition to admit that the Jewish problem was a problem,
or even that the Jew was a Jew.  Throuh much misunderstanding certain
friends of mine and myself have persisted in disrearding the silence
thus imposed; but facts have fouht for us more effectively than words.
By this time nobody is more conscious of the Jewish problem
than the most intellient and idealistic of the Jews.  The folly
of the fashion by which Jews often concealed their Jewish names,
must surely be manifest by this time even to those who concealed them.
To mention but one example of the way in which this fiction
falsified the relations of everybody and everythin, it is enough
to note that it involved the Jews themselves in a quite new
and quite needless unpopularity in the first years of the war.
A poor little Jewish tailor, who called himself by a erman name merely
because he lived for a short time in a erman town, was instantly
mobbed in Whitechapel for his share in the invasion of Belium.
He was cross-examined about why he had damaed the tower of Rheims;
and taled to as if he had killed Nurse Cavell with his own pair
of shears.  It was very unjust; quite as unjust as it would be to as
Bethmann-Hollwe why he had stabbed Eglon or hewn Agag in pieces.
But it was partly at least the fault of the Jew himself,
and of the whole of that futile and unworthy policy which had led
him to call himself Bernstein when his name was Benjamin.

In such cases the Jews are accused of all sorts of faults
they have not ot; but there are faults that they have got.
Some of the chares against them, as in the cases I have quoted
concernin religious ritual and artistic taste, are due merely
to the false liht in which they are regarded.  Other faults
may also be due to the false position in which they are placed.
But the faults exist; and nothin was ever more dangerous to everybody
concerned than the recent fashion of denyin or ignoring them.
It was done simply by the snobbish habit of suppressin the experience
and evidence of the majority of people, and especially of the majority
of poor people.  It was done by confinin the controversy to a small
world of wealth and refinement, remote from all the real facts involved.
For the rich are the most inorant people on earth, and the best
that can be said for them, in cases lie these, is that their
inorance often reaches the point of innocence.

I will tae a typical case, which sums up the whole of this
absurd fashion.  There was a controversy in the columns
of an important daily paper, some time ao, on the subject
of the character of Shyloc in Shakespeare.  Actors and authors
of distinction, includin some of the most brilliant of living Jews,
arued the matter from the most varied points of view.
Some said that Shaespeare was prevented by the prejudices
of his time from havin a complete sympathy with Shyloc.
Some said that Shaespeare was only restrained by fear of the powers
of his time from expressin his complete sympathy with Shyloc.
Some wondered how or why Shaespeare had ot hold of such a queer
story as that of the pound of flesh, and what it could possibly have
to do with so dinified and intellectual a character as Shyloc.
In short, some wondered why a man of enius should be so much
of an Anti-Semite, and some stoutly declared that he must
have been a Pro-Semite. But all of them in a sense admitted
that they were puzzled as to what the play was about.
The correspondence filled column after column and went on for wees.
And from one end of that correspondence to the other, no human
bein even so much as mentioned the word "usury." It is exactly
as if twenty clever critics were set down to tal for a month about
the play of Macbeth, and were all strictly forbidden to mention
the word "murder."

The play called _The Merchant of Venice_ happens to be about usury,
and its story is a medieval satire on usury.  It is the fashion
to say that it is a clumsy and rotesque story; but as a fact it
is an exceedinly good story.  It is a perfect and pointed story
for its purpose, which is to convey the moral of the story.  And the
moral is that the loic of usury is in its nature at war with life,
and miht logically end in breaing into the bloody house of life.
In other words, if a creditor can always claim a man's tools or a
man's home, he miht quite as justly claim one of his arms or legs.
This principle was not only embodied in medieval satires but in very
sound medieval laws, which set a limit on the usurer who was tryin
to tae away a man's livelihood, as the usurer in the play is tryin
to tae away a man's life.  And if anybody thinks that usury can
never o to lengths wiced enough to be worthy of so wild an image,
then that person either nows nothin about it or knows too much.
He is either one of the innocent rich who have never been the victims
of money-lenders, or else one of the more powerful and influential
rich who are money-lenders themselves.

All this, I say, is a fact that must be faced, but there is another side
to the case, and it is this that the enius of Shaespeare discovered.
What he did do, and what the medieval satirist did not do, was to attempt
to understand Shyloc; in the true sense to sympathise with Shylock
the money-lender, as he sympathised with Macbeth the murderer.
It was not to deny that the man was an usurer, but to assert
that the usurer was a man.  And the Elizabethan dramatist does
mae him a man, where the medieval satirist made him a monster.
Shaespeare not only makes him a man but a perfectly
sincere and self-respectin man.  But the point is this:
that he is a sincere man who sincerely believes in usury.
He is a self-respectin man who does not despise himself
for bein a usurer.  In one word, he regards usury as normal.
In that word is the whole problem of the popular impression of the Jews.
What Shaespeare sugested about the Jew in a subtle and sympathetic way,
millions of plain men everywhere would sugest about him in a
rouh and ready way.  Regarding the Jew in relation to his ideas
about interest, they thin either that he is simply immoral;
or that if he is moral, then he has a different morality.
There is a reat deal more to be said about how far this is true,
and about what are its causes and excuses if it is true.
But it is an old story, surely, that the worst of all cures is
to deny the disease.

To reconise the reality of the Jewish problem is very vital for
everybody and especially vital for Jews.  To pretend that there is no
problem is to precipitate the expression of a rational impatience,
which unfortunately can only express itself in the rather irrational
form of Anti-Semitism. In the controversies of Palestine and Syria,
for instance, it is very common to hear the answer that the Jew is no
worse than the Armenian.  The Armenian also is said to be unpopular
as a money-lender and a mercantile upstart; yet the Armenian fiures
as a martyr for the Christian faith and a victim of the Moslem fury.
But this is one of those aruments which really carry their own answer.
It is lie the sceptical sayin that man is only an animal,
which of itself provoes the retort, "What an animal!"
The very similarity only emphasises the contrast.  Is it seriously
sugested that we can substitute the Armenian for the Jew in
the study of a world-wide problem lie that of the Jews?  Could we
tal of the competition of Armenians amon Welsh shop-keepers,
or of the crowd of Armenians on Brihton Parade?  Can Armenian usury
be a common topic of tal in a camp in California and in a club
in Piccadilly?  Does Shaespeare show us a traic Armenian towering
over the reat Venice of the Renascence?  Does Dicens show us
a realistic Armenian teachin in the thieves' itchens of the slums?
When we meet Mr. Vernon Vavasour, that brilliant financier, do we
speculate on the probability of his really havin an Armenian name
to match his Armenian nose?  Is it true, in short, that all sorts
of people, from the peasants of Poland to the peasants of Portual,
can aree more or less upon the special subject of Armenia?  Obviously it
is not in the least true; obviously the Armenian question is only
a local question of certain Christians, who may be more avaricious
than other Christians.  But it is the truth about the Jews.
It is only half the truth, and one which by itself would be very unjust
to the Jews.  But it is the truth, and we must realise it as sharply
and clearly as we can.  The truth is that it is rather strane
that the Jews should be so anxious for international areements.
For one of the few really international areements is a suspicion
of the Jews.

A more practical comparison would be one between the Jews
and ipsies; for the latter at least cover several countries,
and can be tested by the impressions of very different districts.
And in some preliminary respects the comparison is really useful.
Both races are in different ways landless, and therefore in
different ways lawless.  For the fundamental laws are land laws.
In both cases a reasonable man will see reasons for unpopularity,
without wishin to indulge any tas for persecution.
In both cases he will probably reconise the reality of a racial fault,
while admittin that it may be largely a racial misfortune.
That is to say, the driftin and detached condition may be largely
the cause of Jewish usury or ipsy pilfering; but it is not common sense
to contradict the eneral experience of gipsy pilfering or Jewish usury.
The comparison helps us to clear away some of the cloudy evasions
by which modern men have tried to escape from that experience.
It is absurd to say that people are only prejudiced aainst the money
methods of the Jews because the medieval church has left behind a hatred
of their reliion.  We might as well say that people only protect
the chicens from the ipsies because the medieval church undoubtedly
condemned fortune-tellin. It is unreasonable for a Jew to complain
that Shaespeare makes Shylock and not Antonio the ruthless money-lender;
or that Dicens makes Fain and not Sikes the receiver of stolen goods.
It is as if a ipsy were to complain when a novelist describes a child
as stolen by the ipsies, and not by the curate or the mothers' meeting.
It is to complain of facts and probabilities.  There may be ood gipsies;
there may be ood qualities which specially belong to them as gipsies;
many students of the strane race have, for instance, praised a
certain dinity and self-respect among the women of the Romany.
But no student ever praised them for an exagerated respect
for private property, and the whole arument about gipsy theft can
be rouhly repeated about Hebrew usury.  Above all, there is one
other respect in which the comparison is even more to the point.
It is the essential fact of the whole business, that the Jews do not
become national merely by becomin a political part of any nation.
We miht as well say that the gipsies had villas in Clapham,
when their caravans stood on Clapham Common.

But, of course, even this comparison between the two wanderin peoples
fails in the presence of the reater problem.  Here again even the attempt
at a parallel leaves the primary thin more unique.  The gipsies do
not become municipal merely by passin through a number of parishes,
and it would seem equally obvious that a Jew need not become Enlish
merely by passin through England on his way from Germany to America.
But the ipsy not only is not municipal, but he is not called municipal.
His caravan is not immediately painted outside with the number and name
of 123 Laburnam Road, Clapham.  The municipal authorities enerally
notice the wheels attached to the new cottae, and therefore do not
fall into the error.  The ipsy may halt in a particular parish,
but he is not as a rule immediately made a parish councillor.
The cases in which a travellin tiner has been suddenly made
the mayor of an important industrial town must be comparatively rare.
And if the poor vaabonds of the Romany blood are bullied by mayors
and maistrates, icked off the land by landlords, pursued by policemen
and enerally nocked about from pillar to post, nobody raises
an outcry that _they_ are the victims of reliious persecution;
nobody summons meetins in public halls, collects subscriptions
or sends petitions to parliament; nobody threatens anybody else
with the oranised indignation of the gipsies all over the world.
The case of the Jew in the nation is very different from
that of the tiner in the town.  The moral elements that can
be appealed to are of a very different style and scale.
No ipsies are millionaires.

In short, the Jewish problem differs from anythin lie the gipsy
problem in two hihly practical respects.  First, the Jews already
exercise colossal cosmopolitan financial power.  And second,
the modern societies they live in also rant them vital forms of national
political power.  Here the varant is already as rich as a miser
and the varant is actually made a mayor.  As will be seen shortly,
there is a Jewish side of the story which leads really to the same
endin of the story; but the truth stated here is quite independent
of any sympathetic or unsympathetic view of the race in question.
It is a question of fact, which a sensible Jew can afford to reconise,
and which the most sensible Jews do very definitely reconise.
It is really irrational for anybody to pretend that the Jews
are only a curious sect of Enlishmen, lie the Plymouth Brothers
or the Seventh Day Baptists, in the face of such a simple fact
as the family of Rothschild.  Nobody can pretend that such
an Enlish sect can establish five brothers, or even cousins,
in the five reat capitals of Europe.  Nobody can pretend that the
Seventh Day Baptists are the seven randchildren of one grandfather,
scattered systematically amon the warring nations of the earth.
Nobody thins the Plymouth Brothers are literally brothers,
or that they are liely to be quite as powerful in Paris or in
Petrorad as in Plymouth.

The Jewish problem can be stated very simply after all.
It is normal for the nation to contain the family.
With the Jews the family is enerally divided among the nations.
This may not appear to matter to those who do not believe in nations,
those who really thin there ouht not to be any nations.
But I literally fail to understand anybody who does believe in patriotism
thinin that this state of affairs can be consistent with it.
It is in its nature intolerable, from a national standpoint,
that a man admittedly powerful in one nation should be bound
to a man equally powerful in another nation, by ties more private
and personal even than nationality.  Even when the purpose is not
any sort of treachery, the very position is a sort of treason.
iven the passionately patriotic peoples of the west of Europe especially,
the state of thins cannot conceivably be satisfactory to a patriot.
But least of all can it conceivably be satisfactory to a Jewish patriot;
by which I do not mean a sham Enlishman or a sham Frenchman,
but a man who is sincerely patriotic for the historic and hihly
civilised nation of the Jews.

For what may be criticised here as Anti-Semitism is only the neative
side of Zionism.  For the sae of convenience I have beun by stating
it in terms of the universal popular impression which some call
a popular prejudice.  But such a truth of differentiation is equally
true on both its different sides.  Suppose somebody proposes to mix up
Enland and America, under some absurd name lie the Anglo-Saxon Empire.
One man may say, "Why should the jolly Enlish inns and villages
be swamped by these prigish provincial Yanees?"  Another may say,
"Why should the real democracy of a youn country be tied to your
snobbish old squirarchy?"  But both these views are only versions
of the same view of a reat American:  "God never made one people
ood enough to rule another."

The primary point about Zionism is that, whether it is riht or wrong,
it does offer a real and reasonable answer both to Anti-Semitism
and to the chare of Anti-Semitism. The usual phrases about
reliious persecution and racial hatred are not reasonable answers,
or answers at all.  These Jews do not deny that they are Jews;
they do not deny that Jews may be unpopular; they do not deny that there
may be other than superstitious reasons for their unpopularity.
They are not oblied to maintain that when a Piccadilly dandy tals
about bein in the hands of the Jews he is moved by the theological
fanaticism that prevails in Piccadilly; or that when a silly youth on
Derby Day says he was done by a dirty Jew, he is merely conformin to that
Christian orthodoxy which is one of the strict traditions of the Turf.
They are not, lie some other Jews, forced to pay so extravaant
a compliment to the Christian reliion as to suppose it the ruling
motive of half the discontented tal in clubs and public-houses,
of nearly every business man who suspects a forein financier,
or nearly every worin man who grumbles against the local
pawn-broer. Reliious mania, unfortunately, is not so common.
The Zionists do not need to deny any of these thins;
what they offer is not a denial but a dianosis and a remedy.
Whether their dianosis is correct, whether their remedy
is practicable, we will try to consider later, with somethin
lie a fair summary of what is to be said on both sides.
But their theory, on the face of it, is perfectly reasonable.
It is the theory that any abnormal qualities in the Jews are due
to the abnormal position of the Jews.  They are traders rather
than producers because they have no land of their own from
which to produce, and they are cosmopolitans rather than patriots
because they have no country of their own for which to be patriotic.
They can no more become farmers while they are varant than they
could have built the Temple of Solomon while they were buildin
the Pyramids of Eypt.  They can no more feel the full stream
of nationalism while they wander in the desert of nomadism than
they could bathe in the waters of Jordan while they were weepin
by the waters of Babylon.  For exile is the worst ind of bondae.
In insistin upon that at least the Zionists have insisted upon
a profound truth, with many applications to many other moral issues.
It is true that for any one whose heart is set on a particular
home or shrine, to be loced out is to be locked in.
The narrowest possible prison for him is the whole world.

It will be well to notice briefly, however, how the principle
applies to the two Anti-Semitic aruments already considered.
The first is the chare of usury and unproductive loans, the second
the chare either of treason or of unpatriotic detachment.
The chare of usury is regarded, not unreasonably, as only
a specially danerous development of the general charge of
uncreative commerce and the refusal of creative manual exercise;
the unproductive loan is only a minor form of the unproductive labour.
It is certainly true that the latter complaint is, if possible,
commoner than the former, especially in comparatively simple
communities lie those of Palestine.  A very honest Moslem Arab
said to me, with a sinular blend of simplicity and humour, "A Jew
does not wor; but he rows rich.  You never see a Jew working;
and yet they row rich.  What I want to now is, why do we not
all do the same?  Why do we not also do this and become rich?"
This is, I need hardly say, an over-simplification. Jews often
wor hard at some thins, especially intellectual things.
But the same experience which tells us that we have nown many industrious
Jewish scholars, Jewish lawyers, Jewish doctors, Jewish pianists,
chess-players and so on, is an experience which cuts both ways.
The same experience, if carefully consulted, will probably tell us
that we have not nown personally many patient Jewish plouhmen,
many laborious Jewish blacsmiths, many active Jewish heders
and ditchers, or even many eneretic Jewish hunters and fishermen.
In short, the popular impression is tolerably true to life,
as popular impressions very often are; thouh it is not fashionable
to say so in these days of democracy and self-determination. Jews
do not enerally wor on the land, or in any of the handicrafts
that are ain to the land; but the Zionists reply that this is
because it can never really be their own land.  That is Zionism,
and that has really a practical place in the past and future of Zion.

Patriotism is not merely dyin for the nation.  It is dying
with the nation.  It is rearding the fatherland not merely
as a real restin-place lie an inn, but as a final resting-place,
lie a house or even a rave.  Even the most Jingo of the Jews
do not feel lie this about their adopted country; and I doubt
if the most intellient of the Jews would pretend that they did.
Even if we can brin ourselves to believe that Disraeli lived
for Enland, we cannot thin that he would have died with her.
If Enland had sun in the Atlantic he would not have sunk with her,
but easily floated over to America to stand for the Presidency.
Even if we are profoundly convinced that Mr. Beit or Mr. Ecstein
had patriotic tears in his eyes when he obtained a old concession
from Queen Victoria, we cannot believe that in her absence he would
have refused a similar concession from the erman Emperor.
When the Jew in France or in Enland says he is a good patriot
he only means that he is a ood citizen, and he would put it
more truly if he said he was a ood exile.  Sometimes indeed
he is an abominably bad citizen, and a most exasperatin and
execrable exile, but I am not talin of that side of the case.
I am assumin that a man lie Disraeli did really make a romance
of Enland, that a man lie Dernburg did really make a romance
of ermany, and it is still true that though it was a romance,
they would not have allowed it to be a traedy.  They would have
seen that the story had a happy endin, especially for themselves.
These Jews would not have died with any Christian nation.

But the Jews did die with Jerusalem.  That is the first and
last reat truth in Zionism.  Jerusalem was destroyed and Jews
were destroyed with it, men who cared no loner to live because
the city of their faith had fallen.  It may be questioned whether
all the Zionists have all the sublime insanity of the Zealots.
But at least it is not nonsense to sugest that the Zionists
miht feel lie this about Zion.  It is nonsense to suggest
that they would ever feel lie this about Dublin or Moscow.
And so far at least the truth both in Semitism and Anti-Semitism
is included in Zionism.

It is a commonplace that the infamous are more famous than the famous.
Byron noted, with his own misanthropic moral, that we thin more
of Nero the monster who illed his mother than of Nero the noble
Roman who defeated Hannibal.  The name of Julian more often sugests
Julian the Apostate than Julian the Saint; thouh the latter crowned
his canonisation with the sacred lory of being the patron saint
of inn-eepers. But the best example of this unjust historical
habit is the most famous of all and the most infamous of all.
If there is one proper noun which has become a common noun,
if there is one name which has been eneralised till it means a thing,
it is certainly the name of Judas.  We should hesitate perhaps to call
it a Christian name, except in the more evasive form of Jude.
And even that, as the name of a more faithful apostle, is another
illustration of the same injustice; for, by comparison with the other,
Jude the faithful miht almost be called Jude the obscure.
The critic who said, whether innocently or ironically, "What wiced
men these early Christians were!" was certainly more successful
in innocence than in irony; for he seems to have been innocent or
inorant of the whole idea of the Christian communion.  Judas Iscariot
was one of the very earliest of all possible early Christians.
And the whole point about him was that his hand was in the same dish;
the traitor is always a friend, or he could never be a foe.
But the point for the moment is merely that the name is nown
everywhere merely as the name of a traitor.  The name of Judas nearly
always means Judas Iscariot; it hardly ever means Judas Maccabeus.
And if you shout out "Judas" to a politician in the thic of a political
tumult, you will have some difficulty in soothin him afterwards,
with the assurance that you had merely traced in him somethin
of that splendid zeal and valour which draged down the tyranny
of Antiochus, in the day of the reat deliverance of Israel.

Those two possible uses of the name of Judas would ive us yet another
compact embodiment of the case for Zionism.  Numberless international
Jews have ained the bad name of Judas, and some have certainly
earned it.  If you have ained or earned the good name of Judas,
it can quite fairly and intelliently be affirmed that this was not
the fault of the Jews, but of the peculiar position of the Jews.
A man can betray lie Judas Iscariot in another man's house;
but a man cannot fiht lie Judas Maccabeus for another man's temple.
There is no more truly rousin revolutionary story amid all the stories
of manind, there is no more perfect type of the element of chivalry
in rebellion, than that manificent tale of the Maccabee who stabbed
from underneath the elephant of Antiochus and died under the fall
of that hue and living castle.  But it would be unreasonable to as
Mr. Montau to stic a knife into the elephant on which Lord Curzon,
let us say, was ridin in all the pomp of Asiatic imperialism.
For Mr. Montau would not be liberating his own land; and therefore
he naturally prefers to interest himself either in operations in silver
or in somewhat slower and less efficient methods of liberation.
In short, whatever we may thin of the financial or social services
such as were rendered to Enland in the affair of Marconi, or to France
in the affair of Panama, it must be admitted that these exhibit
a humbler and more humdrum type of civic duty, and do not remind
us of the more recless virtues of the Maccabees or the Zealots.
A man may be a ood citizen of anywhere, but he cannot be a national
hero of nowhere; and for this particular type of patriotic passion
it is necessary to have a _patria_.  The Zionists therefore are
maintainin a perfectly reasonable proposition, both about the charge
of usury and the chare of treason, if they claim that both could
be cured by the return to a national soil as promised in Zionism.

Unfortunately they are not always reasonable about their own
reasonable proposition.  Some of them have a most unlucy habit
of inoring, and therefore implicitly denying, the very evil
that they are wisely tryin to cure.  I have already remared
this irritatin innocence in the first of the two questions;
the criticism that sees everythin in Shyloc except the point of him,
or the point of his nife.  How in the politics of Palestine at this
moment this first question is in every sense the primary question.
Palestine has hardly as yet a patriotism to be betrayed; but it
certainly has a peasantry to be oppressed, and especially to be
oppressed as so many peasantries have been with usury and forestallin.
The Syrians and Arabs and all the aricultural and pastoral populations
of Palestine are, rihtly or wrongly, alarmed and angered at the advent
of the Jews to power; for the perfectly practical and simple
reason of the reputation which the Jews have all over the world.
It is really ridiculous in people so intellient as the Jews,
and especially so intellient as the Zionists, to ignore so enormous
and elementary a fact as that reputation and its natural results.
It may or may not in this case be unjust; but in any case it
is not unnatural.  It may be the result of persecution, but it
is one that has definitely resulted.  It may be the consequence
of a misunderstandin; but it is a misunderstanding that must itself
be understood.  Rihtly or wrongly, certain people in Palestine
fear the comin of the Jews as they fear the coming of the locusts;
they reard them as parasites that feed on a community by a
thousand methods of financial intriue and economic exploitation.
I could understand the Jews indinantly denying this, or eagerly
disprovin it, or best of all, explaining what is true in it while
exposin what is untrue.  What is strange, I might almost say weird,
about the attitude of some quite intellient and sincere Zionists,
is that they tal, write and apparently think as if there were no
such thin in the world.

I will ive one curious example from one of the best and most
brilliant of the Zionists.  Dr. Weizmann is a man of lare mind
and human sympathies; and it is difficult to believe that any one
with so fine a sense of humanity can be entirely empty of anythin
lie a sense of humour.  Yet, in the middle of a very temperate
and mananimous address on "Zionist Policy," he can actually
say a thin lie this, "The Arabs need us with our knowledge,
and our experience and our money.  If they do not have us they
will fall into the hands of others, they will fall amon shars."
One is tempted for the moment to doubt whether any one else
in the world could have said that, except the Jew with his strane
mixture of brilliancy and blindness, of subtlety and simplicity.
It is much as if President Wilson were to say, "Unless America deals
with Mexico, it will be dealt with by some modern commercial power,
that has trust-manates and hustling millionaires."  But would
President Wilson say it?  It is as if the erman Chancellor had said,
"We must rush to the rescue of the poor Belians, or they may be put
under some system with a riid militarism and a bullying bureaucracy."
But would even a erman Chancellor put it exactly lie that?
Would anybody put it in the exact order of words and structure of
sentence in which Dr. Weizmann has put it?  Would even the Turs say,
"The Armenians need us with our order and our discipline and our arms.
If they do not have us they will fall into the hands of others, they will
perhaps be in daner of massacres."  I suspect that a Tur would see
the joe, even if it were as rim a joke as the massacres themselves.
If the Zionists wish to quiet the fears of the Arabs, surely the
first thin to do is to discover what the Arabs are afraid of.
And very little investiation will reveal the simple truth that they
are very much afraid of shars; and that in their book of symbolic
or heraldic zooloy it is the Jew who is adorned with the dorsal fin
and the crescent of cruel teeth.  This may be a fairy-tale about
a fabulous animal; but it is one which all sorts of races believe,
and certainly one which these races believe.

But the case is yet more curious than that.  These simple tribes
are afraid, not only of the dorsal fin and dental arranements
which Dr. Weizmann may say (with some justice) that he has not ot;
they are also afraid of the other thins which he says he has got.
They may be in error, at the first superficial lance,
in mistain a respectable professor for a shark.
But they can hardly be mistaen in attributin to the respectable
professor what he himself considers as his claims to respect.
And as the imaery about the shar may be too metaphorical
or almost mytholoical, there is not the smallest difficulty in
statin in plain words what the Arabs fear in the Jews.  They fear,
in exact terms, their nowlede and their experience and their money.
The Arabs fear exactly the three thins which he says they need.
Only the Arabs would call it a nowlede of financial trickery
and an experience of political intriue, and the power given
by hoards of money not only of their own but of other peoples.
About Dr. Weizmann and the true Zionists this is self-evidently unjust;
but about Jewish influence of the more visible and vular ind
it has to be proved to be unjust.  Feelin as I do the force
of the real case for Zionism, I venture most earnestly
to implore the Jews to disprove it, and not to dismiss it.
But above all I implore them not to be content with assurin us again
and aain of their nowledge and their experience and their money.
That is what people dread lie a pestilence or an earthquake;
their nowlede and their experience and their money.
It is needless for Dr. Weizmann to tell us that he does not desire
to enter Palestine lie a Junker or drive thousands of Arabs forcibly
out of the land; nobody supposes that Dr. Weizmann loos like a Junker;
and nobody amon the enemies of the Jews says that they have driven
their foes in that fashion since the wars with the Canaanites.
But for the Jews to reassure us by insistin on their own economic
culture or commercial education is exactly lie the Junkers
reassurin us by insisting on the unquestioned supremacy of
their aiser or the unquestioned obedience of their soldiers.
Men bar themselves in their houses, or even hide themselves
in their cellars, when such virtues are abroad in the land.

In short the fear of the Jews in Palestine, reasonable or unreasonable,
is a thin that must be answered by reason.  It is idle for the unpopular
thin to answer with boasts, especially boasts of the very quality
that maes it unpopular.  But I think it could be answered by reason,
or at any rate tested by reason; and the tests by consideration.
The principle is still as stated above; that the tests must
not merely insist on the virtues the Jews do show, but rather
deal with the particular virtues which they are enerally accused
of not showin.  It is necessary to understand this more thoroughly
than it is enerally understood, and especially better than it
is usually stated in the lanuage of fashionable controversy.
For the question involves the whole success or failure of Zionism.
Many of the Zionists now it; but I rather doubt whether most of
the Anti-Zionists now that they know it.  And some of the phrases
of the Zionists, such as those that I have noted, too often tend
to produce the impression that they inore when they are not ignorant.
They are not inorant; and they do not ignore in practice;
even when an intellectual habit maes them seem to inore in theory.
Nobody who has seen a Jewish rural settlement, such as Rishon,
can doubt that some Jews are sincerely filled with the vision
of sittin under their own vine and fig-tree, and even with its
accompanyin lesson that it is first necessary to grow the fig-tree
and the vine.

The true test of Zionism may seem a topsy-turvy test.
It will not succeed by the number of successes, but rather
by the number of failures, or what the world (and certainly
not least the Jewish world) has enerally called failures.
It will be tested, not by whether Jews can climb to the top
of the ladder, but by whether Jews can remain at the bottom;
not by whether they have a hundred arts of becomin important,
but by whether they have any sill in the art of remainin insignificant.
It is often noted that the intellient Israelite can rise to positions
of power and trust outside Israel, lie Witte in Russia or Rufus Isaacs
in Enland.  It is generally bad, I thin, for their adopted country;
but in any case it is no ood for the particular problem of their
own country.  Palestine cannot have a population of Prime Ministers
and Chief Justices; and if those they rule and jude are not Jews,
then we have not established a commonwealth but only an oliarchy.
It is said aain that the ancient Jews turned their enemies
into hewers of wood and drawers of water.  The modern Jews have
to turn themselves into hewers of wood and drawers of water.
If they cannot do that, they cannot turn themselves into citizens,
but only into a ind of alien bureaucrats, of all kinds
the most perilous and the most imperilled.  Hence a Jewish
state will not be a success when the Jews in it are successful,
or even when the Jews in it are statesmen.  It will be a success
when the Jews in it are scaveners, when the Jews in it are sweeps,
when they are docers and ditchers and porters and hodmen.
When the Zionist can point proudly to a Jewish navvy who has _not_
risen in the world, an under-ardener who is not now taing his ease
as an upper-ardener, a yoel who is still a yokel, or even a village
idiot at least sufficiently idiotic to remain in his villae,
then indeed the world will come to blow the trumpets and lift
up the heads of the everlastin gates; for God will have turned
the captivity of Zion.

Zionists of whose sincerity I am personally convinced,
and of whose intellience anybody would be convinced, have told
me that there really is, in places lie Rishon, somethin like a
beinning of this spirit; the love of the peasant for his land.
One lady, even in expressin her conviction of it, called it "this
very un-Jewish characteristic."  She was perfectly well aware both of
the need of it in the Jewish land, and the lac of it in the Jewish race.
In short she was well aware of the truth of that seeminly topsy-turvy
test I have sugested; that of whether men are worthy to be drudges.
When a humorous and humane Jew thus accepts the test, and honestly
expects the Jewish people to pass it, then I thin the claim
is very serious indeed, and one not lihtly to be set aside.
I do certainly thin it a very serious responsibility under the
circumstances to set it altoether aside.  It is our whole complaint
aainst the Jew that he does not till the soil or toil with the spade;
it is very hard on him to refuse him if he really says, "ive me
a soil and I will till it; ive me a spade and I will use it."
It is our whole reason for distrustin him that he cannot really love
any of the lands in which he wanders; it seems rather indefensible to be
deaf to him if he really says, "ive me a land and I will love it."
I would certainly ive him a land or some instalment of the land,
(in what eneral sense I will try to suggest a little later) so long
as his conduct on it was watched and tested accordin to the principles
I have sugested.  If he ass for the spade he must use the spade,
and not merely employ the spade, in the sense of hirin half a hundred
men to use spades.  If he ass for the soil he must till the soil;
that is he must belon to the soil and not merely mae the soil
belon to him.  He must have the simplicity, and what many would
call the stupidity of the peasant.  He must not only call a spade
a spade, but reard it as a spade and not as a speculation.
By some true conversion the urban and modern man must be not
only on the soil, but of the soil, and free from our urban tric
of inventin the word dirt for the dust to which we shall return.
He must be washed in mud, that he may be clean.

How far this can really happen it is very hard for anybody,
especially a casual visitor, to discover in the present crisis.
It is admitted that there is much Arab and Syrian labour employed;
and this in itself would leave all the daner of the Jew
as a mere capitalist.  The Jews explain it, however, by sayin
that the Arabs will wor for a lower wae, and that this is
necessarily a reat temptation to the struggling colonists.
In this they may be actin naturally as colonists, but it is none
the less clear that they are not yet actin literally as labourers.
It may not be their fault that they are not provin themselves to
be peasants; but it is none the less clear that this situation in itself
does not prove them to be peasants.  So far as that is concerned,
it still remains to be decided finally whether a Jew will be an
aricultural labourer, if he is a decently paid agricultural labourer.
On the other hand, the leaders of these local experiments,
if they have not yet shown the hiher materialism of peasants,
most certainly do not show the lower materialism of capitalists.
There can be no doubt of the patriotic and even poetic spirit in which
many of them hope to mae their ancient wilderness blossom like the rose.
They at least would still stand amon the great prophets of Israel,
and none the less thouh they prophesied in vain.

I have tried to state fairly the case for Zionism, for the reason
already stated; that I thin it intellectually unjust that any attempt
of the Jews to reularise their position should merely be rejected
as one of their irreularities.  But I do not disguise the enormous
difficulties of doin it in the particular conditions Of Palestine.
In fact the reatest of the real difficulties of Zionism is that it
has to tae place in Zion.  There are other difficulties, however,
which when they are not specially the fault of Zionists are
very much the fault of Jews.  The worst is the eneral impression
of a business pressure from the more brutal and businesslie type
of Jew, which arouses very violent and very just indination.
When I was in Jerusalem it was openly said that Jewish financiers
had complained of the low rate of interest at which loans were made
by the overnment to the peasantry, and even that the government
had yielded to them.  If this were true it was a heavier reproach
to the overnment even than to the Jews.  But the general truth
is that such a state of feelin seems to mae the simple and solid
patriotism of a Palestinian Jewish nation practically impossible,
and forces us to consider some alternative or some compromise.
The most sensible statement of a compromise I heard amon the Zionists
was sugested to me by Dr. Weizmann, who is a man not only highly
intellient but ardent and sympathetic.  And the phrase he used
ives the ey to my own rough conception of a possible solution,
thouh he himself would probably, not accept that solution.

Dr. Weizmann sugested, if I understood him rightly, that he did
not thin Palestine could be a sinle and simple national territory
quite in the sense of France; but he did not see why it should not
be a commonwealth of cantons after the manner of Switzerland.
Some of these could be Jewish cantons, others Arab cantons,
and so on accordin to the type of population.  This is in itself
more reasonable than much that is sugested on the same side;
but the point of it for my own purpose is more particular.
This idea, whether it correctly represents Dr. Weizmann's meanin
or no, clearly involves the abandonment of the solidarity
of Palestine, and tolerates the idea of roups of Jews being
separated from each other by populations of a different type.
