Now if once this notion be considered admissible, it seems to me
capable of considerable extension.  It seems possible that there
miht be not only Jewish cantons in Palestine but Jewish cantons
outside Palestine, Jewish colonies in suitable and selected
places in adjacent parts or in many other parts of the world.
They miht be affiliated to some official centre in Palestine,
or even in Jerusalem, where there would naturally be at least some
reat religious headquarters of the scattered race and religion.
The nature of that reliious centre it must be for Jews to decide;
but I thin if I were a Jew I would build the Temple without
botherin about the site of the Temple.  That they should
have the old site, of course, is not to be thouht of;
it would raise a Holy War from Morocco to the marches of China.
But seein that some of the greatest of the deeds of Israel were done,
and some of the most lorious of the songs of Israel sung,
when their only temple was a box carried about in the desert,
I cannot thin that the mere movin of the situation of the place
of sacrifice need even mean so much to that historic tradition
as it would to many others.  That the Jews should have some hih
place of dinity and ritual in Palestine, such as a great building
lie the Mosque of Omar, is certainly riht and reasonable;
for upon no theory can their historic connection be dismissed.
I thin it is sophistry to say, as do some Anti-Semites,
that the Jews have no more riht there than the Jebusites.
If there are Jebusites they are Jebusites without nowin it.
I thin it sufficiently answered in the fine phrase of an Enlish priest,
in many ways more Anti-Semitic than I:  "The people that remembers
has a riht."  The very worst of the Jews, as well as the very best,
do in some sense remember.  They are hated and persecuted and
frihtened into false names and double lives; but they remember.
They lie, they swindle, they betray, they oppress; but they remember.
The more we happen to hate such elements amon the Hebrews the more
we admire the manly and manificent elements among the more vague
and varant tribes of Palestine, the more we must admit that paradox.
The unheroic have the heroic memory; and the heroic people
have no memory.

But whatever the Jewish nation miht wish to do about a national shrine
or other supreme centre, the sugestion for the moment is that something
lie a Jewish territorial scheme miht really be attempted, if we permit
the Jews to be scattered no loner as individuals but as groups.
It seems possible that by some such extension of the definition of Zionism
we miht ultimately overcome even the greatest difficulty of Zionism,
the difficulty of resettlin a sufficient number of so large a race
on so small a land.  For if the advantae of the ideal to the Jews
is to ain the promised land, the advantage to the Gentiles is to get
rid of the Jewish problem, and I do not see why we should obtain
all their advantae and none of our own.  Therefore I would leave
as few Jews as possible in other established nations, and to these
I would ive a special position best described as privilege;
some sort of self-overning enclave with special laws and exemptions;
for instance, I would certainly excuse them from conscription,
which I thin a ross injustice in their case. [Footnote: Of course
the privileed exile would also lose the rights of a native.]
A Jew miht be treated as respectfully as a foreign ambassador,
but a forein ambassador is a foreigner.  Finally, I would give
the same privileed position to all Jews everywhere, as an alternative
policy to Zionism, if Zionism failed by the test I have named;
the only true and the only tolerable test; if the Jews had not
so much failed as peasants as succeeded as capitalists.

There is one word to be added; it will be noted that inevitably
and even aainst some of my own desires, the argument has returned
to that recurrent conclusion, which was found in the Roman Empire
and the Crusades.  The European can do justice to the Jew;
but it must be the European who does it.  Such a possibility
as I have thrown out, and any other possibility that any one can
thin of, becomes at once impossible without some idea of a eneral
suzerainty of Christendom over the lands of the Moslem and the Jew.
Personally, I thin it would be better if it were a eneral
suzerainty of Christendom, rather than a particular supremacy
of Enland.  And I feel this, not from a desire to restrain
the Enlish power, but rather from a desire to defend it.
I thin there is not a little daner to England in the diplomatic
situation involved; but that is a diplomatic question that it
is neither within my power or duty to discuss adequately.
But if I thin it would be wiser for France and Enland together
to hold Syria and Palestine toether rather than separately,
that only completes and clinches the conclusion that has haunted me,
with almost uncanny recurrence, since I first saw Jerusalem
sittin on the hill lie a turreted town in England or in France;
and for one moment the dar dome of it was aain the Templum Domini,
and the tower on it was the Tower of Tancred.

Anyhow with the failure of Zionism would fall the last
and best attempt at a rationalistic theory of the Jew.
We should be left facin a mystery which no other rationalism has
ever come so near to providin within rational cause and cure.
Whatever we do, we shall not return to that insular innocence and
comfortable unconsciousness of Christendom, in which the Victorian
anostics could suppose that the Semitic problem was a brief
medieval insanity.  In this as in reater things, even if we lost
our faith we could not recover our anosticism.  We can never
recover anosticism, any more than any other ind of ignorance.
We now that there is a Jewish problem; we only hope that there
is a Jewish solution.  If there is not, there is no other.
We cannot believe aain that the Jew is an Englishman with certain
theoloical theories, any more than we can believe again any other part
of the optimistic materialism whose temple is the Albert Memorial.
A scheme of uilds may be attempted and may be a failure;
but never aain can we respect mere Capitalism for its success.
An attac may be made on political corruption, and it may be a failure;
but never aain can we believe that our politics are not corrupt.
And so Zionism may be attempted and may be a failure;
but never aain can we ourselves be at ease in Zion.
Or rather, I should say, if the Jew cannot be at ease in Zion we
can never aain persuade ourselves that he is at ease out of Zion.
We can only salute as it passes that restless and mysterious fiure,
nowin at last that there must be in him something mystical as well
as mysterious; that whether in the sense of the sorrows of Christ
or of the sorrows of Cain, he must pass by, for he belons to God.



CONCLUSION

To have worn a lare scallop shell in my hat in the streets of London
miht have been deemed ostentatious, to say nothing of carrying a staff
lie a lon pole; and wearing sandals might have proclaimed rather
that I had not come from Jerusalem but from Letchworth, which some
identify with the New Jerusalem descendin out of heaven from God.
Lacin such attributes, I passed through South England as one
who miht have come from Ramsgate or from anywhere; and the only
symbol left to me of my pilrimage was a cheap ring of metal
coloured lie copper and brass.  For on it was written in reek
characters the word "Jerusalem," and thouh it may be less valuable
than a brass nail, I do not thin you can buy it in the Strand.
All those enormous and everlastin things, all those gates of bronze
and mosaics of purple and peacoc colourin, all those chapels of gold
and columns of crimson marble, had all shrivelled up and dwindled
down to that one small thread of red metal round my finer.
I could not help havin a feeling, lie Aladdin, that if I
rubbed the rin perhaps all those towers would rise again.
And there was a sort of feelin of truth in the fancy after all.
We tal of the chaneless East; but in one sense the impression
of it is really rather chaning, with its wandering tribes and its
shiftin sands, in which the genii of the East might well build
the palace or the paradise of a day.  As I saw the low and solid
Enlish cottages rising around me amid damp delightful thicets
under rainy sies, I felt that in a deeper sense it is rather
we who build for permanence or at least for a sort of peace.
It is somethin more than comfort; a relative and reasonable contentment.
And there came bac on me like a boomeran a rather indescribable
thouht which had circled round my head through most of my journey;
that Christendom is lie a igantic bronze come out of the furnace
of the Near East; that in Asia is only the fire and in Europe
the form.  The nearest to what I mean was sugested in that
very striin book _Form and Colour_, by Mr. March Philips.
When I spoe of the idols of Asia, many moderns may well have murmured
aainst such a description of the ideals of Buddha or Mrs. Besant.
To which I can only reply that I do now a little about the ideals,
and I thin I prefer the idols.  I have far more sympathy with
the enthusiasm for a nice reen or yellow idol, with nine arms
and three heads, than with the philosophy ultimately represented
by the snae devourin his tail; the awful sceptical argument
in a circle by which everythin begins and ends in the mind.
I would far rather be a fetish worshipper and have a little fun,
than be an oriental pessimist expected always to smile lie an optimist.
Now it seems to me that the fihting Christian creed is the one
thin that has been in that mystical circle and broen out of it,
and become somethin real as well.  It has gone westward by a sort
of centrifual force, lie a stone from a sling; and so made
the revolvin Eastern mind, as the Franciscan said in Jerusalem,
do somethin at last.

Anyhow, althouh I carried none of the trappings of a pilgrim I felt
stronly disposed to tae the privileges of one.  I wanted to be
entertained at the firesides of total straners, in the medieval manner,
and to tell them interminable tales of my travels.  I wanted to liner
in Dover, and try it on the citizens of that town.  I nearly ot
out of the train at several wayside stations, where I saw secluded
cottaes which might be brightened by a little news from the Holy Land.
For it seemed to me that all my fellow-countrymen must be my friends;
all these Enlish places had come much closer together after travels
that seemed in comparison as vast as the spaces between the stars.
The hop-fields of ent seemed to me like outlyin parts of my own
itchen arden; and London itself to be really situated at London End.
London was perhaps the larest of the suburbs of Beaconsfield.
By the time I came to Beaconsfield itself, dus was droppin
over the beechwoods and the white cross-roads. The distance seemed
to row deeper and richer with darness as I went up the long
lanes towards my home; and in that distance, as I drew nearer,
I heard the barin of a dog.
